Correction: Nasafi explicitly declares this view to be kufr because it implies that God's power is finite, which contradicts essential Islamic teachings.

Checkpoint

D) It shows God's mercy.

Key Takeaway
Denying that God can do anything more beneficial for disbelievers logically leads to limiting God's power, which is an act of disbelief.

Refuting the Mu'tazila Claim of Unlimited Beneficial Things
Nasafi presents and refutes the Mu'tazila objection with a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila claim that God has unlimited beneficial things by exposing a dilemma that leads to attributing impotence to Allah.

Core Idea
Unlimited divine benefits create a dilemma: either God prevents benefit or lacks power.

The Mu'tazila argue that God has endless beneficial things but only gives what is needed. Nasafi exposes the flaw: if giving more would not cause belief, then God is powerless to grant that benefit. He uses a dilemma and an analogy to show that the Mu'tazila position inadvertently attributes impotence to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila objection
Read the Mu'tazila claim that God has unlimited beneficial things but only gives according to need.
Notice they assert no limit to God's power over beneficial things.
2
Understand the author's framing
Nasafi attributes the objection to al-Ka'bi to expose their deception. Reflect on why he mentions him.
Al-Ka'bi is a prominent Mu'tazili imam.
3
Follow the first dilemma
If God gave more to the disbeliever, would he believe? If yes, God prevented his benefit. If no, God cannot make him believe.
This shows the Mu'tazila cannot escape both horns.
4
Trace the lutf extension
Nasafi extends the argument: denying God's power over a grace that would cause belief is like denying power to move what He knows He will not move.
This parallels the original dilemma.
5
Reflect on the outcome
The Mu'tazila position leads to describing God with impotence, contradicting their own principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: نحن لا نقول لما في مقدوره نهاية بل نزعم أنه ليس لما عند الله مما فيه الصلاح غاية ولا نهاية، وأن في سلطانه وقدرته وخزائن رحمته من أمثال ما فعل هو أصلح لهم مما لا غاية له ولا نهاية، والله جل ذكره إنما مما هو بهم يفعل بهم من ذلك ويعطيهم منه في كل وقت مقدار حاجتهم وما يعلم أنه أصلح لهم وأدعى إلى الطاعة وأزجر عن المعصية، ذكرت السؤال بلفظ الكعبي وهو عندهم إمام أهل الأرض ليعلم المتأمل تمويههم في كل ما يتكلمون به، وزوغانهم عن محز الكلام والاشتغال بالتلبيس على العوام. فأقول في جوابه وبالله التوفيق -: إن كان في قدرة الله تعالى وخزائن رحمته أمثال ما فعل من الأصلح فما قولك لو فعل ذلك في الحال بهذا الكافر هل يؤمن؟ فإن قال : نعم. فقد أقر بمنع الأصلح إذ لم يفعل ذلك كله ليؤمن، وإن قال : لا قيل فإذا لا قدرة له على ما به لو آمن من هذا العبد فحصل له صلاحه، فإذا السؤال أنه هل يقدر أن يفعل به في الوقت أصلح من هذا الذي فعل أم لا يصفه بالقدرة عليه؟ فينبغي أن يجيب عن هذا الحرف أو يعترض عليه، وإذا لم يكن عندك في مقدوره أن يفعل به في الوقت أصلح من هذا فهذا هو آية النفاد والعجز عن تحصيل المراد. ثم يقال له : أليس من زعم أن الله تعالى يقدر أن يفعل في الوقت صلاحًا هو أصلح الأشياء للإنسان ولا يوصف بالقدرة على مثله يكون معجزا ربه؟ فلا بد من بلي. فقيل له: فإنك وإن أثبت قدرة على أمثال ما فعل فإذا لم تصفه بالقدرة على ما هو أصلح له أصلح له من هذا وصفته بالعجز أيضا ، ثم يقال له أليس أن من لم يصف الله تعالى بالقدرة على لطف يؤمن به من علم أنه يؤمن واصفًا له بالعجز؟ فإذا قال نعم قيل فكذا من لم يصف الله تعالى بالقدرة على لطف يؤمن به من علم أنه لا يؤمن به يكون واصفا له بالعجز كما أن من لم يصف الله تعالى بالقدرة على تحريك من يعلم أنه لا يحركه يكون واصفًا له بالعجز كمن لا يصفه بالقدرة على تحريك
If they say: 'We do not say that what is in His power has an end; rather we claim that there is no limit or end to what is with Allah of beneficial things, and that in His dominion, power, and treasures of mercy are examples like what He did that are more beneficial for them without limit or end, and Allah, exalted be His mention, only does to them from that and gives them from it at each time the amount of their need and what He knows is more beneficial for them and more conducive to obedience and more deterrent from disobedience' – I mentioned the question in the wording of al-Ka'bi, who is according to them the imam of the people of the earth, so that the contemplative person may know their obfuscation in everything they speak, and their deviation from the core of speech and their occupation with deception of the common people. I say in response, and with Allah is success: If in the power of Allah the Exalted and the treasures of His mercy are examples like what He did of the more beneficial, then what do you say – if He did that now to this disbeliever, would he believe? If he says 'Yes,' then he has confessed the prevention of the more beneficial, since He did not do all of that so that he would believe. And if he says 'No,' then it is said: Then He has no power over that by which this servant would believe and thus attain his benefit. So the question is: either He is able to do for him at the time what is more beneficial than this which He did, or He is not – do you ascribe power to Him? He should answer this point or object to it. And if you do not consider that in His power to do for him at the time something more beneficial than this, then this is a sign of exhaustion and inability to accomplish the intended. Then it is said to him: Is it not the case that whoever claims that Allah the Exalted is able to do at the time a benefit that is the most beneficial thing for the person, yet does not ascribe power to Him over its like, would be attributing impotence to his Lord? There is no escape from [answering]. So it is said to him: Even if you affirm power over examples like what He did, if you do not ascribe to Him power over what is more beneficial for him than this, you are also describing Him with impotence. Then it is said to him: Is it not that whoever does not ascribe to Allah the Exalted power over a grace by which one who is known to believe will believe is describing Him with impotence? If he says 'Yes,' then it is said: Likewise, whoever does not ascribe to Allah the Exalted power over a grace by which one who is known not to believe would believe is describing Him with impotence, just as whoever does not ascribe to Allah the Exalted power to move one whom He knows He will not move is describing Him with impotence, like one who does not ascribe to Him power to move.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage contains the Mu'tazila objection and the author's detailed refutation. The Mu'tazila first object that they do not claim God's power has an end; rather, God has unlimited beneficial things but only gives what is needed. The author notes that he phrased the question according to al-Ka'bi (a prominent Mu'tazili) to expose their deception. Then he presents a dilemma: if God gave more benefits to the disbeliever, would he believe? If yes, then God prevented his benefit by not giving, which negates al-aslah. If no, then God cannot do it, showing impotence. The author then extends the argument: denying God's power to do a specific grace (luṭf) that would cause belief (even if the person is known not to believe) is equivalent to denying God's power to move what He knows He will not move; both attribute impotence to God. Thus, the Mu'tazila position is refuted by their own logic.

Key Terms
الأصلح (al-aṣlaḥ)
اللطف (al-luṭf)
العجز (al-ʿajz)
الكعبي (al-Kaʿbī)
المعتزلة (al-Muʿtazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila objection seems reasonable because God indeed has unlimited power and knowledge.
Correction: Nasafi shows the objection leads to attributing impotence to God, either by preventing what is beneficial or by lacking power to cause belief.

Checkpoint
In refuting the Mu'tazila, what does Nasafi say would follow if God gave more to the disbeliever and he still did not believe?
A) God prevented his benefit.
B) God has no power to make him believe.
C) The disbeliever is excused.
D) It proves al-aslah is correct.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila by showing their claim of unlimited benefits leads to a dilemma that ultimately attributes impotence to Allah.

Refuting al-Aslah with a Conditional Dilemma
A refutation through a conditional dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma used to refute the Mu'tazilī doctrine of al-aṣlaḥ (the most beneficial).

Core Idea
A dilemma shows that the doctrine of al-aṣlaḥ leads to either admission of prevention of good or limitation of God's power.

In this passage, the author adopts the wording of al-Ka'bi, a leading Mu'tazilī scholar, to expose the flaws in their doctrine of al-aṣlaḥ. The argument is a conditional dilemma that forces the opponent into a contradiction. This lesson will walk through that dilemma and its implications for divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Pay attention to the tone and the logical flow.
The author uses the opponent's own leader's words to frame the argument.
2
Identify the structure of the dilemma
Notice that the passage presents a conditional question with two possible answers, each leading to a problem for the opponent.
3
Examine the first answer (yes) and its implication
If the opponent says 'yes', they admit that God could have done something more beneficial earlier but didn't, thus preventing the best.
This contradicts the claim that God always does the most beneficial.
4
Examine the second answer (no) and its implication
If the opponent says 'no', they admit that God does not have the power to do something more beneficial, which limits divine power.
This contradicts divine omnipotence.
5
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that if the opponent cannot attribute to God the power to do more beneficial, that is a sign of depletion and incapacity, which is impossible for God.
Thus, the doctrine of al-aslah is refuted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكرت السؤال بلفظ الكعبي وهو عندهم إمام أهل الأرض ليعلم المتأمل تمويههم في كل ما يتكلمون به، وزوغانهم عن محز الكلام والاشتغال بالتلبيس على العوام.
فأقول في جوابه وبالله التوفيق -: إن كان في قدرة الله تعالى وخزائن رحمته أمثال ما فعل من الأصلح فما قولك لو فعل ذلك في الحال بهذا الكافر هل يؤمن؟ فإن قال : نعم. فقد أقر بمنع الأصلح إذ لم يفعل ذلك كله ليؤمن، وإن قال : لا قيل فإذا لا قدرة له على ما به لو آمن من هذا العبد فحصل له صلاحه، فإذا السؤال أنه هل يقدر أن يفعل به في الوقت أصلح من هذا الذي فعل أم لا يصفه بالقدرة عليه؟ فينبغي أن يجيب عن هذا الحرف أو يعترض عليه، وإذا لم يكن عندك في مقدوره أن يفعل به في الوقت أصلح من هذا فهذا هو آية النفاد والعجز عن تحصيل المراد.
I mentioned the question in the wording of al-Ka'bi, who among them is the imam of the people of the earth, so that the thoughtful person may know their deception in everything they speak, and their deviation from the essence of discourse and their occupation with obfuscation of the common people. So I say in response to it, and with God is success: If there are in the power of God, exalted, and the treasuries of His mercy, equivalents of what He did of the most beneficial, then what do you say if He did that at the present time to this disbeliever, would he believe? If he says: Yes, then he has admitted the prevention of the most beneficial, since He did not do all of that so that he would believe. And if he says: No, then it is said: Then he has no power over that by which, if this servant believed, his benefit would be achieved. So the question is whether He is able to do with him at the time something more beneficial than this which He did, or he does not attribute to Him power over it? So he must answer this point or object to it. And if in your view there is not in His power to do with him at the time something more beneficial than this, then this is the sign of depletion and incapacity to achieve the desired.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilī doctrine of al-aṣlaḥ (the most beneficial) through a strategic dilemma. The author begins by stating that he framed the question using the words of al-Ka'bi, a leading figure among the Mu'tazila, to expose their deceptive methods. He then presents a conditional argument: If God has in His power equivalents of the most beneficial act He performed, what if He were to perform that equivalent right now to this disbeliever—would the disbeliever then believe? The opponent has two possible answers, both leading to contradictions. If he answers 'yes,' he admits that God prevented the most beneficial, because He did not do that equivalent earlier to cause faith. If he answers 'no,' then he admits that God lacks power over that which would bring about the disbeliever's benefit. The author then concludes that if the opponent cannot attribute to God the power to do something more beneficial at that moment, then that is a proof of depletion and incapacity, which is impossible for God.

الأصلح
القدرة
العجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is obliged to do the most beneficial at every moment.
Correction: This dilemma shows that such an obligation leads to either admitting that God prevents good or limiting His power, both impossible.

Key Takeaway

Attributing Incapacity to God by Denying His Power Over Beneficial Grace
A series of analogies linking denial of God's power over beneficial graces to attributing incapacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that denying God's power over any possible beneficial act, even if He chooses not to actualize it, is equivalent to attributing incapacity to Him.

Core Idea
God's power is not limited by His actual choices; denying Him power over possibilities attributes incapacity.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla presents a series of analogical arguments to counter a theological position that would limit God's power by claiming He cannot grant the most beneficial grace or a grace that would cause belief in certain individuals. The author demonstrates that such views necessarily attribute incapacity to Allah. As you read the passage, pay attention to the logical structure of each analogy and how it forces the opponent to concede the same principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Note the repeated question-and-answer pattern.
The passage uses rhetorical questions to guide the opponent to concede.
2
Identify the first analogy
The first argument: if someone claims God can do the most beneficial act but denies He can do something similar, that person attributes incapacity to God. Why? Because power over one similar act implies power over others.
This sets the principle that denying power over a similar act is tantamount to denying power altogether.
3
Track the extension to graces leading to faith
The argument then extends to a grace that would cause belief. If one denies God power over such a grace for a person whom God knows will believe, that is also attributing incapacity.
Even though the grace is described as 'لطف يؤمن به', the focus is on God's power, not His actual doing.
4
Consider the case of the disbeliever
The third analogy: denying God power over a grace that would cause belief in a person whom God knows will not believe is likewise attributing incapacity.
This shows that God's power is not contingent on His foreknowledge of outcomes.
5
Reflect on the final analogy about motion
The passage concludes with an analogy: denying God power to move someone whom He knows will not be moved is like denying power to move someone whom He knows will be moved—both attribute incapacity.
Thus, all denials of God's power over any possible act are equivalent in attributing weakness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال له : أليس من زعم أن الله تعالى يقدر أن يفعل في الوقت صلاحًا هو أصلح الأشياء للإنسان ولا يوصف بالقدرة على مثله يكون معجزا ربه؟ فلا بد من بلي. فقيل له: فإنك وإن أثبت قدرة على أمثال ما فعل فإذا لم تصفه بالقدرة على ما هو أصلح له أصلح له من هذا وصفته بالعجز أيضا ، ثم يقال له أليس أن من لم يصف الله تعالى بالقدرة على لطف يؤمن به من علم أنه يؤمن واصفًا له بالعجز؟ فإذا قال نعم قيل فكذا من لم يصف الله تعالى بالقدرة على لطف يؤمن به من علم أنه لا يؤمن به يكون واصفا له بالعجز كما أن من لم يصف الله تعالى بالقدرة على تحريك من يعلم أنه لا يحركه يكون واصفًا له بالعجز كمن لا يصفه بالقدرة على تحريك من يعلم أنه يحركه
Then it is said to him: Is it not that whoever claims that God, exalted, is able to do at the time a benefit that is the most beneficial of things for a person, and does not attribute to Him power over its like, would be attributing incapacity to his Lord? So he must say yes. So it is said to him: For even if you affirm power over the likes of what He did, if you do not attribute to Him power over what is more beneficial for him, more beneficial than this, you describe Him with incapacity as well. Then it is said to him: Is it not that whoever does not attribute to God, exalted, power over a grace by which he would believe, knowing that he would believe, is describing Him with incapacity? So if he says yes, it is said: Likewise, whoever does not attribute to God, exalted, power over a grace by which he would believe, knowing that he would not believe, would be describing Him with incapacity, just as whoever does not attribute to God, exalted, power to move someone whom he knows that he does not move is describing Him with incapacity, like one who does not attribute to Him power to move someone whom he knows that he moves.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage presents a series of analogical arguments to demonstrate that denying God the power to grant a more beneficial grace or even a grace that would lead to faith (even if He knows the person will not believe) is tantamount to attributing incapacity to Him. The author first establishes that if one says God can do the most beneficial act but not something similar, that is attributing incapacity. Then he extends this: if one denies God power over a grace that would cause faith in someone whom He knows will believe, that is also incapacity. Similarly, if one denies God power over a grace for someone whom He knows will not believe, it is likewise incapacity, just as if one denies God power to move someone whom He knows will not be moved—that too is attributing incapacity. The argument is that God's power is not limited by His knowledge; He has power over all possibilities, even if they are not actualized.

Key Terms

Gathering Multiple Graces and Divine Power
Dilemma refuting the claim that gathering graces does not increase benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma posed by the author regarding the gathering of equivalent graces and how it leads to the conclusion of the finitude of divine power.

Core Idea
If gathering graces does not increase benefit, then God's power is limited.

This passage continues a dialectical refutation of those who argue that God does not need to provide the most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ). The author presents a dilemma: if God can gather multiple equivalent graces, would that achieve greater benefit? The opponent's answer either leads to contradiction or to the admission that God's power is limited. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the dialectical setup
The author imagines a scenario where God has equivalent graces. Understand the premise before following the argument.
2
Trace the two branches of the dilemma
Follow what happens if the opponent says 'yes' (benefit achieved) and if he says 'no' (benefit not achieved). Each leads to a problem.
3
Analyze the additional question
The author asks why not combine graces even if faith is not achieved, to increase exposure. The opponent's response is anticipated and refuted.
4
Understand the final conclusion
The author concludes that the opponent's reasoning implies God's power is limited (tanāhī al-qudra) and that their objection is deceptive.
This connects to the broader theme of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال له : إذا كان له أمثال فلو جمع تلك الأمثال لهذا اللطف الذي حصل في الوقت، هل يحصل به الصلاح أم لا؟ فإن قال نعم، فقد ترك قوله باللطف والأصلح ، وقيل :له كان جميع تلك الألطاف أصلح ولم يفعل فقد ترك الأصلح، وإن قال لا فلم يثبت بقدرته على تلك الأمثال قدرة على ما هو الأصلح لهذا الكافر من هذا الذي فعل وهو القول بتناهي القدرة، ثم يقال: هلا بين تلك الألطاف وإن كان لا يحصل بها الإيمان ليكون أشد تعريضا للإيمان كما فعل هذا اللطف، فإن قالوا: لو جمع بين هذا الأصلح الذي فعل وبين ما هو من أمثال لم يحصل بها الصلاح، بل يكون ذلك أفسد للكافر.
قلنا وبهذا تبين تمويهكم في الاعتراض على فصل التعجيز وإثبات نهاية المقدور على ما قررنا. وتقرر بما أجبنا لكم أن لا يقدر فى الوقت أن يفعل ما هو الأصلح له مما فعل
Then it is said to him: If He has equivalents, then if He were to gather those equivalents for this grace which occurred at the time, would the benefit be achieved by it or not? If he says yes, then he has abandoned his statement of grace and the most beneficial, and it is said: He would have had all those graces as more beneficial and did not do them, so he abandoned the most beneficial. And if he says no, then he has not established by His power over those equivalents a power over what is more beneficial for this disbeliever than this which He did, and that is the statement of the finitude of power. Then it is said: Why not combine those graces even if faith is not achieved by them, so that it would be a stronger exposure to faith, as this grace did? If they say: If one were to combine this most beneficial which He did with what is of its equivalents, the benefit would not be achieved by them; rather, that would be more corruptive for the disbeliever. We say: By this, your deception is exposed in objecting to the chapter of disabling and establishing the end of the determined as we have established. And it is established by what we have answered you that He is not able at the time to do what is more beneficial for him than what He did.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation by considering the possibility of gathering multiple equivalent graces. The author poses a dilemma: if one claims that God has equivalents of the grace He performed, and those were collected together, would the benefit be greater? If the opponent says yes, then God's abandoning all those graces means He did not do the most beneficial. If he says no, then God's power over those equivalents does not extend to what is more beneficial, implying finitude of power (tanāhī al-qudra). Then the author raises another point: why not add those graces to increase the exposure to faith? The opponent's reply that combining them would be more corruptive is then refuted: this exposes their deception. The conclusion is that, based on their own reasoning, God is not able at that time to do what is more beneficial, which is a position of incapacity.

اللطف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: More grace always means more benefit.
Correction: The passage shows that combining graces could be more corruptive for the disbeliever, so more grace is not always beneficial.

Checkpoint
What is the implication if the opponent says that gathering multiple graces would not achieve benefit?
A) It confirms God's power is limitless
B) It returns to the statement of finitude of power
C) It proves the opponent's sincerity
D) It shows that God does not want benefit for disbelievers

Key Takeaway
The argument that gathering equivalent graces does not increase benefit leads to the conclusion that God's power is finite, which is unacceptable in Sunni theology.

Goodness Without Limit: God's Unlimited Power
Al-Nasafi proves that goodness increases infinitely, hence God's power is not limited.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi argues that goodness can increase infinitely and how that proves God's power is unlimited.

Core Idea
Goodness increases; God's power cannot be limited to any finite measure.

In this passage, al-Nasafi tackles a subtle theological point: if God can create the highest truth (the Quran), can He create something even truer? He argues that truth does not increase, but goodness does. Since goodness can always be increased, limiting God's power to a specific amount would imply incapacity. Through the analogy of the indivisible atom, he shows that God's power over goodness is endless.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the flow of the argument: contrast between truth and goodness, then the proof from benefit, then the atom analogy.
2
Identify the key distinction
Find where al-Nasafi distinguishes between truth (sidq) and goodness (salah). Why does he say truth does not increase?
He gives the example that no one says God can create a truth truer than the Quran.
3
Understand the proof that goodness increases
Al-Nasafi gives two arguments: one from fulfilling needs and one from the possibility of lighter worship with greater reward. Why do these show that goodness increases?
He also says denying this is denying observable fact.
4
Grasp the atom analogy
The indivisible atom (juz' la yatajazza) is the smallest possible thing. Just as God can add parts to it ad infinitum, so He can add more goodness to any given goodness. Why does this prove His power is unlimited?
If you say goodness terminates, you imply God cannot go beyond, which is like saying He cannot enlarge an atom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بإثبات النهاية لمقدوره، فإن أحدًا لا يقول الله تعالى قادر على صدق أصدق من القرآن ولا على أصغر من الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ، وهذا لأنا بينا أن الصلاح يتزايد والصدق لا يتزايد، وكذا أصغر من الجزء محال وجوده، فكان إثبات القدرة على ذلك إثباتًا للقدرة على المحال، وفيما نحن فيه الأمر بخلافه إذ هو متزايد في نفسه، وقصر القدرة على قدر مقدر مما لا يتزايد في نفسه إثبات للعجز وراءه كمن يقول إن الله تعالى لا يقدر على أن يزيد على العالم شيئًا ولا أن يخلق لزيد ولدًا آخر، والدليل على كون الصلاح متزايد في نفسه أن من رد كل حاجة لإنسان كان ذلك أصلح له من أن يقضي له حاجة واحدة، وكذا لو كان الله تعالى افترض من العبادات أقل مما افترض وجعل ثوابها أكثر مما جعل، لا شك أن ذلك كان أصلح، على أن من أنكر كون المصلحة متزايدة في نفسها فقد أنكر المشاهدة، وإذا ثبت تزايدها كان قصر القدرة على قدر منها مقدر إثباتا للعجز عما وراءه. ثم نقول له: فصل الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ دليل خصمك لو عقلت، فإن خصمك يقول كما أثبتنا الابتداء منتهيًا إلى ما لا أصغر منه فكذلك نثبت الابتداء في الصلاح منتهيًا إلى حد لا يكون في الصلاح أقل منه، ثم كما أن جزءًا لا يتجزأ لا يزاد عليه جزء إلا والله تعالى قدرة أن يزيد عليه أجزاء إلى ما لا يتناهى، فكذلك ما من صلاح يوجد إلا والله تعالى عليه من القدرة أن يزيد عليه آخر إلى ما لا يتناهى فصار القول بتناهي الصلاح وسلب قدرة الله تعالى على أن يزيد على قدر مقدر تعجيزا له كما في أجزاء الجسم كان القول ببلوغ الأجزاء غاية لا يقدر الله تعالى على الزيادة عليها تعجيزا، والله الموفق.
By establishing the end for what is possible, for no one says that Allah is able to create a truth truer than the Quran, nor (to create) smaller than the indivisible atom. And this is because we have shown that goodness (al-salah) increases, while truth (al-sidq) does not increase. Likewise, smaller than the atom is impossible in existence. So establishing power over that is establishing power over the impossible. But in our present case, the matter is contrary to that, for it is increasing in itself. And restricting power to a limited measure of what does not increase in itself is establishing incapability beyond it, like one who says that Allah is not able to add anything to the world, nor to create another son for Zayd. The proof that goodness increases in itself is that one who fulfills every need of a person is more beneficial to him than one who fulfills one need. Likewise, if Allah had prescribed fewer acts of worship and made their reward greater, there is no doubt that would be more beneficial. Moreover, whoever denies that benefit (al-maslaha) increases in itself has denied observable fact. And when its increase is established, restricting power to a limited amount of it is establishing incapability for what is beyond it. Then we say to him: The case of the indivisible atom is the proof of your opponent, if only you understood. For your opponent says: Just as we have established the beginning ending at what has nothing smaller than it, so we establish the beginning in goodness ending at a limit where there is no smaller goodness. Then just as the indivisible atom, when no part is added to it, yet Allah has power to add to it parts ad infinitum, so also no goodness exists except that Allah has power to add another to it ad infinitum. So the statement that goodness terminates, and the negation of Allah's power to increase beyond a limited measure, is an incapacitation of Him, just as in the parts of a body, the statement that the parts reach a limit beyond which Allah cannot increase is an incapacitation. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage is a proof that goodness (al-salah) can increase without limit, and therefore God's power is not restricted to a specific amount of goodness. Al-Nasafi begins by contrasting goodness with truth (al-sidq): truth does not increase, but goodness does. He then argues that if one limits God's power to a certain amount of goodness, that implies God is incapable of doing more, which is absurd. He gives an example: God could have prescribed fewer duties and greater rewards, which would be more beneficial. He also uses the analogy of the indivisible atom (juz' la yatajazza): just as God can add more parts beyond the smallest particle, He can add more goodness beyond any given goodness. Thus, saying that goodness terminates and God cannot exceed it is equivalent to declaring Him powerless.

Key Terms
صلاح
قدرة
صدق
جزء لا يتجزأ
مصلحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since truth (sidq) does not increase, goodness (salah) also does not increase.
Correction: Al-Nasafi argues that goodness is fundamentally different from truth; goodness increases because it is based on benefit (maslaha), which is observable and can always be increased.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what is the analogy used to prove that God's power over goodness is unlimited?
A) The impossibility of creating a truth truer than the Quran
B) The indivisible atom and the ability to add parts to it ad infinitum
C) The fact that God could have made worship obligations lighter
D) The fact that goodness is observable

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi proves that goodness increases infinitely, so God's power cannot be limited to any finite measure, using the atom analogy to show endless capacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God has done 'enough' for you, and stopped expecting more? How does the truth that goodness can always increase challenge you to hope for more from His unlimited power?
?
When life feels stagnant or your prayers seem unanswered, do you subconsciously act as if God's power is limited? What would change if you truly believed He can always add more goodness, beyond what you can see?
?
If goodness can increase infinitely, what does that say about your own pursuit of good? Are you content with the minimum, or are you compelled to seek greater justice, charity, and faith, knowing God's power backs every increase?

Refuting the Claim about God's Power over Truth
Al-Nasafi refutes the claim that God cannot create a truth truer than the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine truth (sidq) is not subject to God's power according to al-Nasafi's refutation.

Core Idea
Divine truth is an attribute, not an act — it cannot be 'more truthful'.

Al-Nasafi confronts an objection that God cannot create a truth truer than the Quran. The objection rests on a false assumption about what falls under God's power. This lesson clarifies the distinction between divine attributes and created actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice al-Nasafi's concise style.
2
Identify the objection and response
The opponents claim that God cannot be described with power over a truth truer than the Quran. Find how al-Nasafi responds: he dismisses the claim as based on a corrupt principle.
3
Understand the key distinction
Al-Nasafi explains that divine truth (sidq Allah) is not subject to power because it is an attribute, not a created act. Similarly, the Quran is not subject to improvement. Why does this distinction refute the objection?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ما يزعمون أن الله تعالى لا يوصف بالقدرة على صدق أصدق من القرآن شيء بنوه على أصلهم الفاسد، فأما صدق الله تعالى فغير داخل تحت القدرة، وكذا القرآن على ما مر.

Commentary
This passage is a concise refutation of an objection. The opponents claim that God cannot be described with power to create a truth that is truer than the Quran. Al-Nasafi responds by pointing out that this claim is based on a false principle. He explains that the truth of Allah (sidq Allah) is not something that falls under power—it is an attribute, not an act of creation. Similarly, the Quran, being the speech of God, is not subject to power in the sense of being improved upon. This argument dismisses the objection as resting on a misunderstanding of the divine attribute of truth and the nature of the Quran.

God's Truthfulness Beyond Power
Refutes the claim that God lacks power over truthfulness beyond the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's truthfulness and the Quran's truthfulness are not subject to His power, and how this refutes a sectarian objection.

Core Idea
God's truthfulness is an essential attribute, not a product of His power.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi addresses a subtle objection from those who claim God cannot have power over the truthfulness of something more truthful than the Quran. He explains that truthfulness is an essential attribute of God, not something He 'does' or 'makes'. This clarification is crucial for understanding divine attributes and refuting misconceptions about God's power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style — the passage packs a lot of meaning in few words.
2
Identify the opponents' claim
Find where the author quotes the opponents: 'they claim that God cannot be described as having power over the truthfulness of something more truthful than the Quran.' Understand why this would be problematic.
The opponents' claim implies a limitation on God's power.
3
Focus on the refutation
The author responds by stating that God's truthfulness is 'not subject to [His] power' — it is an essential attribute. Similarly, the Quran as God's speech shares this quality.
Essential attributes are not acts; they are what God is, not what He does.
4
Reflect on the 'corrupt principle'
The opponents built their argument on a 'corrupt foundation.' Ask yourself: why is it corrupt? The answer lies in confusing essential attributes with contingent acts.
This illustrates a key difference between Sunni and Mu'tazili views on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Similarly, the Quran itself, being the speech of God, is also not subject to power in the sense of being made more or less truthful. The opponents' premise is thus based on a corrupt foundation.

Power for Opposites Proves God's Free Will
Argues that if power is for opposites, then God must be free, not compelled.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazilite principle that power is for opposites leads to the conclusion that God acts freely, not by compulsion.

Core Idea
If power is for opposites, then God must be free, not compelled.

In this passage, the author engages with a Mu'tazilite opponent by using their own premise against them. He first gets them to agree that power is valid for opposites and that lacking that property implies compulsion. Then he asks whether God is compelled or free, leading to the admission that God is free and acts by choice. Finally, he cites Quranic verses showing that God has power over actions that would cause universal disbelief, reinforcing that His power is not limited to causing belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of rhetorical questions to force the opponent's admission.
2
Identify the key premise about power and opposites
The passage starts by affirming that power is suitable for two opposites. Understand that this Mu'tazilite view means that if an agent cannot do the opposite of an act, that agent is compelled.
This premise is crucial for the entire argument.
3
Follow the two questions to the opponent
The author asks: (1) Do you say God is compelled or free? (2) Does He act by power or not? The opponent must answer that God is free and acts by power and choice.
This directly refutes any notion that God is compelled.
4
Reflect on the Quranic evidence
The author cites two verses showing God has power over actions that would lead to universal disbelief. This demonstrates that God's power extends to opposites (e.g., causing belief or disbelief).
The verses serve as proof that God's power is not limited to one outcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : أليس أن من قولكم أن القدرة تصلح للضدين وأن كل ما لا يصلح لضد لا يصلح لضد ذلك الضد بل ما لا يصلح للضدين فهو اضطرار؟ فلا بد من بلى. قيل : أتقولون أن الله تعالى مضطر أم مختار ؟ وهل يفعل ما يفعل عن قدرة أم لا؟ فلابد من القول إنه تعالى قادر يفعل ما يفعل عن اختيار وقدرة. قيل : أليس أن الله تعالى أثبت لنفسه القدرة على ما لو فعل لكفر به عباده كلهم بقوله تعالى: { وَلَوْ بَسَطَ اللهُ الرِّزْقَ لِعِبَادِهِ لَبَغَوْا فِي الْأَرْضِ } وقوله : { وَلَوْلَا أَن يَكُونَ النَّاسُ أُمَّةً وَاحِدَةً لَجَعَلْنَا لِمَن يَكْفُرُ بِالرَّحْمَنِ لِبُيُوتِهِمْ سُقُفًا مِن فِضَّةٍ وَمَعَارِجَ عَلَيْهَا يَظْهَرُونَ } فلا بد من بلى.
Then we say: Is it not part of your statement that power is suitable for two opposites, and that whatever is not suitable for an opposite is not suitable for the opposite of that opposite, but rather what is not suitable for two opposites is compulsion (iḍṭirār)? There must be an affirmative. It was said: Do you say that God Almighty is compelled (muḍṭarr) or free (mukhtār)? And does He do what He does by power or not? It must be said that He is powerful, doing what He does by choice and power. It was said: Is it not that God Almighty has affirmed for Himself power over that which, if He did it, all His servants would disbelieve? He said, Most High: {And if Allah had extended provision for His servants, they would have transgressed in the earth} and His saying: {And were it not that mankind would become one community, We would have made for those who disbelieve in the Most Merciful, for their houses, ceilings of silver and stairways upon which they ascend}. There must be an affirmative.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage uses the Mu'tazilite premise that power (qudra) is valid for two opposites (e.g., both belief and disbelief) to argue that God is not compelled but acts freely. The author first gets the opponent to affirm that power is for opposites and that lacking this property indicates compulsion. Then he asks whether God is compelled or free. The opponent must say God is free and acts by power and choice. Then he cites Quranic verses showing God has power over what would cause universal disbelief (e.g., expanding provision or giving worldly wealth to disbelievers). The opponent must affirm this. This sets up a further argument about why God does not do what is most beneficial, which is taken up in the next lesson.

اضطرار
اختيار
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's power is limited to causing belief in all people.
Correction: The passage shows God has power to cause universal disbelief, but He chooses not to, proving His power extends to opposites and He acts freely.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the Mu'tazilite principle about power for two opposites imply about God?
A) That God is compelled.
B) That God is free and acts by choice.
C) That God cannot cause disbelief.
D) That God's power is limited.

Key Takeaway
God's power is valid for opposites, proving He acts freely and is not compelled—contrary to views that limit His power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
In moments of spiritual weakness, do you attribute your state to God's decree or your own choices? What does this lesson teach you about God's will and your own free will? Commit to one concrete action you will take to exercise your free will toward Him.

The passage shows God refrains from actions that would lead all people to disbelief. Reflect on a time when God's mercy withheld from you what you deserved. Will you thank Him for that restraint specifically? Write down one such instance and thank Him now.

The Nature of Divine Power and Compulsion
Definition of divine power and compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational principle that divine power is suitable for both opposites, and that compulsion is its absence.

Core Idea
Divine power covers both opposites; compulsion is its absence.

The author opens with a conceptual distinction: power inherently applies to two opposites, while compulsion applies to none. This sets up a chain of reasoning to affirm that God acts by choice and power, not by necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation. Note the question-and-answer style: the author poses rhetorical questions to force the opponent to agree.
The passage uses 'qīla' (it was said) to indicate the opponent's expected response.
2
Identify the definitions
Find the definition of 'qudra' (power) as suitable for both opposites, and 'iḍṭirār' (compulsion) as not suitable for both. Understand the logic: if something is not suitable for both, it is compulsion.
3
Follow the argument to its conclusion
After establishing the definitions, the author asks whether God is compelled or free. The opponent must admit He is qādir (powerful) and free, acting by choice.
This admission is crucial for the later trap.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : أليس أن من قولكم أن القدرة تصلح للضدين وأن كل ما لا يصلح لضد لا يصلح لضد ذلك الضد بل ما لا يصلح للضدين فهو اضطرار؟ فلا بد من بلى. قيل : أتقولون أن الله تعالى مضطر أم مختار ؟ وهل يفعل ما يفعل عن قدرة أم لا؟ فلابد من القول إنه تعالى قادر يفعل ما يفعل عن اختيار وقدرة. قيل : أليس أن الله تعالى أثبت لنفسه القدرة على ما لو فعل لكفر به عباده كلهم بقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَوْ بَسَطَ اللهُ الرِّزْقَ لِعِبَادِهِ لَبَغَوْا فِي الْأَرْضِ ﴾ وقوله : ﴿ وَلَوْلَا أَن يَكُونَ النَّاسُ أُمَّةً وَاحِدَةً لَجَعَلْنَا لِمَن يَكْفُرُ بِالرَّحْمَنِ لِبُيُوتِهِمْ سُقُفًا مِن فِضَّةٍ وَمَعَارِجَ عَلَيْهَا يَظْهَرُونَ ﴾ فلا بد من بلى. قيل : إذا كان قادرًا على ما لو فعل لكفر به الخلق فهل تصفون بأنه قادر على ما لو فعل لآمن به الخلق؟ فإن قالوا: لا. فقد قصروا قدرته على أحد الضدين وهو عندهم اضطرار والقول به كفر وإن قالوا: نعم. قلنا : فإذا لم يفعل فقد ترك ما هو الأصلح لهم؟ فإن قالوا: لم يفعل ذلك لأنه لو فعل ثم آمنوا لكان ثواب إيمانهم أقل أو لم يكن لإيمانهم ثواب قلنا : ما قولكم أن القليل من الثواب على الإيمان أصلح لهم، أم الخلود في النار ؟ فإن قالوا : القليل من الثواب كان أصلح لهم. قد أقروا أنه ترك ما هو الأصلح لهم وإن قالوا : الخلود في النار أصلح. فقد تجانفوا وجعلوا نفوسهم ضحكة للخلق. ثم نقول لهم : إذا كانت قدرة الكفر وقدرة الإيمان واحدة، فقد أعطى الله تعالى كلا من الممتحنين تلك القدرة ، فلم قلتم أن الله تعالى فعل بعباده، ما هو الأصلح لهم وهو لم يعطهم إلا ما هو صالح للأمرين جميعا خصوصا في حق من علم أنه يكفر ولا يؤمن؟ وبم كنتم أولى ممن يقولون إنه فعل بعباده ما هو الأفسد لهم إذ أعطاهم ما يحصلون به الكفر خصوصا في حق من علم أنه يكفر ؟ وهذا مما لا اتصال لهم عنه، والله الموفق.
Then we say: Is it not from your statement that power (al-qudra) is suitable for both opposites, and that whatever is not suitable for an opposite is not suitable for the opposite of that opposite, but rather what is not suitable for both opposites is compulsion (iḍṭirār)? So there is no avoiding an affirmative. It was said: Do you say that God Most High is compelled or free? And does He do what He does by power or not? So it is necessary to say that He Most High is powerful (qādir), doing what He does by choice and power. It was said: Does not God Most High affirm for Himself power over that which, if He did it, all His servants would disbelieve in Him, by His saying: 'And if God had extended provision to His servants, they would have transgressed in the earth' and His saying: 'And were it not that mankind would become one community, We would have made for those who disbelieve in the Most Merciful, for their houses, ceilings of silver and stairways upon which they ascend'? So there is no avoiding an affirmative. It was said: If He is powerful over that which, if He did it, the creation would disbelieve, do you describe Him as powerful over that which, if He did it, the creation would believe? If they say: No, then they have limited His power to one of the two opposites, and that according to them is compulsion, and holding that is unbelief. And if they say: Yes, we say: Then if He did not do it, He has abandoned what is most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ) for them? If they say: He did not do that because if He did it and then they believed, the reward of their faith would be less, or there would be no reward for their faith, we say: What do you say? Is the small amount of reward for faith most beneficial for them, or eternal punishment in the Fire? If they say: The small amount of reward was most beneficial for them, they have admitted that He abandoned what is most beneficial for them. And if they say: Eternal punishment in the Fire is most beneficial, then they have deviated and made themselves a laughingstock for the people. Then we say to them: If the power for disbelief and the power for belief are one, then God Most High has given each of those tested that power, so why do you say that God Most High did for His servants what is most beneficial for them, while He gave them only what is suitable for both matters, especially regarding one whom He knows will disbelieve and not believe? And in what are you more entitled than those who say that He did for His servants what is most corrupt for them, since He gave them that by which they obtain disbelief, especially regarding one whom He knows will disbelieve? And this is something they cannot escape from. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument against the Mu'tazila regarding divine power (qudra) and the principle of al-aṣlaḥ (the most beneficial). The author proceeds by a series of questions that force the opponent to admit contradictory positions. He begins by establishing that power (qudra) is suitable for both opposites (al-ḍiddān), and that compulsion (iḍṭirār) is when something is not suitable for both. Then he asks whether God is compelled or free, leading to the admission that God acts by choice and power. Next, he cites Quranic verses showing that God has power over actions that would cause all people to disbelieve, and asks whether God also has power to cause universal belief. If the Mu'tazila deny this, they limit God's power to one opposite, which they themselves consider compulsion and thus unbelief. If they affirm it, then why did God not do it? They respond that if He had, the reward for faith would be less, so not doing it is more beneficial. The author then traps them: either the small reward is more beneficial, in which case God abandoned the more beneficial by not giving it, or eternal punishment is more beneficial, which is absurd. Finally, he points out that since the same power is given to all, God gave the same ability to all, including those He knows will disbelieve, so He did not do the most beneficial for them. The argument concludes that the Mu'tazila cannot escape this contradiction.

Key Terms
القدرة
الضدان
الاضطرار
المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if God has power over something, He necessarily does it, or that power cannot be applied to contradictory actions.
Correction: In Islamic theology, power (qudra) is defined as the ability to act or not act, and it applies to both of two opposites. Compulsion (iḍṭirār) is when the agent has no choice.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of compulsion (iḍṭirār)?
A) Being suitable for both opposites
B) Not being suitable for both opposites
C) Being suitable for only one opposite
D) Being suitable for the opposite of an opposite

Key Takeaway
Power by definition applies to both opposites; compulsion is its negation, and God is free and powerful, not compelled.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah’s power encompasses both belief and disbelief, and He chose to guide you to faith, does that thought fill you with humble gratitude or with complacency? What one thing will you do today to show that you do not take His gift of iman for granted?
?
Have you ever caught yourself assuming Allah cannot bring about a particular outcome because it seemed impossible or unlikely? What does this lesson reveal about the limitlessness of His power, and how will that change the way you make du'a?
?
You make choices daily, but this lesson reminds you that Allah is the only truly free agent, acting by His power without external compulsion. Are you living as though you are compelled by your habits, environment, or desires, or as one who voluntarily submits to the All-Powerful? What is one area of your life where you need to stop blaming circumstances and instead consciously submit to Allah's will?

The Dilemma of Universal Belief and the Aslah Principle
Dialectical trap using God's power over belief and disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dialectical trap posed by the author: if God can cause universal belief, why doesn't He? If He cannot, His power is limited.

Core Idea
God's power over belief and disbelief forces a dilemma regarding the aslah principle.

Building on the previous admission that God is powerful over both opposites, the author now introduces Quranic evidence that God has power to cause universal disbelief. He then springs a trap: does God also have power to cause universal belief? The Mu'tazila must answer carefully, or they contradict their own position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with the Quranic verses
Read the two Quranic verses cited. They show that Allah restrains provision and worldly blessings to prevent people from disbelieving or becoming arrogant. This implies He has power over their actions.
The verses are used as evidence for God's power over universal outcomes.
2
Analyze the question about belief
The author asks: Is God also powerful over that which, if done, would cause all people to believe? The answer leads to two problems: if 'no', then God's power is limited to one opposite (compulsion, which they reject); if 'yes', then why didn't He do it? This is the central trap.
3
Consider the Mu'tazila's response
The Mu'tazila try to avoid the trap by saying that if He caused universal belief, the reward would be less or nonexistent. The author will later counter this.
This response is based on the aslah principle: God must do what is most beneficial for His servants.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : أليس أن من قولكم أن القدرة تصلح للضدين وأن كل ما لا يصلح لضد لا يصلح لضد ذلك الضد بل ما لا يصلح للضدين فهو اضطرار؟ فلا بد من بلى. قيل : أتقولون أن الله تعالى مضطر أم مختار ؟ وهل يفعل ما يفعل عن قدرة أم لا؟ فلابد من القول إنه تعالى قادر يفعل ما يفعل عن اختيار وقدرة. قيل : أليس أن الله تعالى أثبت لنفسه القدرة على ما لو فعل لكفر به عباده كلهم بقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَوْ بَسَطَ اللهُ الرِّزْقَ لِعِبَادِهِ لَبَغَوْا فِي الْأَرْضِ ﴾ وقوله : ﴿ وَلَوْلَا أَن يَكُونَ النَّاسُ أُمَّةً وَاحِدَةً لَجَعَلْنَا لِمَن يَكْفُرُ بِالرَّحْمَنِ لِبُيُوتِهِمْ سُقُفًا مِن فِضَّةٍ وَمَعَارِجَ عَلَيْهَا يَظْهَرُونَ ﴾ فلا بد من بلى. قيل : إذا كان قادرًا على ما لو فعل لكفر به الخلق فهل تصفون بأنه قادر على ما لو فعل لآمن به الخلق؟ فإن قالوا: لا. فقد قصروا قدرته على أحد الضدين وهو عندهم اضطرار والقول به كفر وإن قالوا: نعم. قلنا : فإذا لم يفعل فقد ترك ما هو الأصلح لهم؟ فإن قالوا: لم يفعل ذلك لأنه لو فعل ثم آمنوا لكان ثواب إيمانهم أقل أو لم يكن لإيمانهم ثواب قلنا : ما قولكم أن القليل من الثواب على الإيمان أصلح لهم، أم الخلود في النار ؟ فإن قالوا : القليل من الثواب كان أصلح لهم. قد أقروا أنه ترك ما هو الأصلح لهم وإن قالوا : الخلود في النار أصلح. فقد تجانفوا وجعلوا نفوسهم ضحكة للخلق. ثم نقول لهم : إذا كانت قدرة الكفر وقدرة الإيمان واحدة، فقد أعطى الله تعالى كلا من الممتحنين تلك القدرة ، فلم قلتم أن الله تعالى فعل بعباده، ما هو الأصلح لهم وهو لم يعطهم إلا ما هو صالح للأمرين جميعا خصوصا في حق من علم أنه يكفر ولا يؤمن؟ وبم كنتم أولى ممن يقولون إنه فعل بعباده ما هو الأفسد لهم إذ أعطاهم ما يحصلون به الكفر خصوصا في حق من علم أنه يكفر ؟ وهذا مما لا اتصال لهم عنه، والله الموفق.
Then we say: Is it not from your statement that power (al-qudra) is suitable for both opposites, and that whatever is not suitable for an opposite is not suitable for the opposite of that opposite, but rather what is not suitable for both opposites is compulsion (iḍṭirār)? So there is no avoiding an affirmative. It was said: Do you say that God Most High is compelled or free? And does He do what He does by power or not? So it is necessary to say that He Most High is powerful (qādir), doing what He does by choice and power. It was said: Does not God Most High affirm for Himself power over that which, if He did it, all His servants would disbelieve in Him, by His saying: 'And if God had extended provision to His servants, they would have transgressed in the earth' and His saying: 'And were it not that mankind would become one community, We would have made for those who disbelieve in the Most Merciful, for their houses, ceilings of silver and stairways upon which they ascend'? So there is no avoiding an affirmative. It was said: If He is powerful over that which, if He did it, the creation would disbelieve, do you describe Him as powerful over that which, if He did it, the creation would believe? If they say: No, then they have limited His power to one of the two opposites, and that according to them is compulsion, and holding that is unbelief. And if they say: Yes, we say: Then if He did not do it, He has abandoned what is most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ) for them? If they say: He did not do that because if He did it and then they believed, the reward of their faith would be less, or there would be no reward for their faith, we say: What do you say? Is the small amount of reward for faith most beneficial for them, or eternal punishment in the Fire? If they say: The small amount of reward was most beneficial for them, they have admitted that He abandoned what is most beneficial for them. And if they say: Eternal punishment in the Fire is most beneficial, then they have deviated and made themselves a laughingstock for the people. Then we say to them: If the power for disbelief and the power for belief are one, then God Most High has given each of those tested that power, so why do you say that God Most High did for His servants what is most beneficial for them, while He gave them only what is suitable for both matters, especially regarding one whom He knows will disbelieve and not believe? And in what are you more entitled than those who say that He did for His servants what is most corrupt for them, since He gave them that by which they obtain disbelief, especially regarding one whom He knows will disbelieve? And this is something they cannot escape from. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument against the Mu'tazila regarding divine power (qudra) and the principle of al-aṣlaḥ (the most beneficial). The author proceeds by a series of questions that force the opponent to admit contradictory positions. He begins by establishing that power (qudra) is suitable for both opposites (al-ḍiddān), and that compulsion (iḍṭirār) is when something is not suitable for both. Then he asks whether God is compelled or free, leading to the admission that God acts by choice and power. Next, he cites Quranic verses showing that God has power over actions that would cause all people to disbelieve, and asks whether God also has power to cause universal belief. If the Mu'tazila deny this, they limit God's power to one opposite, which they themselves consider compulsion and thus unbelief. If they affirm it, then why did God not do it? They respond that if He had, the reward for faith would be less, so not doing it is more beneficial. The author then traps them: either the small reward is more beneficial, in which case God abandoned the more beneficial by not giving it, or eternal punishment is more beneficial, which is absurd. Finally, he points out that since the same power is given to all, God gave the same ability to all, including those He knows will disbelieve, so He did not do the most beneficial for them. The argument concludes that the Mu'tazila cannot escape this contradiction.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the Mu'tazila deny that God has power to cause universal belief?
A) They admit God is all-powerful
B) They affirm the aslah principle
C) They limit God's power to one opposite, which is compulsion and thus unbelief
D) They prove the Quran is inerrant

Key Takeaway
By affirming God's power over both belief and disbelief, the Mu'tazila are forced to explain why God did not cause universal belief, leading to the aslah principle's self-contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God has the power to make everyone believe but chooses not to, are you tempted to question His wisdom when you see widespread disbelief? How does this lesson correct that tendency, and what will you do the next time such a thought arises?
?
The Mu'tazila assumed God must always do what seems 'most beneficial' to them. In your own life, where have you imposed your own idea of 'benefit' on God's decisions—perhaps in trials, unanswered prayers, or hardships? Will you now surrender that assumption and trust His wisdom even when it hurts?
?
God could have caused you to disbelieve, yet He guided you. Does your daily gratitude reflect that reality? Name one specific change you will make today in how you live your faith, because it is a gift and not merely your own choice.

The Final Trap: Reward vs. Punishment and the Inescapable Conclusion
Final trap and conclusion of the dialectical argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will see how the author exposes the absurdity of the Mu'tazila position by comparing small reward to eternal punishment, and concludes that God giving the same power to all does not fulfill the aslah principle.

Core Idea
Small reward is not more beneficial than eternal punishment; God's giving power to all leads to injustice for those He knows will disbelieve.

The author now tightens the noose. He presents a stark choice: is a small reward for faith more beneficial than eternal punishment? The Mu'tazila must choose one, but both options refute their position. The final blow is that God gives the same power to all, including those He knows will disbelieve, which contradicts the aslah principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the final exchange
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the two options presented to the Mu'tazila: small reward or eternal punishment.
2
Examine the consequences of each option
If they say small reward is more beneficial, they admit God abandoned the more beneficial (because He didn't give that reward). If they say eternal punishment is more beneficial, that is absurd.
The author says they make themselves a laughingstock.
3
Understand the final point about identical power
The author argues that since God gives the same power for both belief and disbelief to everyone, including those He knows will choose disbelief, He is not doing the most beneficial for them. In fact, one could argue He is doing the most corrupt.
This mirrors the accusation the Mu'tazila level against others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : أليس أن من قولكم أن القدرة تصلح للضدين وأن كل ما لا يصلح لضد لا يصلح لضد ذلك الضد بل ما لا يصلح للضدين فهو اضطرار؟ فلا بد من بلى. قيل : أتقولون أن الله تعالى مضطر أم مختار ؟ وهل يفعل ما يفعل عن قدرة أم لا؟ فلابد من القول إنه تعالى قادر يفعل ما يفعل عن اختيار وقدرة. قيل : أليس أن الله تعالى أثبت لنفسه القدرة على ما لو فعل لكفر به عباده كلهم بقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَوْ بَسَطَ اللهُ الرِّزْقَ لِعِبَادِهِ لَبَغَوْا فِي الْأَرْضِ ﴾ وقوله : ﴿ وَلَوْلَا أَن يَكُونَ النَّاسُ أُمَّةً وَاحِدَةً لَجَعَلْنَا لِمَن يَكْفُرُ بِالرَّحْمَنِ لِبُيُوتِهِمْ سُقُفًا مِن فِضَّةٍ وَمَعَارِجَ عَلَيْهَا يَظْهَرُونَ ﴾ فلا بد من بلى. قيل : إذا كان قادرًا على ما لو فعل لكفر به الخلق فهل تصفون بأنه قادر على ما لو فعل لآمن به الخلق؟ فإن قالوا: لا. فقد قصروا قدرته على أحد الضدين وهو عندهم اضطرار والقول به كفر وإن قالوا: نعم. قلنا : فإذا لم يفعل فقد ترك ما هو الأصلح لهم؟ فإن قالوا: لم يفعل ذلك لأنه لو فعل ثم آمنوا لكان ثواب إيمانهم أقل أو لم يكن لإيمانهم ثواب قلنا : ما قولكم أن القليل من الثواب على الإيمان أصلح لهم، أم الخلود في النار ؟ فإن قالوا : القليل من الثواب كان أصلح لهم. قد أقروا أنه ترك ما هو الأصلح لهم وإن قالوا : الخلود في النار أصلح. فقد تجانفوا وجعلوا نفوسهم ضحكة للخلق. ثم نقول لهم : إذا كانت قدرة الكفر وقدرة الإيمان واحدة، فقد أعطى الله تعالى كلا من الممتحنين تلك القدرة ، فلم قلتم أن الله تعالى فعل بعباده، ما هو الأصلح لهم وهو لم يعطهم إلا ما هو صالح للأمرين جميعا خصوصا في حق من علم أنه يكفر ولا يؤمن؟ وبم كنتم أولى ممن يقولون إنه فعل بعباده ما هو الأفسد لهم إذ أعطاهم ما يحصلون به الكفر خصوصا في حق من علم أنه يكفر ؟ وهذا مما لا اتصال لهم عنه، والله الموفق.
Then we say: Is it not from your statement that power (al-qudra) is suitable for both opposites, and that whatever is not suitable for an opposite is not suitable for the opposite of that opposite, but rather what is not suitable for both opposites is compulsion (iḍṭirār)? So there is no avoiding an affirmative. It was said: Do you say that God Most High is compelled or free? And does He do what He does by power or not? So it is necessary to say that He Most High is powerful (qādir), doing what He does by choice and power. It was said: Does not God Most High affirm for Himself power over that which, if He did it, all His servants would disbelieve in Him, by His saying: 'And if God had extended provision to His servants, they would have transgressed in the earth' and His saying: 'And were it not that mankind would become one community, We would have made for those who disbelieve in the Most Merciful, for their houses, ceilings of silver and stairways upon which they ascend'? So there is no avoiding an affirmative. It was said: If He is powerful over that which, if He did it, the creation would disbelieve, do you describe Him as powerful over that which, if He did it, the creation would believe? If they say: No, then they have limited His power to one of the two opposites, and that according to them is compulsion, and holding that is unbelief. And if they say: Yes, we say: Then if He did not do it, He has abandoned what is most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ) for them? If they say: He did not do that because if He did it and then they believed, the reward of their faith would be less, or there would be no reward for their faith, we say: What do you say? Is the small amount of reward for faith most beneficial for them, or eternal punishment in the Fire? If they say: The small amount of reward was most beneficial for them, they have admitted that He abandoned what is most beneficial for them. And if they say: Eternal punishment in the Fire is most beneficial, then they have deviated and made themselves a laughingstock for the people. Then we say to them: If the power for disbelief and the power for belief are one, then God Most High has given each of those tested that power, so why do you say that God Most High did for His servants what is most beneficial for them, while He gave them only what is suitable for both matters, especially regarding one whom He knows will disbelieve and not believe? And in what are you more entitled than those who say that He did for His servants what is most corrupt for them, since He gave them that by which they obtain disbelief, especially regarding one whom He knows will disbelieve? And this is something they cannot escape from. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument against the Mu'tazila regarding divine power (qudra) and the principle of al-aṣlaḥ (the most beneficial). The author proceeds by a series of questions that force the opponent to admit contradictory positions. He begins by establishing that power (qudra) is suitable for both opposites (al-ḍiddān), and that compulsion (iḍṭirār) is when something is not suitable for both. Then he asks whether God is compelled or free, leading to the admission that God acts by choice and power. Next, he cites Quranic verses showing that God has power over actions that would cause all people to disbelieve, and asks whether God also has power to cause universal belief. If the Mu'tazila deny this, they limit God's power to one opposite, which they themselves consider compulsion and thus unbelief. If they affirm it, then why did God not do it? They respond that if He had, the reward for faith would be less, so not doing it is more beneficial. The author then traps them: either the small reward is more beneficial, in which case God abandoned the more beneficial by not giving it, or eternal punishment is more beneficial, which is absurd. Finally, he points out that since the same power is given to all, God gave the same ability to all, including those He knows will disbelieve, so He did not do the most beneficial for them. The argument concludes that the Mu'tazila cannot escape this contradiction.

Key Terms
الأصلح
الأفسد
القدرة
الثواب
النار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's aslah principle means He always does what is best for each individual in this world.
Correction: The Maturidi reply is that God gives the same capacity to all, and people misuse it; the aslah principle cannot be applied to worldly outcomes because God's wisdom may require different outcomes.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if the Mu'tazila say eternal punishment is more beneficial?
A) They are consistent with their principle
B) They have made a correct theological statement
C) They have deviated and become a laughingstock
D) They have proven the aslah principle

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila position leads to an absurd choice between admitting God abandoned the more beneficial or claiming eternal fire is beneficial, and the identical power given to all undermines their aslah claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed God must always give you what is most beneficial. But this lesson shows that His wisdom is deeper. When you face a trial or a loss, do you secretly believe God owes you something better? How can you train your heart to accept that His withholding may be the real mercy, even if you cannot see it? Right now, write down one area where you have been angry at God for not giving you what you thought was 'best' and consciously surrender that to His greater wisdom.
?
You have the same capacity to believe or disbelieve as anyone else. The power is from God, but the choice is yours. Are you using that power to draw closer to Him or to drift away? Be honest: is there a sin you are holding onto because you think 'God gave me this desire, so it's not my fault'? This lesson refutes that. Take a moment to repent and make a firm intention to stop using His gift against Him starting today.
?
If the Mu'tazila were right, God would be forced to make everyone a believer to be just. But He didn't. He gave you the gift of faith while others remain in disbelief. That is not because you deserved it—it is pure grace. Do you truly thank Him for that grace, or do you take it for granted? What is one practical way you can show gratitude today for the faith He has given you, especially when others are deprived of it?
