Refuting Anthropomorphic Obligation: The Flawed Analogy
Al-Nasafi rejects analogy and poses a dilemma about the wise being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why analogical reasoning from human obligations to divine obligations fails, and the dilemma Al-Nasafi poses to the objectors.

Core Idea
God cannot be analogized to a needy human agent.

Before we can properly understand why God is not obliged to give every possible means for obedience, we must first see why the very method of drawing analogies from human behavior to God is flawed. Al-Nasafi opens his refutation by attacking this analogical reasoning and then presents a clever dilemma about the nature of the wise being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on analogy and dilemma
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the first part where Al-Nasafi criticizes the analogy from witnessed to unseen, and the subsequent dilemma about the wise being.
2
Understand the analogy critique
Al-Nasafi argues that analogizing God to a human who fails to prevent wrongdoing leads to absurdities like materialism. Notice how he uses the example of the cuckold (dayyuth) to show the difference.
3
Analyze the dilemma
The dilemma asks: is the obligated wise being needy or self-sufficient? If needy, it cannot apply to God; if self-sufficient, why would He be obligated? Grasp the logical structure.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
See that the objectors are left with two unacceptable options: either they admit God is needy (false) or they deny the obligation (contradiction).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إثباته في الغائب واجبًا من غير اعتبار المعنى. فذلك باطل وقد بينا قبل هذا أن من رأى عبده يزني بأمته فلم يمنع مع القدرة على المنع بل اشتغل بتهيئة أسباب ذلك كان ديوثا سفيها وفي الغائب الأمر بخلافه، وكذا في الشاهد لا قدرة لفاعل ما على إيجاد الأجسام وإنما يقدر الفاعل في الشاهد على بعض الأعراض وعندكم لا يقدر أيضا إلا على إثبات الأفعال في محال هذه الأجسام فلو قسم الغائب على الشاهد لبطل القول بخلق الأجسام وهو قول الدهرية. وإن أثبتم ذلك في الغائب وإن امتنع إثبات مثله في الشاهد أبطلتم كلامكم. ثم يقال : على أي حكيم يجب ما ذكرت من بذل جميع ما في مقدوره لمن دعاه إلى طاعته وطلب رجوعه عن معصيته؟ أعلى حكيم محتاج إلى طاعة المطيع، ورجوع العدو عن العداوة متعذر بكثرة أعوانه وأنصاره وتكاتف حزبه وأوليائه ذليل عند كثرة أعدائه ؟ أم على حكيم مستغن عن طاعة غيره إياه عزيز في ذاته قوي في سلطانه لا يضعفه كثرة الأعداء ولا يقويه كثرة الأولياء؟ فإن قالوا: بالأول. فهو مسلم، ولكن لا وجه إلى تعديته إلى الغائب. وإن قالوا: بالثاني. فهو ممنوع ثم نقول لهم: لو كان الأمر على ما ذكرتم أن الأمر كان مريدًا لطاعة المأمور محبًا لها، وكذا لرجوع العدو لكان ما قلت مسلمًا ولكان يعطي لا محالة ويبذل ما يحتاج إليه المأمور للامتثال ولكن لم قلتم أن البار جل وعلا مريد لطاعة الكافر وأن أمره بها، ومريد لرجوع العدو وإن دعاه إلى الرجوع ونهاه عن العداوة والعصيان؟ ولم قلتم أن الحكيم إذا كان أمره لمن علم أنه لا يأتمر لإلزام الحجة لا لتحصيل المأمور به أنه وجب عليه بذل ما ذكرت؟ ثم نقول: إن أمر الله تعالى للكافر بالإيمان ونهيه إياه عن الكفر مع علمه أنه يكفر ولا يؤمن ما كان ليفعل إذ في فعله تجهيله وتكذيبه فيما أخبر بقوله: لَأَمْلَأَنَّ جَهَنَّمَ () الآية، ومن أراد ذلك وأعان عليه وبذل لتحصيل ذلك أقصى ما في مقدوره لكان من أسفه السفهاء حيث بذل مقدوره في إزالة وإبطال سلطانه ومقدرته، بل كان لإلزام الحجة واستيجابهم التعذيب في النيران بعصيانهم ليصير ما يتحقق به علمه ويتأكد خبره من تعذيبهم خالدًا مخلدًا عدلاً منه وحكمة، وإذا كان الأمر على ذلك لم يكن بذله أقصى ما في مقدوره ونهاية ما في قوته واجبًا بل إعطاء قدر ما يصح به التكليف كاف، وقد مر تقرير هذا في مسألة الإرادة. وهكذا الجواب عن قولهم : إن من اتخذ ضيافة لرجل وأمره بحضوره وأراد ذلك، وعلم أنه لو دعاه ببشر وملاينة لحضر وحصل مراده، ولو فعل ذلك بغلظة وعبوس لم يحضر فإنا نقول لهم: نعم لو أراد حضوره لكان الأمر كما زعمتم، ولكن لم قلتم أنه تعالى أراد الإيمان من الكافر، وكذا ذلك فيمن ينتفع بحضور المدعو وإجابته وهاهنا الأمر بخلافه، وكذا الأمر هاهنا لإلزام الحجة لا لحصول المأمور به على ما قررنا، والله الموفق.
Proving it in the unseen as obligatory without consideration of the meaning. That is false, and we have explained before this that whoever sees his slave committing adultery with his slave-girl and does not prevent despite ability to prevent, but rather busies himself with preparing the causes for that, he is a cuckold (dayyuth), foolish, and in the unseen the matter is contrary to that. Likewise in the witnessed (al-shahid), no agent has power to create bodies; rather, the agent in the witnessed has power only over some accidents, and according to you he also has power only to effect actions in the loci of these bodies. So if the unseen were analogized to the witnessed, the statement about the creation of bodies would be invalidated, and that is the statement of the materialists (al-dahriyya). And if you affirm that in the unseen, even if affirming its like in the witnessed is impossible, you invalidate your own statement. Then it is said: Upon which wise being (hakim) is it obligatory what you mentioned of expending all that is in his power for one who calls him to his obedience and seeks his return from his disobedience? Upon a wise being who is in need of the obedience of the obedient, and the return of the enemy from enmity is hindered by the multitude of his helpers and supporters and the solidarity of his party and allies, and he is humiliated by the multitude of his enemies? Or upon a wise being who is independent of the obedience of others to him, mighty in His essence, strong in His authority, whom the multitude of enemies does not weaken nor the multitude of allies strengthen? If they say: the first. It is granted, but there is no way to transfer it to the unseen. And if they say: the second. It is rejected. Then we say to them: If the matter were as you mentioned, that the commander desires the obedience of the commanded and loves it, and likewise the return of the enemy, then what you said would be granted, and he would surely give and expend what the commanded needs for compliance. But why do you say that the Most High and Exalted desires the obedience of the disbeliever and commands him with it, and desires the return of the enemy even though he calls him to return and forbids him from enmity and disobedience? And why do you say that when the wise being commands one whom he knows will not comply, for the purpose of establishing the proof (ilzam al-hujja) not for attaining the commanded thing, that it is obligatory upon him to expend what you mentioned? Then we say: Indeed, the command of God Most High to the disbeliever to believe and His prohibition to him from disbelief, while He knows that he will disbelieve and not believe, would not be done because in His doing so there would be making him foolish and denying His own report when He said: 'I will surely fill Hell' (Surah 32:13). And whoever intended that and assisted it and expended the utmost of what is in his power to attain that would be among the most foolish of fools, where he expends his power in removing and nullifying his own authority and power. Rather, it is for establishing the proof (ilzam al-hujja) and making them deserve punishment in the fires due to their disobedience, so that what His knowledge realizes and His report confirms regarding their punishment becomes eternal and everlasting, as justice from Him and wisdom. And when the matter is like that, his expending the utmost of what is in his power and the limit of his strength is not obligatory, rather giving enough to make the obligation (taklif) sound is sufficient, and the clarification of this has already passed in the issue of will (irada). And likewise the answer to their saying: 'Whoever prepares a banquet for a man and commands him to attend and desires that, and knows that if he invites him with cheerfulness and gentleness he would attend and his purpose would be achieved, but if he does that with harshness and frowning he would not attend.' So we say to them: Yes, if he desired his attendance, the matter would be as you claim. But why do you say that He Most High desires faith from the disbeliever? And likewise that in the case of one who benefits from the attendance and response of the invitee, while here the matter is contrary to that. And likewise the matter here is for establishing the proof (ilzam al-hujja), not for the attainment of the commanded thing, as we have established. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage is a sustained refutation of an objection that seeks to impose upon God an obligation analogous to that of a human agent. The author employs multiple argumentative steps. First, he denies the validity of analogical reasoning (qiyas) from the witnessed (al-shahid) to the unseen (al-gha'ib) in matters of obligation, because the conditions differ: a human who fails to prevent wrongdoing is blameworthy, but God is not. He further argues that if one analogizes God's creative power to human power, it leads to materialism (qawl al-dahriyya), which is false. Second, he poses a dilemma: the wise being who might be obligated to expend all means could either be a needy being (who needs obedience) or a self-sufficient being (who does not). If the objectors say the first, it is granted but cannot be transferred to God; if they say the second, it is rejected because such a being has no need to give all. Third, he argues that even if one assumes God desires obedience, the objectors must prove that God desires faith from the disbeliever specifically, which is not evident. Moreover, God's commands to those He knows will disobey serve the purpose of establishing the proof (ilzam al-hujja) against them, not fulfilling a need. Fourth, he explains that if God's command were for the sake of attaining obedience, then God would be foolish to expend means when He knows it would fail, but since it is for just punishment, minimal means sufficient for accountability are enough. Fifth, he answers the example of the dinner invitation: if the host truly desired attendance, he would invite gently; but God's purpose is not to make the disbeliever believe but to establish proof, so harshness (i.e., command despite knowledge of non-compliance) is appropriate. The passage thus defends the Maturidi doctrine that God's actions are governed by wisdom, not by anthropomorphic obligations.

Key Terms
إثبات الغائب
الشاهد
الغائب
الدهرية
حكيم
ديوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is obligated to give every possible means for obedience, just as a human ruler would be.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that the analogy is invalid and the obligation only applies to needy beings, not to God.

Checkpoint
What does Al-Nasafi say would happen if we analogize God's power to human power in the witnessed world?
A) It would prove God's omnipotence.
B) It would invalidate the creation of bodies, leading to materialism.
C) It would show God is wise.
D) It would confirm the obligation.

Key Takeaway
The analogy from human obligations to divine obligations fails because God is not a needy being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself expecting God to act according to human standards of justice or responsibility? What does this lesson reveal about the flaw in that expectation, and how can you consciously remind yourself of God's absolute independence when you face situations that seem unjust?
?
If God is truly self-sufficient and not in need of your obedience, what does that mean for your motivation to worship and obey Him? Does it challenge you to move from a transactional relationship (earning rewards) to a reverent one based on His majesty?
?
When you see evil or disobedience flourishing in the world, do you sometimes question why God doesn't intervene more forcefully? How does al-Nasafi's dilemma—that a self-sufficient wise being has no obligation to exert all power—help you trust in God's wisdom even when His actions are not what a human ruler would do?

The Purpose of Divine Command: Proof, Not Need
Al-Nasafi explains that divine commands are for ilzam al-hujja and justice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's command to the disbeliever serves to establish the proof (ilzam al-hujja) and justify punishment, not to fulfill a need or achieve obedience.

Core Idea
God commands to establish proof, not to satisfy a need.

Having refuted the flawed analogy, Al-Nasafi now addresses the core objection more directly. The objectors argue that if God truly desired obedience, He would provide all means. Al-Nasafi counters by showing that God does not desire obedience from the disbeliever in a way that would obligate Him; rather, the command is for establishing the proof against them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the purpose of command
Read the Arabic and translation from 'ثم نقول لهم: لو كان الأمر على ما ذكرتم' to the end. Focus on the argument about ilzam al-hujja and the banquet example.
2
Understand the main claim
Al-Nasafi argues that if God desired obedience, He would give all means. But we do not know that God desires faith from the disbeliever. In fact, His command is for establishing proof.
3
Analyze the banquet analogy
The objectors give an example of a host who should invite gently if he desires attendance. Al-Nasafi responds: Yes, but we don't know God desires attendance. The command is for proof.
4
Reflect on the justice aspect
Al-Nasafi mentions that the command and punishment are just; God's knowledge and promise are fulfilled.

محتاج إلى طاعة المطيع، ورجوع العدو عن العداوة متعذر بكثرة أعوانه وأنصاره وتكاتف حزبه وأوليائه ذليل عند كثرة أعدائه ؟ أم على حكيم مستغن عن طاعة غيره إياه عزيز في ذاته قوي في سلطانه لا يضعفه كثرة الأعداء ولا يقويه كثرة الأولياء؟ فإن قالوا: بالأول. فهو مسلم، ولكن لا وجه إلى تعديته إلى الغائب. وإن قالوا: بالثاني. فهو ممنوع ثم نقول لهم: لو كان الأمر على ما ذكرتم أن الأمر كان مريدًا لطاعة المأمور محبًا لها، وكذا لرجوع العدو لكان ما قلت مسلمًا ولكان يعطي لا محالة ويبذل ما يحتاج إليه المأمور للامتثال ولكن لم قلتم أن البار جل وعلا مريد لطاعة الكافر وأن أمره بها، ومريد لرجوع العدو وإن دعاه إلى الرجوع ونهاه عن العداوة والعصيان؟ ولم قلتم أن الحكيم إذا كان أمره لمن علم أنه لا يأتمر لإلزام الحجة لا لتحصيل المأمور به أنه وجب عليه بذل ما ذكرت؟ ثم نقول: إن أمر الله تعالى للكافر بالإيمان ونهيه إياه عن الكفر مع علمه أنه يكفر ولا يؤمن ما كان ليفعل إذ في فعله تجهيله وتكذيبه فيما أخبر بقوله: لَأَمْلَأَنَّ جَهَنَّمَ () الآية، ومن أراد ذلك وأعان عليه وبذل لتحصيل ذلك أقصى ما في مقدوره لكان من أسفه السفهاء حيث بذل مقدوره في إزالة وإبطال سلطانه ومقدرته، بل كان لإلزام الحجة واستيجابهم التعذيب في النيران بعصيانهم ليصير ما يتحقق به علمه ويتأكد خبره من تعذيبهم خالدًا مخلدًا عدلاً منه وحكمة، وإذا كان الأمر على ذلك لم يكن بذله أقصى ما في مقدوره ونهاية ما في قوته واجبًا بل إعطاء قدر ما يصح به التكليف كاف، وقد مر تقرير هذا في مسألة الإرادة. وهكذا الجواب عن قولهم : إن من اتخذ ضيافة لرجل وأمره بحضوره وأراد ذلك، وعلم أنه لو دعاه ببشر وملاينة لحضر وحصل مراده، ولو فعل ذلك بغلظة وعبوس لم يحضر فإنا نقول لهم: نعم لو أراد حضوره لكان الأمر كما زعمتم، ولكن لم قلتم أنه تعالى أراد الإيمان من الكافر، وكذا ذلك فيمن ينتفع بحضور المدعو وإجابته وهاهنا الأمر بخلافه، وكذا الأمر هاهنا لإلزام الحجة لا لحصول المأمور به على ما قررنا، والله الموفق.
Proving it in the unseen as obligatory without consideration of the meaning. That is false, and we have explained before this that whoever sees his slave committing adultery with his slave-girl and does not prevent despite ability to prevent, but rather busies himself with preparing the causes for that, he is a cuckold (dayyuth), foolish, and in the unseen the matter is contrary to that. Likewise in the witnessed (al-shahid), no agent has power to create bodies; rather, the agent in the witnessed has power only over some accidents, and according to you he also has power only to effect actions in the loci of these bodies. So if the unseen were analogized to the witnessed, the statement about the creation of bodies would be invalidated, and that is the statement of the materialists (al-dahriyya). And if you affirm that in the unseen, even if affirming its like in the witnessed is impossible, you invalidate your own statement. Then it is said: Upon which wise being (hakim) is it obligatory what you mentioned of expending all that is in his power for one who calls him to his obedience and seeks his return from his disobedience? Upon a wise being who is in need of the obedience of the obedient, and the return of the enemy from enmity is hindered by the multitude of his helpers and supporters and the solidarity of his party and allies, and he is humiliated by the multitude of his enemies? Or upon a wise being who is independent of the obedience of others to him, mighty in His essence, strong in His authority, whom the multitude of enemies does not weaken nor the multitude of allies strengthen? If they say: the first. It is granted, but there is no way to transfer it to the unseen. And if they say: the second. It is rejected. Then we say to them: If the matter were as you mentioned, that the commander desires the obedience of the commanded and loves it, and likewise the return of the enemy, then what you said would be granted, and he would surely give and expend what the commanded needs for compliance. But why do you say that the Most High and Exalted desires the obedience of the disbeliever and commands him with it, and desires the return of the enemy even though he calls him to return and forbids him from enmity and disobedience? And why do you say that when the wise being commands one whom he knows will not comply, for the purpose of establishing the proof (ilzam al-hujja) not for attaining the commanded thing, that it is obligatory upon him to expend what you mentioned? Then we say: Indeed, the command of God Most High to the disbeliever to believe and His prohibition to him from disbelief, while He knows that he will disbelieve and not believe, would not be done because in His doing so there would be making him foolish and denying His own report when He said: 'I will surely fill Hell' (Surah 32:13). And whoever intended that and assisted it and expended the utmost of what is in his power to attain that would be among the most foolish of fools, where he expends his power in removing and nullifying his own authority and power. Rather, it is for establishing the proof (ilzam al-hujja) and making them deserve punishment in the fires due to their disobedience, so that what His knowledge realizes and His report confirms regarding their punishment becomes eternal and everlasting, as justice from Him and wisdom. And when the matter is like that, his expending the utmost of what is in his power and the limit of his strength is not obligatory, rather giving enough to make the obligation (taklif) sound is sufficient, and the clarification of this has already passed in the issue of will (irada). And likewise the answer to their saying: 'Whoever prepares a banquet for a man and commands him to attend and desires that, and knows that if he invites him with cheerfulness and gentleness he would attend and his purpose would be achieved, but if he does that with harshness and frowning he would not attend.' So we say to them: Yes, if he desired his attendance, the matter would be as you claim. But why do you say that He Most High desires faith from the disbeliever? And likewise that in the case of one who benefits from the attendance and response of the invitee, while here the matter is contrary to that. And likewise the matter here is for establishing the proof (ilzam al-hujja), not for the attainment of the commanded thing, as we have established. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage is a sustained refutation of an objection that seeks to impose upon God an obligation analogous to that of a human agent. The author employs multiple argumentative steps. First, he denies the validity of analogical reasoning (qiyas) from the witnessed (al-shahid) to the unseen (al-gha'ib) in matters of obligation, because the conditions differ: a human who fails to prevent wrongdoing is blameworthy, but God is not. He further argues that if one analogizes God's creative power to human power, it leads to materialism (qawl al-dahriyya), which is false. Second, he poses a dilemma: the wise being who might be obligated to expend all means could either be a needy being (who needs obedience) or a self-sufficient being (who does not). If the objectors say the first, it is granted but cannot be transferred to God; if they say the second, it is rejected because such a being has no need to give all. Third, he argues that even if one assumes God desires obedience, the objectors must prove that God desires faith from the disbeliever specifically, which is not evident. Moreover, God's commands to those He knows will disobey serve the purpose of establishing the proof (ilzam al-hujja) against them, not fulfilling a need. Fourth, he explains that if God's command were for the sake of attaining obedience, then God would be foolish to expend means when He knows it would fail, but since it is for just punishment, minimal means sufficient for accountability are enough. Fifth, he answers the example of the dinner invitation: if the host truly desired attendance, he would invite gently; but God's purpose is not to make the disbeliever believe but to establish proof, so harshness (i.e., command despite knowledge of non-compliance) is appropriate. The passage thus defends the Maturidi doctrine that God's actions are governed by wisdom, not by anthropomorphic obligations.

Key Terms
إلزام الحجة
البارئ جل وعلا
تجهيله
تكذيبه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God must provide all means for obedience because He commands it.
Correction: God's command is not for His benefit but for establishing proof; He is not obligated to give more than necessary for taklif.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Nasafi, why does God command the disbeliever to believe even though He knows they will not obey?
A) To test them.
B) To establish the proof against them and make them deserve punishment.
C) Because He hopes they will change.
D) To show His power.

Key Takeaway
Divine commands are not expressions of need but means of establishing justice through proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that God's commands are for your benefit alone, forgetting that they also establish His proof against you? What would change in your obedience if you truly believed that every command you disobey will be used as evidence against you on the Day of Judgment?
?
If God's command to the disbeliever is not out of need but for justice, how does that affect your understanding of His wisdom in commanding even when He knows you will fail? Can you name a specific area where you have treated God's command as if He needs your obedience, rather than as a test that holds you accountable?
?
Consider your own prayers and struggles: do you sometimes expect God to make obedience easy for you, as if He must provide all means? How does the lesson's argument that God only gives enough to make taklif sound challenge that expectation? What would it mean for you to take responsibility for your own effort without demanding God's facilitation, trusting that He is just even when obedience is hard?

God's Command Establishes Proof, Not Compliance
Response to an objection about God commanding the disbeliever despite foreknowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's command to the disbeliever is not futile despite foreknowledge of disobedience, and how it serves to establish proof and just punishment.

Core Idea
God's commands are for justice, not for achieving outcomes He knows will not happen.

In this passage, the author responds to a common objection: if God knows a person will not believe, why command them? The answer reveals a key principle of divine justice: the purpose of command is to establish proof (إلزام الحجة) against the disobedient, making them deserving of punishment. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for understanding divine attributes and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense argumentative style; the author anticipates and refutes objections.
2
Identify the objection and the author's answer
Find where the objection is stated (starting with 'وعلا مريد...') and then where the answer begins ('ثم نقول...'). Understand the key move: the purpose is 'إلزام الحجة' not 'تحصيل المأمور به'.
3
Reflect on the host analogy
The author uses an analogy of a feast host. Think about what it illustrates: the difference between wanting compliance and wanting to establish proof. Consider why the analogy works and what it shows about divine wisdom.
The analogy also refutes the claim that God must exert maximum effort.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلا مريد لطاعة الكافر وأن أمره بها، ومريد لرجوع العدو وإن دعاه إلى الرجوع ونهاه عن العداوة والعصيان؟ ولم قلتم أن الحكيم إذا كان أمره لمن علم أنه لا يأتمر لإلزام الحجة لا لتحصيل المأمور به أنه وجب عليه بذل ما ذكرت؟ ثم نقول: إن أمر الله تعالى للكافر بالإيمان ونهيه إياه عن الكفر مع علمه أنه يكفر ولا يؤمن ما كان ليفعل إذ في فعله تجهيله وتكذيبه فيما أخبر بقوله: لَأَمْلَأَنَّ جَهَنَّمَ () الآية، ومن أراد ذلك وأعان عليه وبذل لتحصيل ذلك أقصى ما في مقدوره لكان من أسفه السفهاء حيث بذل مقدوره في إزالة وإبطال سلطانه ومقدرته، بل كان لإلزام الحجة واستيجابهم التعذيب في النيران بعصيانهم ليصير ما يتحقق به علمه ويتأكد خبره من تعذيبهم خالدًا مخلدًا عدلاً منه وحكمة، وإذا كان الأمر على ذلك لم يكن بذله أقصى ما في مقدوره ونهاية ما في قوته واجبًا بل إعطاء قدر ما يصح به التكليف كاف، وقد مر تقرير هذا في مسألة الإرادة. وهكذا الجواب عن قولهم: إن من اتخذ ضيافة لرجل وأمره بحضوره وأراد ذلك، وعلم أنه لو دعاه ببشر وملاينة لحضر وحصل مراده، ولو فعل ذلك بغلظة وعبوس لم يحضر فإنا نقول لهم: نعم لو أراد حضوره لكان الأمر كما زعمتم، ولكن لم قلتم أنه تعالى أراد الإيمان من الكافر، وكذا ذلك فيمن ينتفع بحضور المدعو وإجابته وهاهنا الأمر بخلافه، وكذا الأمر هاهنا لإلزام الحجة لا لحصول المأمور به على ما قررنا، والله الموفق.
And why is He willing the obedience of the disbeliever and commanding it, and willing the return of the enemy even though He invites him to return and forbids him from enmity and disobedience? And why have you said that the wise one, when He commands one whom He knows will not comply, [does so] for establishing the proof, not for achieving the commanded matter, that it is obligatory upon Him to exert what you mentioned? Then we say: Indeed, the command of Allah to the disbeliever to believe and His prohibition of him from disbelief, with His knowledge that he will disbelieve and not believe, would not be done [for compliance] because in doing so there would be rendering Him ignorant and denying His statement when He informed: 'I will surely fill Hell [with the jinn and mankind]' () the verse. And whoever intends that and assists it and exerts to achieve it the utmost of what is in his power would be among the most foolish of the foolish, since he expends his capacity in removing and nullifying his own authority and power. Rather, it is for establishing the proof and making them deserve punishment in the Fire due to their disobedience, so that what His knowledge entails and His report confirms regarding their eternal punishment becomes realized, as justice and wisdom from Him. And if the matter is so, then exerting the utmost of what is in His power and the limit of His strength is not obligatory; rather, giving just enough for the validity of the obligation is sufficient. And this has already been discussed in the issue of will. And likewise is the answer to their statement: 'If a man prepares a feast for a person and commands him to attend and wants that, and knows that if he invites him with cheerfulness and gentleness he would attend and his purpose would be achieved, but if he does so with harshness and frowning he would not attend.' For we say to them: Yes, if He wanted his attendance, then the matter would be as you claimed. But why have you said that He, exalted, wanted faith from the disbeliever? And likewise in the case of one who benefits from the attendance and response of the invitee, and here the matter is otherwise. And likewise, the matter here is for establishing the proof, not for achieving the commanded matter, as we have established. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection against the Maturidi position on divine command and human action. The objector asks why a wise being would command someone He knows will disobey; the implication is futility. The author answers that the purpose of divine command is not necessarily to achieve compliance (تحصيل المأمور به) but to establish the proof (إلزام الحجة) against the disobedient, making them deserving of punishment. He gives the analogy of a host who invites a guest: if the host wants the guest to come, he would be gentle; but God's command is not for His benefit or to achieve faith from the disbeliever; rather, it is for just punishment. The author also refutes the claim that God must exert maximum effort; enough is given to make obligation valid.

Key Terms
إلزام الحجة
التكليف
الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's command to the disbeliever is futile or pointless because He foreknows disobedience.
Correction: The command is not for achieving compliance but to establish proof against the disbeliever, making them deserving of just punishment.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary purpose of God's command to the disbeliever?
A) To actually make him believe
B) To establish the proof against him
C) To test His own knowledge
D) To benefit God Himself

Key Takeaway
God's command to the disbeliever is an act of justice to establish proof, not a futile attempt to change His foreknown outcome.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that God's command to the disbeliever is to establish proof against him, do you realize that every single command from Allah to you is also establishing proof against you? Are you living as if that proof matters, or are you ignoring it? What will you do today to respond to one command you've been neglecting?
?
If God were to command you to do something He knows you will never do, would you still feel obligated? How does this truth affect your understanding of His mercy? Does it make you more grateful for His guidance, or more fearful of His justice? What emotion stirs in your heart when you think about the fact that Allah's commands are for your own good, even when you fail?
?
Are you like the disbeliever who hears the command yet disobeys, thinking that since Allah knows you will disobey, the command is meaningless? How does this passage correct that arrogance? How will you change your attitude toward Allah's commands today, knowing that He gives them to establish proof of His justice and your accountability?

The Servant Has No Rightful Claim Over God
Argument that the servant has no right over God and generosity is voluntary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the servant has no rightful claim over Allah and how divine generosity is defined as voluntary giving, not an obligation.

Core Idea
True generosity is giving what is not owed, not paying a debt.

This passage tackles a key debate in Islamic theology: does God owe anything to His creatures? The Mu'tazilites argued that God must do what is best for humans, but the Maturidi position refutes that by redefining generosity and showing that obligation would negate divine generosity. The author uses logical arguments to show that the servant has no rightful claim on God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the argument unfold naturally.
The passage uses a question-and-answer dialectical style.
2
Identify the two key dichotomies
Notice the two binary choices presented: (1) withholding a right vs. not a right, and (2) generosity as obligatory vs. non-obligatory giving. These structure the refutation.
The author forces the opponent into either an admission or a contradiction.
3
Analyze the analogy from the witnessed world
The Mu'tazilites analogize from human generosity, but the author points out that humans are affected by giving (wealth decreases) whereas God is not. This undermines the analogy.
4
Understand the consequences for Mu'tazilite doctrine
If God is obligated to do the most beneficial, then His actions are necessary, not generous. This contradicts the Qur'anic descriptions of God as generous and bountiful.
The passage concludes that this position amounts to denying God and His messenger.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how this defense protects divine freedom and generosity while affirming that God's actions are just and wise.
This is a key Maturidi position in the debate on divine attributes and human destiny.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليس للعبد حق مستحق على الله: وما تزعمون أن منع ما بالغير إليه حاجة من غير أن ينتفع المانع بالمنع أو يتضرر بالإعطاء بخل فاسد فإنا نقول: منع ما هو حق للمحتاج قبل المانع؟ أم منع ما ليس بحق مستحق للمحتاج؟ فإن قال: بالأول فهو مسلم لكن لا نسلم أن للعبد على الله تعالى حقا مستحقاً وإن قال: بالثاني فهو ممنوع، ثم نقول له: الجود ما هو؟ بذل ما هو واجب على الباذل أم بذل ما ليس بواجب عليه؟ فإن قال: بالأول ظهرت مكابرته، لما أن من قضى دينا عليه لا يعد جوادًا، وإن قال: بالثاني قيل: أتقول إنه تعالى جواد متفضل ذو فضل على العالمين؟ فإن قال: لا فقد أنكر النصوص وخالف الإجماع ووصفه بالبخل إذ كل عالم ليس بجواد فهو بخيل، وإن قال: نعم أقر أنه تعالى يفعل ما يفعل بعباده، غير مؤد حقا واجبًا عليه، وأنه لا حق لغيره قبله بل يعطي ما يشاء من يشاء فضلاً منه، ويحرم من يشاء ما يشاء بعدله، وهو الحكيم في ذلك كله. ثم نقول: ما ذكرت فاسد من وجه آخر لأن ما اعتبرت به من الشاهد غير موجود وهو من لا يضره الإعطاء ولا ينفعه المنع لأن بالإعطاء ينتقص ماله وبالمنع لا ينتقص واعتبار الغائب بشاهد لا يتحقق ثبوته فاسد. ثم نقول: الجواد المتفضل من أعطى ما ليس عليه إعطاؤه وكان له أن يعطي وعلى مذهبهم لا يتصور أن يعطي الله أحدًا ما ليس عليه إعطاؤه إذ كل ما يفعل هو الأصلح بخلقه على التعين وفعل ذلك واجب عليه فلم يكن الله تعالى عندهم جوادًا ولا منفصلاً ولا ذا فضل ولا منعماً ولا محسنًا، وهذا كله تكذيب الله تعالى ورسوله
There is no rightful claim for the servant over Allah: And your claim that withholding something which another has need of, without the withholder benefiting from the withholding or being harmed by giving, is miserly, is false. For we say: Is it withholding that which is a right of the needy over the withholder? Or withholding that which is not a rightful claim of the needy? If he says the first, it is accepted, but we do not accept that the servant has a rightful claim over Allah. If he says the second, it is denied. Then we say to him: What is generosity? Is it spending what is obligatory upon the spender? Or spending what is not obligatory upon him? If he says the first, his stubbornness is shown, because one who pays a debt due from him is not considered generous. If he says the second, it is said: Do you say that He, exalted, is generous, bountiful, possessing favor over the worlds? If he says no, then he has denied the texts, opposed the consensus, and described Him as miserly, since every being that is not generous is miserly. If he says yes, he acknowledges that He, exalted, does what He does with His servants, without paying an obligatory right due from Him, and that there is no right of anyone over Him; rather, He gives what He wills to whom He wills as favor from Him, and deprives whom He wills of what He wills according to His justice, and He is wise in all of that. Then we say: What you mentioned is false from another aspect, because what you analogized from the witnessed world does not exist: that is, one who is not harmed by giving nor benefited by withholding; because by giving his wealth is diminished, and by withholding it is not diminished; and analogizing the unseen to a witnessed thing whose existence is not established is false. Then we say: The generous, bountiful one is he who gives what he is not obliged to give, and it is his prerogative to give. And according to their doctrine, it is inconceivable that Allah gives anyone anything He is not obliged to give, since everything He does is the most beneficial for His creatures determinately, and doing that is obligatory upon Him. Thus, according to them, Allah is not generous, nor bountiful, nor possessing favor, nor a benefactor, nor good; and all of this is denial of Allah and His messenger.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that God is obligated to do what is most beneficial (الأصلح) for His creatures, implying that withholding such benefit would be miserly. The author argues that generosity is giving what is not owed; if giving were owed, it would not be generosity. Since God is described in revelation as generous and bountiful, His giving must be voluntary, not obligatory. The author also criticizes the analogy from human experience: a human may be unaffected by giving or withholding, but God is not analogous because His giving does not diminish Him, and His withholding does not increase Him. The passage concludes that the Mu'tazilite position, by making God's actions obligatory, actually strips Him of the attribute of generosity and leads to denial of revelation.

Key Terms
حق مستحق
الجود
الأصلح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is obligated to do what is best for His creatures and withholding benefit is miserly.
Correction: Generosity is giving what is not owed; if God were obligated, His actions would not be generous. The servant has no rightful claim.

Checkpoint
How does the author define true generosity (الجود) in this passage?
A) Paying what is owed
B) Giving what is not obligatory
C) Giving only to those who deserve it
D) Giving abundantly

Key Takeaway
The servant has no rightful claim on Allah; divine generosity is voluntary giving, not an obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When disaster strikes or a prayer goes unanswered, do you ever feel that God is being unfair? How does this lesson's affirmation that you have no rightful claim on Him change your perspective? Commit to repeating 'I have no claim over You, O Allah' in your next moment of disappointment.
?
Think of a blessing you received recently—perhaps health, wealth, or a relationship. Did you receive it with a sense of entitlement, or did you recognize it as pure favor? Right now, before God, can you honestly say you see it as a gift you did not earn? If not, repent and thank Him.
?
The author says that if generosity were paying a debt, it would not be true generosity. In your relationship with God, do you treat your good deeds as if they create a debt He must repay? Are you secretly tallying a scoreboard? Today, ask God to forgive you for treating His blessings as payments owed, and resolve to worship Him out of love, not obligation.

Defining Generosity: Obligatory vs. Voluntary Giving
Sanusi defines generosity and initiates the refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's definition of generosity (al-jūd) and how the Mu'tazilite claim about divine miserliness is refuted by distinguishing between obligatory and non-obligatory giving.

Core Idea
Generosity is giving what is not owed, not paying a debt.

This passage tackles a key Mu'tazilite argument that God would be miserly if He withholds benefits from His creatures. Sanusi begins by clarifying the very definition of generosity, showing that it only applies to voluntary giving, not obligatory acts. He then draws out the consequences for the Mu'tazilite position.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening refutation
Read the first part of the passage carefully. Sanusi responds to the claim that prohibiting what another needs is miserliness. Notice how he breaks down the claim into a question about the right of the needy.
This step sets up the entire argument.
2
Understand the two-part question
Sanusi asks: is the prohibited thing a right owed to the needy, or not? Trace the two alternatives and how he responds to each. This is the logical framework of the refutation.
The first alternative is accepted but denied in principle; the second is simply denied.
3
Analyze the definition of generosity
Sanusi then asks: is generosity giving what is obligatory or what is not obligatory? Work through the implications of each answer. Notice that if it were obligatory, paying a debt would be generosity, which is absurd.
This definition is central to the argument.
4
Apply the definition to God
If the Mu'tazilite says generosity is giving what is not obligatory, then they must admit God is generous. If they deny God's generosity, they contradict revelation and consensus and implicitly call God miserly. See how Sanusi forces this dilemma.
This step completes the first refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما تزعمون أن منع ما بالغير إليه حاجة من غير أن ينتفع المانع بالمنع أو يتضرر بالإعطاء بخل فاسد فإنا نقول : منع ما هو حق للمحتاج قبل المانع؟ أم منع ما ليس بحق مستحق للمحتاج؟ فإن قال : بالأول فهو مسلم لكن لا نسلم أن للعبد على الله تعالى حقا مستحقاً وإن قال : بالثاني فهو ممنوع، ثم نقول له: الجود ما هو؟ بذل ما هو واجب على الباذل أم بذل ما ليس بواجب عليه؟ فإن قال: بالأول ظهرت مكابرته، لما أن من قضى دينا عليه لا يعد جوادًا، وإن قال: بالثاني قيل : أتقول إنه تعالى جواد متفضل ذو فضل على العالمين؟ فإن قال : لا فقد أنكر النصوص وخالف الإجماع ووصفه بالبخل إذ كل عالم ليس بجواد فهو بخيل، وإن قال : نعم أقر أنه تعالى يفعل ما يفعل بعباده، غير مؤد حقا واجبًا عليه، وأنه لا حق لغيره قبله بل يعطي ما يشاء من يشاء فضلاً منه، ويحرم من يشاء ما يشاء بعدله، وهو الحكيم في ذلك كله. ثم نقول : ما ذكرت فاسد من وجه آخر لأن ما اعتبرت به من الشاهد غير موجود وهو من لا يضره الإعطاء ولا ينفعه المنع لأن بالإعطاء ينتقص ماله وبالمنع لا ينتقص واعتبار الغائب بشاهد لا يتحقق ثبوته فاسد. ثم نقول: الجواد المتفضل من أعطى ما ليس عليه إعطاؤه وكان له أن يعطي وعلى مذهبهم لا يتصور أن يعطي الله أحدًا ما ليس عليه إعطاؤه إذ كل ما يفعل هو الأصلح بخلقه على التعين وفعل ذلك واجب عليه فلم يكن الله تعالى عندهم جوادًا ولا منفصلاً ولا ذا فضل ولا منعماً ولا محسنًا، وهذا كله تكذيب الله تعالى ورسوله وجميع المسلمين في وصفهم الله تعالى بذلك وإنزاله فيما وصف به نفسه بهذه الصفات منزلة المتصف بما لا اتصاف له به المحب أن يحمد بما لم يفعل الطالب شكر ما لم ينعم، وهذا كله مذموم ثم الله تعالى وإن كان لا يتضرر بإعطاء ما قدر عليه من الأصلح ولا ينتفع بالمنع فهو الجواد الكريم، إلا أن الحكمة في منعه لما فيه من تحقيق ما أخبر وعلم على ما أخبر وعلم وجود الله تعالى لا يمنع حكمته ولا ينقصها، وكذا رأفته ورحمته، والله الموفق.
And what you claim that prohibiting what the other has need of, without the prohibitor benefiting from the prohibition or being harmed by giving, is miserliness, is invalid. For we say: Is the prohibition of what is a right of the needy from the prohibitor? Or the prohibition of what is not a right owed to the needy? If he says: the first, then it is accepted, but we do not accept that the servant has a right owed to Allah Almighty. If he says: the second, then it is denied. Then we say to him: What is generosity? Is it giving what is obligatory upon the giver, or giving what is not obligatory upon him? If he says: the first, then his obstinacy appears, because one who pays a debt due from him is not considered generous. If he says: the second, it is said: Do you say that He Almighty is generous, bountiful, possessing bounty over the worlds? If he says: no, then he has denied the texts, opposed consensus, and described Him as miserly, for every being that is not generous is miserly. If he says: yes, then he admits that He Almighty does what He does with His servants, not fulfilling a right obligatory upon Him, and that there is no right for others over Him; rather, He gives to whom He wills what He wills as a favor from Him, and deprives whom He wills of what He wills by His justice, and He is wise in all that. Then we say: What you mentioned is invalid from another aspect, because what you analogize from the witnessed world does not exist: it is one whom giving does not harm and prohibition does not benefit, because by giving his wealth decreases and by prohibition it does not decrease; and analogizing the absent with a witness whose existence is not verified is invalid. Then we say: The generous, bountiful one is he who gives what he is not obliged to give and has the option to give. According to their school, it is inconceivable that Allah should give anyone what He is not obliged to give, since everything He does is the most beneficial for His creation determinately, and doing that is obligatory upon Him. Thus Allah Almighty is not generous according to them, nor bountiful, nor possessor of bounty, nor bestower of favors, nor beneficent. And all of this is a denial of Allah Almighty and His Messenger and all Muslims, in their describing Allah Almighty with that, and placing Him in what He described Himself with these attributes in the position of one described with what he has no description of: one who loves to be praised for what he did not do, one who seeks thanks for what he did not bestow. All of this is blameworthy. Then Allah Almighty, even though He is not harmed by giving what He has power over of the most beneficial, nor benefited by prohibition, He is the Generous, the Noble. However, the wisdom in His prohibition is due to what it contains of realizing what He has informed and known, as He has informed and known. The existence of Allah Almighty does not prevent His wisdom nor diminish it, and likewise His compassion and mercy. And Allah is the Giver of success.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage is a refutation and definition. The author responds to the claim that prohibiting someone in need without benefit to the prohibitor is miserliness. He first distinguishes whether the prohibited thing is a right of the needy or not. If it is a right, the opponent accepts, but the author denies that any creature has a right against God. If it is not a right, the opponent's claim is denied. Then he defines generosity (al-jūd) through a question: is it giving what is obligatory or what is not obligatory? If the former, then paying a debt is not generosity, which is an absurdity. If the latter, then the opponent must admit that God is generous and bountiful because He gives without obligation. If the opponent denies God's generosity, he contradicts revelation and consensus, and implicitly describes God as miserly. If he affirms it, he concedes that God's actions are not owed as rights. The author then gives a second refutation: the analogy from the visible world fails because in the visible world, giving decreases wealth and prohibition prevents loss, but this does not apply to God. Then the author defines the generous bountiful one as one who gives what he is not obliged to give. According to the Mu'tazilite school, God is obliged to do the most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ), so He cannot be generous. This leads to denying God's attributes of generosity, bounty, and favor, which is a denial of explicit texts and Muslim consensus. Finally, the author notes that God is indeed the Generous, Noble, but His wisdom in withholding is based on His knowledge and decree, which do not diminish His wisdom or mercy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Generosity includes giving what is obligatory or owed.
Correction: The passage clarifies that generosity is giving what is not obligatory, as paying a debt is not considered generous.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the correct definition of generosity?
A) Giving what is obligatory
B) Giving what is not obligatory
C) Giving equally to all
D) Giving what benefits the giver

Key Takeaway
Generosity is defined as giving what is not obligatory; therefore, God's generosity is affirmed because He gives without any obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you ask Allah for something, do you truly feel it is a favor from Him, or do you sometimes feel He 'owes' it to you because you have been good or because of your need? The lesson says no creature has a right over Him. How does that realization reshape your dua and your expectations?
?
Think of a time you gave to someone. Was it out of a sense of obligation (e.g., paying a debt, fulfilling a duty) or was it pure generosity? The lesson defines generosity as giving what is not obligatory. How often do you give from that place? What would change in your giving if you practiced true generosity as defined here?
?
If you truly believed that every blessing in your life is a free gift from God, not something He was obliged to give, would your gratitude deepen? Or would you feel insecure? The passage forces you to confront whether you trust God's wisdom when He withholds. Where in your life do you struggle to accept His withholding as wise and just?

Refuting the Analogy and Affirming Divine Generosity
Sanusi gives the second refutation and concludes with divine generosity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's second refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim by showing that the analogy from the visible world fails and that God's generosity is consistent with His wisdom.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilite analogy from human giving fails because God's giving does not diminish Him.

Having established the definition of generosity, Sanusi now delivers a second, more powerful refutation. He shows that the Mu'tazilite use of analogy from human experience is invalid because the human condition of loss through giving does not apply to God. He then concludes that God is truly generous, and His withholding is wise.

Guided Path
1
Read the second refutation
Read the section beginning with 'ثم نقول : ما ذكرت فاسد من وجه آخر'. Notice that Sanusi now attacks the method of analogy (qiyās) used by the Mu'tazilites.
This is a separate argument, building on the first.
2
Identify the flaw in the analogy
The Mu'tazilites analogize God to a human giver who loses wealth by giving. Sanusi points out that this human condition does not apply to God, because God's wealth does not decrease. Therefore, the analogy is invalid.
The key point: the 'witness' (shāhid) does not actually exist.
3
Understand the consequence for the Mu'tazilite position
Since the Mu'tazilites hold that God must do the most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ), they cannot conceive of God giving what is not obligatory. Therefore, they cannot call God generous. Sanusi shows this leads to denying God's attributes of generosity, bounty, and favor.
This is a devastating critique.
4
Affirm divine generosity with wisdom
Sanusi concludes by affirming that God is indeed generous, but His withholding is based on wisdom and knowledge, not miserliness. This harmonizes divine generosity with divine justice.
The final statement anchors the discussion in Quranic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما تزعمون أن منع ما بالغير إليه حاجة من غير أن ينتفع المانع بالمنع أو يتضرر بالإعطاء بخل فاسد فإنا نقول : منع ما هو حق للمحتاج قبل المانع؟ أم منع ما ليس بحق مستحق للمحتاج؟ فإن قال : بالأول فهو مسلم لكن لا نسلم أن للعبد على الله تعالى حقا مستحقاً وإن قال : بالثاني فهو ممنوع، ثم نقول له: الجود ما هو؟ بذل ما هو واجب على الباذل أم بذل ما ليس بواجب عليه؟ فإن قال: بالأول ظهرت مكابرته، لما أن من قضى دينا عليه لا يعد جوادًا، وإن قال: بالثاني قيل : أتقول إنه تعالى جواد متفضل ذو فضل على العالمين؟ فإن قال : لا فقد أنكر النصوص وخالف الإجماع ووصفه بالبخل إذ كل عالم ليس بجواد فهو بخيل، وإن قال : نعم أقر أنه تعالى يفعل ما يفعل بعباده، غير مؤد حقا واجبًا عليه، وأنه لا حق لغيره قبله بل يعطي ما يشاء من يشاء فضلاً منه، ويحرم من يشاء ما يشاء بعدله، وهو الحكيم في ذلك كله. ثم نقول : ما ذكرت فاسد من وجه آخر لأن ما اعتبرت به من الشاهد غير موجود وهو من لا يضره الإعطاء ولا ينفعه المنع لأن بالإعطاء ينتقص ماله وبالمنع لا ينتقص واعتبار الغائب بشاهد لا يتحقق ثبوته فاسد. ثم نقول: الجواد المتفضل من أعطى ما ليس عليه إعطاؤه وكان له أن يعطي وعلى مذهبهم لا يتصور أن يعطي الله أحدًا ما ليس عليه إعطاؤه إذ كل ما يفعل هو الأصلح بخلقه على التعين وفعل ذلك واجب عليه فلم يكن الله تعالى عندهم جوادًا ولا منفصلاً ولا ذا فضل ولا منعماً ولا محسنًا، وهذا كله تكذيب الله تعالى ورسوله وجميع المسلمين في وصفهم الله تعالى بذلك وإنزاله فيما وصف به نفسه بهذه الصفات منزلة المتصف بما لا اتصاف له به المحب أن يحمد بما لم يفعل الطالب شكر ما لم ينعم، وهذا كله مذموم ثم الله تعالى وإن كان لا يتضرر بإعطاء ما قدر عليه من الأصلح ولا ينتفع بالمنع فهو الجواد الكريم، إلا أن الحكمة في منعه لما فيه من تحقيق ما أخبر وعلم على ما أخبر وعلم وجود الله تعالى لا يمنع حكمته ولا ينقصها، وكذا رأفته ورحمته، والله الموفق.

Commentary

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Argument on Divine Favor: The Case of Abu Jahl
Refutation of the Mu'tazilite view using the example of Abu Jahl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dilemma posed against the Mu'tazilite view that God must give the most beneficial to everyone equally.

Core Idea
God's wisdom permits differential favor without miserliness.

This passage presents a powerful refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that God must treat all humans equally. By examining the specific case of Abu Jahl and the Prophet, the author forces the opponent into an impossible dilemma: either God becomes miserly or God is subject to need. Both conclusions are unacceptable in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the translation. Notice the three options presented and how each is refuted.
The passage ends abruptly; the final conclusion is implied.
2
Identify the dilemma
The author asks: why did God withhold favors from Abu Jahl? Then presents three possible answers. Identify each and its consequence.
The first two options lead to disbelief; the third is absurd.
3
Analyze the refutation
Understand why each option is rejected: the first implies miserliness in God, the second implies God is subject to need, the third denies any distinction between a prophet and a disbeliever.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author ends by stating that miserliness applies to the opponents. Consider how this refutes the Mu'tazilite position.
The full argument may continue on a missing page.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول لهم: لماذا منع الله تعالى أبا جهل ما أعطى من الألطاف والمصالح المصطفى عليه السلام، ولا شك أنه لو لم يمنع لكان ذلك أصلح له، أفصار بخيلاً بذلك المنع أم لم يصر بخيلاً؟ أم كان لم يصر بخيلاً لما أنه كان يضره الإعطاء أو ينفعه المنع؟ فإن قالوا: صار بخيلاً كفروا. وإن قالوا: لم يصر بخيلاً لما أن الإعطاء كان يضره أو المنع كان ينفعه كفروا أيضا حيث جعلوه محلاً للحاجات ينتفع بدفعها ويتضرر بانعدام ما يدفعها وإن قالوا: إن الله تعالى ما فضل محمدًا عليه السلام على أبي جهل بشيء بل أعطى كلا منهما غاية ما في مقدوره من الأصلح إذ ليس له أن يعطي شيئًا أحدًا يحرم غيره ذلك الشيء لما فيه من البخل والميل والمحاباة في حق من أعطاه ذلك وكان أبو جهل ممن أنعم الله عليه كما أنعم على النبيين والصديقين والشهداء والصالحين. وهذا محال، فإن عارضوا ببيان طريق الدين وإرسال الرسل والنهي فذاك غير لازم، لأن ذلك كله لإلزام الحجة عليه لا غير، ثم قد بينا أن الوصف بالبخل يتحقق منهم لا منا، وفي
Then we say to them: Why did Allah Almighty withhold from Abu Jahl what He gave of gentle favors and benefits to the Chosen One, peace be upon him? And there is no doubt that if He had not withheld, that would have been more beneficial for him. Did He become miserly by that withholding, or did He not become miserly? Or was it that He did not become miserly because giving would harm Him or prohibition would benefit Him? If they say: He became miserly, they disbelieve. If they say: He did not become miserly because giving would harm Him or prohibition would benefit Him, they also disbelieve, because they make Him a locus of needs such that He benefits by fulfilling them and is harmed by the lack of what fulfills them. And if they say: Allah Almighty did not favor Muhammad, peace be upon him, over Abu Jahl in anything; rather, He gave each of them the utmost of what was in His power of the most beneficial, since He cannot give something to someone while depriving another of that thing because of the miserliness, bias, and partiality in the right of the one to whom He gave it, and Abu Jahl was one upon whom Allah bestowed favors just as He bestowed upon the prophets, the truthful, the martyrs, and the righteous. This is impossible. If they object by [mentioning] the manifestation of the path of religion and sending messengers and prohibition, that is not necessary, because all of that is only to establish the proof against him, nothing else. Then we have already shown that the description of miserliness applies to them, not to us. And [the text ends].
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer refutation. The author poses the specific case of why God withheld certain favors from Abu Jahl that He gave to the Prophet Muhammad. He presents a dilemma: if the opponent says God became miserly by withholding, they disbelieve. If they say He did not become miserly because giving would harm Him or prohibition would benefit Him, they also disbelieve because that implies God is subject to need. A third option is that God did not favor the Prophet over Abu Jahl but gave each the maximum beneficial acts, but this is absurd as it denies the clear distinction between the Prophet and a disbeliever. The author rejects the objection about revelation and prophets, stating that these are only to establish proof. He concludes that the description of miserliness applies to the opponents, not to the Muslims. The text ends mid-sentence with 'وفي', likely continuing on the next page.

Key Terms
ألطاف
مصالح
أصلح
بخيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God must give the same level of favors to every person.
Correction: God may give different favors based on wisdom, and that does not imply miserliness or injustice.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence if someone says that God became miserly by withholding favors from Abu Jahl?
A) They are correct
B) They disbelieve
C) They are merely mistaken
D) They are forgiven

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite view that God must give equally to all leads to impossible consequences, affirming that God's distribution of favors is based on wisdom, not compulsion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God was unfair to you compared to someone else? How does this passage challenge that feeling, and what would it mean for you to embrace God's wisdom in His distribution of favors?
?
Do you find yourself arguing internally with God's decision to give more to others while you struggle? What would it take for you to surrender your sense of entitlement and trust that His choices are perfectly wise?
?
If you truly believed that God withholds not out of miserliness but out of perfect knowledge of what harms or benefits you, how would that change your reaction to your own failures or others' successes?

Divine Attributes

The Rational Argument Against Divine Stinginess
Nasafi presents a rational dilemma to refute divine stinginess

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational dilemma Nasafi poses to those who accuse God of stinginess and why both possible answers lead to disbelief.

Core Idea
God's withholding is wisdom, not stinginess.

Nasafi begins by establishing that descriptions of God must be based on His own attributes, not human standards. He then presents a powerful dilemma regarding Abu Jahl and the Prophet Muhammad, showing that any attempt to accuse God of stinginess or to deny His generosity leads to theological error.

Guided Path
1
Read the foundational principle
Start by reading the opening statement about how Muslims describe God. Notice the warning against attributing to God what He does not possess.
This sets the rule for all that follows.
2
Understand the dilemma regarding Abu Jahl
Follow the two possible answers the opponents might give: either God became stingy or He did not because giving would harm Him. Nasafi shows both lead to disbelief.
The second answer implies God has needs, which is impossible.
3
Consider the third objection
The opponents might claim God treated everyone equally by giving each the maximum possible benefit. Nasafi calls this impossible and addresses their objection about religion.
This third option denies any divine favoritism, contradicting obvious revelation.
4
Learn the final refutation
Nasafi concludes that stinginess is actually attributable to the opponents, not to God, and explains what true favoritism entails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجميع المسلمين في وصفهم الله تعالى بذلك وإنزاله فيما وصف به نفسه بهذه الصفات منزلة المتصف بما لا اتصاف له به المحب أن يحمد بما لم يفعل الطالب شكر ما لم ينعم، وهذا كله مذموم ثم الله تعالى وإن كان لا يتضرر بإعطاء ما قدر عليه من الأصلح ولا ينتفع بالمنع فهو الجواد الكريم، إلا أن الحكمة في منعه لما فيه من تحقيق ما أخبر وعلم على ما أخبر وعلم وجود الله تعالى لا يمنع حكمته ولا ينقصها، وكذا رأفته ورحمته، والله الموفق. ثم نقول لهم: لماذا منع الله تعالى أبا جهل ما أعطى من الألطاف والمصالح المصطفى عليه السلام، ولا شك أنه لو لم يمنع لكان ذلك أصلح له، أفصار بخيلاً بذلك المنع أم لم يصر بخيلاً؟ أم كان لم يصر بخيلاً لما أنه كان يضره الإعطاء أو ينفعه المنع؟ فإن قالوا: صار بخيلاً . كفروا. وإن قالوا: لم يصر بخيلاً لما أن الإعطاء كان يضره أو المنع كان ينفعه كفروا أيضا حيث جعلوه محلاً للحاجات ينتفع بدفعها ويتضرر بانعدام ما يدفعها وإن قالوا: إن الله تعالى ما فضل محمدًا عليه السلام على أبي جهل بشيء بل أعطى كلا منهما غاية ما في مقدوره من الأصلح إذ ليس له أن يعطي شيئًا أحدًا يحرم غيره ذلك الشيء لما فيه من البخل والميل والمحاباة في حق من أعطاه ذلك وكان أبو جهل ممن أنعم الله عليه كما أنعم على النبيين والصديقين والشهداء والصالحين. وهذا محال، فإن عارضوا ببيان طريق الدين وإرسال الرسل والنهي فذاك غير لازم، لأن ذلك كله لإلزام الحجة عليه لا غير ، ثم قد بينا أن الوصف بالبخل يتحقق منهم لا منا، وفي الميل والمحاباة يكون فيما تجاوز الفاعل حد الحكمة ويفضل من يفضل لهواه. ثم نقول لهم: مالكم توجبون التسوية وقد سمعتم قول الله تعالى: ﴿ يَخْتَصُّ بِرَحْمَتِهِ مَن يَشَاءُ ﴾ الآية، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ يُؤتي الحِكْمَةَ مَن يَشَاءُ ﴾ وقوله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّ اللَّهَ اصْطَفَى ءَادَمَ وَنُوحًا وَءَالَ إِبْرَاهِيمَ وَعَالَ عِمْرَانَ ﴾؟ ومالكم توجبون التسوية بين هؤلاء وبين من أخبر عنهم بقوله ﴿ وَجَعَلْنَا عَلَى قُلُوبِهِمْ أَكِنَّةً أَن يَفْقَهُوهُ ﴾؟ وكيف تسوي بين من قال الله تعالى فيهم: ﴿ إِنَّمَا يُرِيدُ اللهُ لِيُذْهِبَ عَنكُمُ الرِّجْسَ أَهْلَ الْبَيْتِ وَيُطَهِّرَكُمْ تَطْهِيرًا ﴾ وبين من قال فيهم: ﴿ كَذَلِكَ يَجْعَلُ اللَّهُ الرِّجْسَ عَلَى الَّذِينَ لَا يُؤْمِنُونَ ﴾؟ أو بين من قال فيهم: ﴿ فَأُولَئِكَ مَعَ الَّذِينَ أَنْعَمَ اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِم مِّنَ النَّبِيِّينَ والصِّدِّيقِينَ وَالشُّهَدَاءِ وَالصَّلِحِينَ ﴾ وبين من قال فيهم: ﴿ خَتَمَ اللَّهُ عَلَى قُلُوبِهِمْ ﴾ وبين من قال فيهم: ﴿ وَكَذَلِكَ جَعَلْنَكُمْ أُمَّةً وَسَطًا ﴾ وبين من قال: ﴿ وَكَذَلِكَ جَعَلْنَا لِكُلِّ نَبِي عَدُوًا مِنَ الْمُجْرِمِينَ﴾.
And all Muslims, in their describing God Almighty with that and in their applying to Him what He described Himself with these attributes, [are] in the position of one who describes [another] with what he does not possess, like the one who loves to be praised for what he did not do, the one who seeks thanks for what he did not bestow—and all of this is blameworthy. Then God Almighty, even if He is not harmed by giving what He is capable of of the most beneficial, nor benefited by withholding, He is the Generous, the Bountiful. However, the wisdom in His withholding is due to what it contains of the realization of what He informed and knew, according to what He informed and knew. The existence of God Almighty does not prevent His wisdom nor diminish it, and likewise His compassion and mercy. And God is the Grantor of success. Then we say to them: Why did God Almighty withhold from Abu Jahl what He gave of favors and benefits to the Chosen One, peace be upon him? And there is no doubt that if He had not withheld, that would have been more beneficial for him. Did He become stingy by that withholding, or did He not become stingy? Or did He not become stingy because giving would have harmed Him or withholding would have benefited Him? If they say: He became stingy, they disbelieve. And if they say: He did not become stingy because giving would have harmed Him or withholding would have benefited Him, they also disbelieve, since they made Him a locus of needs, benefiting from fulfilling them and being harmed by the lack of what fulfills them. And if they say: God Almighty did not favor Muhammad, peace be upon him, over Abu Jahl in anything, but rather gave each of them the utmost of what is possible for him of the most beneficial, since He cannot give something to someone while depriving another of that thing because of the stinginess, inclination, and favoritism involved toward the one to whom He gave it, and that Abu Jahl was among those upon whom God bestowed favors just as He bestowed upon the prophets, the truthful, the martyrs, and the righteous—this is impossible. And if they object by [mentioning] the clarification of the path of religion, the sending of messengers, and the prohibition, that is not necessary, because all of that is only for establishing the proof against him. Then we have explained that the description of stinginess is realized from them, not from us. And inclination and favoritism occur when the agent exceeds the limit of wisdom and favors one based on his whim. Then we say to them: What is the matter with you that you necessitate equality, while you have heard the saying of God Almighty: "He singles out for His mercy whom He wills"—the verse—and His saying: "He gives wisdom to whom He wills," and His saying: "Indeed, God chose Adam, Noah, the family of Abraham, and the family of Imran"? And what is the matter with you that you necessitate equality between these and those about whom He informed by His saying: "And We have placed over their hearts coverings lest they understand it"? And how can you equate between those about whom God Almighty said: "God only intends to remove impurity from you, O people of the house, and to purify you with [thorough] purification," and those about whom He said: "Thus does God place impurity upon those who do not believe"? Or between those about whom He said: "So those are with those whom God has bestowed favor upon of the prophets, the truthful, the martyrs, and the righteous," and those about whom He said: "God has set a seal upon their hearts"? And between those about whom He said: "And thus We have made you a middle nation"—the verse—and those about whom He said: "And thus We have made for every prophet an enemy from among the criminals"?
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage is a rational and scriptural refutation of the accusation that God's differential treatment of individuals—specifically favoring prophets over disbelievers—implies stinginess or injustice. The argument proceeds in two main parts.

First, the author begins by establishing a foundational principle: all Muslims agree that when describing God with attributes He Himself has stated, we must not liken Him to created beings. To do so would be like praising someone for what they did not do or thanking them for what they did not give—both blameworthy. God, however, is not harmed by giving nor benefited by withholding; He is the Generous, the Bountiful. Yet there is wisdom in His withholding, because it serves to actualize what He has informed and known. This does not diminish His wisdom, compassion, or mercy.

Then the author poses a dilemma to the opponents regarding the case of Abu Jahl: Why did God withhold from Abu Jahl the favors He gave to the Prophet Muhammad? If the opponents say that God became stingy by that withholding, they commit disbelief by attributing stinginess to God. If they say He did not become stingy because giving would harm Him or withholding benefit Him, they also commit disbelief by making God subject to needs—implying He benefits from giving and is harmed by withholding. If they go further and claim that God gave each person the maximum possible benefit, and that He cannot give to one while depriving another because that would entail stinginess or favoritism, this too is impossible, for it would mean that God did not favor the Prophet over Abu Jahl at all, which contradicts obvious facts.

If the opponents object by citing the need to clarify religion, send messengers, and forbid evil, the author replies that these are only for establishing the proof against creatures, not because God is benefitting or being harmed. The accusation of stinginess actually applies to the opponents, not to God. Moreover, true favoritism occurs only when the agent exceeds the bounds of wisdom and acts on whim—which cannot be attributed to God.

Second, the author turns to scriptural proofs, asking how the opponents can demand equality when the Qur'an clearly states that God singles out for mercy whom He wills (Q. Al 'Imran 3:74), gives wisdom to whom He wills (Q. Al-Baqarah 2:269), and chose Adam, Noah, and the families of Abraham and Imran (Q. Al 'Imran 3:33). How can they equate those whom God has sealed their hearts (Q. Al-An'am 6:25) with those He purified (Q. Al-Ahzab 33:33)? How can they equate those who are with the prophets, truthful, martyrs, and righteous (Q. An-Nisa 4:69) with those on whose hearts God has set a seal (Q. Al-Baqarah 2:7)? How can they equate the middle nation (Q. Al-Baqarah 2:143) with the enemies of prophets (Q. Al-Furqan 25:31)? Thus, the argument concludes that God's differential treatment is a matter of divine wisdom and will, not stinginess or injustice.

Key Terms
البخل
الأصلح
الحكمة
الميل والمحاباة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's giving to some and withholding from others implies stinginess or injustice.
Correction: God is not harmed or benefited by giving or withholding; His actions are based on wisdom, not stinginess.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what do the opponents commit if they say God did not become stingy because giving would harm Him?
A) They affirm God's generosity.
B) They commit disbelief by making God subject to needs.
C) They correctly deny stinginess from God.
D) They affirm that God is free from benefit and harm.

Key Takeaway
God's withholding is an act of wisdom, not stinginess, and any attempt to accuse Him of stinginess or to subject Him to needs constitutes disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face hardship or see others suffer, do you ever whisper in your heart that God is being stingy with you? How does this lesson challenge that thought, and what must you say to yourself the next time that whisper arises?
?
You likely believe God is wise, but do you truly live as if His withholding is better for you than His giving? What is one area of your life where you find it hardest to accept His withholding as wisdom, and what would it take for you to surrender that area to Him tonight?
?
This lesson warns against making God like a creature who benefits from your worship or is harmed by your sin. Examine your heart: do you sometimes act as if your obedience is a favor to God, or as if your sin hurts Him? How can you cultivate the awe and adoration that befits His absolute self-sufficiency?

Scriptural Proofs for Divine Selection
Nasafi uses Qur'anic verses to refute the demand for equality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses Qur'anic verses to refute the demand for equality and to affirm God's right to choose whom He favors.

Core Idea
The Qur'an reveals God's exclusive right to choose.

After the rational argument, Nasafi turns to scripture to definitively silence those who demand equal treatment for all. He cites multiple verses showing that God singles out individuals for mercy, wisdom, and prophethood, while others are distanced. Scriptural authority makes the claim of necessary equality untenable.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening question
Nasafi begins with a rhetorical question: 'What is the matter with you that you necessitate equality?' This sets the stage for scriptural counter-evidence.
He directly challenges the opponents' assumption.
2
Examine the cited verses about divine choice
Read the verses: 'He singles out for His mercy whom He wills' (3:74), 'He gives wisdom to whom He wills' (2:269), and the verse about chosen families (3:33). Notice how each emphasizes God's exclusive prerogative.
These verses directly contradict the demand for equality.
3
Contrast the two groups
Nasafi contrasts those whom God purifies (like the People of the House) with those upon whom He places impurity (like the disbelievers). Similarly, he contrasts the righteous with the sealed hearts.
The contrasts show that God treats people differently based on His wisdom.
4
Reflect on the final contrasts
The last set of verses contrasts the 'middle nation' with the 'enemies of the prophets'. This reinforces the idea that God's differentiation is deliberate and just.
Nasafi's conclusion is implicit: the Qur'an itself affirms divine selection, so the demand for equality is invalid.
