جَعَلْنَكُمْ أُمَّةً وَسَطًا ﴾ وبين من قال: ﴿ وَكَذَلِكَ جَعَلْنَا لِكُلِّ نَبِي عَدُوًا مِنَ الْمُجْرِمِينَ﴾.
And all Muslims, in their describing God Almighty with that and in their applying to Him what He described Himself with these attributes, [are] in the position of one who describes [another] with what he does not possess, like the one who loves to be praised for what he did not do, the one who seeks thanks for what he did not bestow—and all of this is blameworthy. Then God Almighty, even if He is not harmed by giving what He is capable of of the most beneficial, nor benefited by withholding, He is the Generous, the Bountiful. However, the wisdom in His withholding is due to what it contains of the realization of what He informed and knew, according to what He informed and knew. The existence of God Almighty does not prevent His wisdom nor diminish it, and likewise His compassion and mercy. And God is the Grantor of success. Then we say to them: Why did God Almighty withhold from Abu Jahl what He gave of favors and benefits to the Chosen One, peace be upon him? And there is no doubt that if He had not withheld, that would have been more beneficial for him. Did He become stingy by that withholding, or did He not become stingy? Or did He not become stingy because giving would have harmed Him or withholding would have benefited Him? If they say: He became stingy, they disbelieve. And if they say: He did not become stingy because giving would have harmed Him or withholding would have benefited Him, they also disbelieve, since they made Him a locus of needs, benefiting from fulfilling them and being harmed by the lack of what fulfills them. And if they say: God Almighty did not favor Muhammad, peace be upon him, over Abu Jahl in anything, but rather gave each of them the utmost of what is possible for him of the most beneficial, since He cannot give something to someone while depriving another of that thing because of the stinginess, inclination, and favoritism involved toward the one to whom He gave it, and that Abu Jahl was among those upon whom God bestowed favors just as He bestowed upon the prophets, the truthful, the martyrs, and the righteous—this is impossible. And if they object by [mentioning] the clarification of the path of religion, the sending of messengers, and the prohibition, that is not necessary, because all of that is only for establishing the proof against him. Then we have explained that the description of stinginess is realized from them, not from us. And inclination and favoritism occur when the agent exceeds the limit of wisdom and favors one based on his whim. Then we say to them: What is the matter with you that you necessitate equality, while you have heard the saying of God Almighty: "He singles out for His mercy whom He wills"—the verse—and His saying: "He gives wisdom to whom He wills," and His saying: "Indeed, God chose Adam, Noah, the family of Abraham, and the family of Imran"? And what is the matter with you that you necessitate equality between these and those about whom He informed by His saying: "And We have placed over their hearts coverings lest they understand it"? And how can you equate between those about whom God Almighty said: "God only intends to remove impurity from you, O people of the house, and to purify you with [thorough] purification," and those about whom He said: "Thus does God place impurity upon those who do not believe"? Or between those about whom He said: "So those are with those whom God has bestowed favor upon of the prophets, the truthful, the martyrs, and the righteous," and those about whom He said: "God has set a seal upon their hearts"? And between those about whom He said: "And thus We have made you a middle nation"—the verse—and those about whom He said: "And thus We have made for every prophet an enemy from among the criminals"?
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage is a rational and scriptural refutation of the accusation that God's differential treatment of individuals—specifically favoring prophets over disbelievers—implies stinginess or injustice. The argument proceeds in two main parts.

First, the author begins by establishing a foundational principle: all Muslims agree that when describing God with attributes He Himself has stated, we must not liken Him to created beings. To do so would be like praising someone for what they did not do or thanking them for what they did not give—both blameworthy. God, however, is not harmed by giving nor benefited by withholding; He is the Generous, the Bountiful. Yet there is wisdom in His withholding, because it serves to actualize what He has informed and known. This does not diminish His wisdom, compassion, or mercy.

Then the author poses a dilemma to the opponents regarding the case of Abu Jahl: Why did God withhold from Abu Jahl the favors He gave to the Prophet Muhammad? If the opponents say that God became stingy by that withholding, they commit disbelief by attributing stinginess to God. If they say He did not become stingy because giving would harm Him or withholding benefit Him, they also commit disbelief by making God subject to needs—implying He benefits from giving and is harmed by withholding. If they go further and claim that God gave each person the maximum possible benefit, and that He cannot give to one while depriving another because that would entail stinginess or favoritism, this too is impossible, for it would mean that God did not favor the Prophet over Abu Jahl at all, which contradicts obvious facts.

If the opponents object by citing the need to clarify religion, send messengers, and forbid evil, the author replies that these are only for establishing the proof against creatures, not because God is benefitting or being harmed. The accusation of stinginess actually applies to the opponents, not to God. Moreover, true favoritism occurs only when the agent exceeds the bounds of wisdom and acts on whim—which cannot be attributed to God.

Second, the author turns to scriptural proofs, asking how the opponents can demand equality when the Qur'an clearly states that God singles out for mercy whom He wills (Q. Al 'Imran 3:74), gives wisdom to whom He wills (Q. Al-Baqarah 2:269), and chose Adam, Noah, and the families of Abraham and Imran (Q. Al 'Imran 3:33). How can they equate those whom God has sealed their hearts (Q. Al-An'am 6:25) with those He purified (Q. Al-Ahzab 33:33)? How can they equate those who are with the prophets, truthful, martyrs, and righteous (Q. An-Nisa 4:69) with those on whose hearts God has set a seal (Q. Al-Baqarah 2:7)? How can they equate the middle nation (Q. Al-Baqarah 2:143) with the enemies of prophets (Q. Al-Furqan 25:31)? Thus, the argument concludes that God's differential treatment is a matter of divine wisdom and will, not stinginess or injustice.

Key Terms
التسوية
الاصطفاء
الرحمة
الحكمة
الرجس
الأكنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God must treat all people equally; otherwise He is unjust.
Correction: The Qur'an explicitly shows that God chooses and favors some over others according to His wisdom, and this is just.

Checkpoint
Which verse does Nasafi cite to show that God gives wisdom to whom He wills?
A) 'He singles out for His mercy whom He wills'
B) 'He gives wisdom to whom He wills'
C) 'Indeed, God chose Adam...'
D) 'And thus We have made you a middle nation'

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an clearly shows that God differentiates between people by His wisdom, making the demand for equality both unscriptural and illogical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What if you are among those whose hearts are covered, yet you do not realise it? How would you know? What signs from this lesson can you use to examine your own heart today?
?
Do you secretly resent that God has given others more than you—more intelligence, wealth, or status? How does this lesson challenge you to submit to God's exclusive right to choose, even when it is not in your favour?
?
If you are part of the 'middle nation' (the Muslim community), what responsibility does that privilege carry? In what specific area of your life are you failing to live up to that favour, and what will you change starting today?

Refuting Reward as Explanation for Divine Favor
Dialectical refutation of reward as explanation for divine favor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical problems with claiming that God's special favors to believers are merely rewards for their merit.

Core Idea
Reward cannot explain why God grants favor before it is deserved.

Imam al-Nasafi begins by examining the claim that what God does for believers is simply reward. He uses a series of conditional questions to expose the inconsistency in this view. The passage guides us through a dialectical method that challenges the very notion of reward as the sole explanation for divine distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on reward
Focus on the first part of the passage where the author addresses the believers' case. Identify the two conditional branches.
The author uses 'if they say... it is said' structure.
2
Understand the first branch
If the reward provides assistance toward good, then why wasn't it given earlier when it would be more beneficial? This implies God withheld the best.
This challenges the Mu'tazilite principle of al-aslah.
3
Understand the second branch
If the reward does not provide assistance, then it has no benefit and cannot be a reward.
4
Consider the disbeliever case briefly
The author also asks whether the same 'meaning' would cause a disbeliever to believe. If yes, then preventing it is unjust; if no, then it's not beneficial.
This transitions to the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يزعمون أن ما فعل الله بالفريقين مما ذكر في الآيات كان بطريق المثوبة للمؤمنين وبطريق العقوبة للكافرين. فاسد، لأنا نقول: ما فعل من ذلك بطريق الثواب هل كان به فضل معونة على الخير والطاعة أم لا؟ فإن قالوا: نعم. قيل: ولم لم يعطه ذلك قبل استحقاقه بطريق الثواب وهو أنفع له وأصلح فقد منع الأصلح إذا قبل استجابة ذلك بفعله. وإن قالوا: ليس يحصل به فضل معونة. قيل: لا فائدة إذا به ولا منه، ولم يكن هو نعمة فلا معنى لجعله ثوابًا. وكذا يقال: هل هذا المعنى بحيث لو فعل الكافر لآمن؟ إن قالوا: لا. فإذا لا فائدة فيه فلا يصلح ثوابًا. وإن قالوا: نعم. قيل: فقد منع الكافر ذلك. فأما ما فعل بالكافر فنقول: أبقى الكفار بعد ما فعل بهم من الختم والطبع وما ذكرنا ممتحنين أم لا؟ فإن قالوا: لا. فقد جعلوهم فيما يعتقدونه ويفترون به على الله تعالى ويرتكبون من أنواع المعاصي معذورين، وجعلوا هذه الأفعال لهم غير جائزة المؤاخذة، والقول بذلك كفر وخروج أيضا عن إجماع أهل القبلة. وإن قالوا: هم بعد ما فعل بهم من الختم والطبع وغير ذلك ممتحنون، قلنا: فإذا كانوا ممتحنين فأي الأمرين أصلح لهم في الدين وجود هذه المعاني أم ارتفاعها؟ فإن قالوا: وجودها. جعلوا ما هو من الله تعالى عقوبة لهم من ختم القلوب وطبعها وجعل الغشاوة على أبصارهم أصلح لهم في الدين من تركها، وهذا جهل عظيم. وإن قالوا: عدمها. فقد أقروا أنه تعالى يفعل بالممتحنين ما ليس بأصلح لهم في الدين وقد هدموا جميع قواعدهم. ثم يقال: وأي ضرر عليهم في الختم والطبع ولم يتألموا بهما في الدنيا بل انتقموا بهما حيث كانا أصلح لهم في أمر دينهم حتى ليعاقبهم الله بذلك على
And their claim that what God did to the two groups mentioned in the verses was by way of reward for the believers and by way of punishment for the disbelievers is invalid. For we say: That which He did by way of reward — did it involve a favor of assistance towards good and obedience or not? If they say: Yes. It is said: Why did He not give him that before his deserving it by way of reward, when it would have been more beneficial and better for him? Then he would have prevented what is best if he had responded to that by his action. And if they say: It does not involve a favor of assistance. It is said: Then there is no benefit in it from him nor from it, and it was not a blessing, so there is no meaning in making it a reward. Likewise it is said: Is this meaning such that if the disbeliever did it he would believe? If they say: No. Then there is no benefit in it, so it is not suitable as a reward. And if they say: Yes. It is said: Then he prevented the disbeliever from that. As for what He did to the disbeliever, we say: Did He leave the disbelievers, after what He did to them of sealing and stamping and what we mentioned, as being tested or not? If they say: No. Then they have made them excused in what they believe and fabricate against God and commit of types of sins, and they made these actions not subject to punishment for them, and saying that is disbelief and also a departure from the consensus of the people of the qibla. And if they say: They are, after what He did to them of sealing and stamping and other things, tested. We say: If they are tested, then which of the two matters is better for their religion: the existence of these meanings or their absence? If they say: Their existence. They made what is from God a punishment for them — sealing of hearts and stamping and placing a covering on their eyes — better for their religion than leaving them, and this is great ignorance. And if they say: Their absence. Then they have acknowledged that He Most High does to the tested that which is not best for them in religion, and they have demolished all their principles. Then it is said: And what harm is there to them in the sealing and stamping, and they did not suffer pain from them in this world? Rather, they took revenge through them where they were better for them in their religious affair, so that God would punish them for that...
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation of the claim that God's differential treatment of believers and disbelievers is purely a matter of reward and punishment. The author uses conditional arguments to expose internal contradictions. He first addresses the case of believers: if God's action (presumably granting guidance or favor) is a reward that provides assistance toward good, why was it not given before the believer deserved it, since it would have been more beneficial? This would imply God withheld the best, which contradicts His wisdom. If the action does not provide assistance, then it has no benefit and cannot be a reward. Similarly, regarding disbelievers: if the same meaning (guidance) would lead a disbeliever to belief, then God withholding it from the disbeliever would be a prevention. As for the sealing and stamping of disbelievers, the author asks: Are the disbelievers still tested after being sealed? If not, they are excused for their disbelief, which is absurd and constitutes disbelief itself. If they are still tested, then does the sealing benefit them or harm them religiously? If it benefits them, then punishment is good, which is contradictory. If it harms them, then God does what is not best for them, undermining the Mu'tazilite principle of al-aslah (God must do what is best for His creatures). Finally, the author notes that sealing causes no physical pain in this world, so how can it be a punishment? The argument systematically dismantles the rationalist claim that divine actions must always be explicable as reward or punishment.

Key Terms
ثواب (thawab)
فضل معونة (fadl ma'unah)
أصلح (aslah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God's actions are reward, they must be just and optimal.
Correction: The passage shows that reward as an explanation leads to contradictions regarding timing and benefit, thus it cannot be the sole reason.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what follows if God's action for believers does not involve a favor of assistance?
A) It is still a valid reward because it is from God.
B) It has no benefit and cannot be a reward.
C) It must be given before merit.
D) It is a punishment in disguise.

Key Takeaway
Reward as a concept cannot fully explain divine favor because it leads to logical contradictions about timing and benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself assuming that the blessings you have are because you 'deserve' them? How does this passage challenge the entitlement in that assumption, and what would change if you genuinely believed that no human deserves anything from God?
?
When you see a disbeliever prospering or a believer suffering, do you find yourself questioning God's justice? How does the argument in this passage free you from needing to explain God's actions through human categories of reward and punishment?
?
If God's favor is not a reward for your deeds, then what is the basis of your hope? Will you now depend solely on His mercy, and what practical step can you take today to live in that dependence?

Refuting Punishment as Explanation for Divine Wrath
Dialectical refutation of punishment as explanation for divine wrath.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the contradictions in claiming that God's sealing of disbelievers is merely punishment.

Core Idea
Punishment that excuses sin or causes no pain cannot be just punishment.

Imam al-Nasafi now turns to the case of disbelievers, focusing on the sealing of hearts and stamping. He asks whether disbelievers remain tested after such actions. The answer leads to a dilemma: either they are excused (absurd) or God does what is not best for them (undermining Mu'tazilite principles). The passage concludes with an incomplete argument about the nature of punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on punishment
Begin from 'فأما ما فعل بالكافر' to the end of the provided Arabic. Notice the questions about testing (imtihan).
The author continues the dialectical style.
2
Analyze the first dilemma
If disbelievers are not tested after sealing, then they are excused for their sins, which is kufr. If they are tested, then does sealing help or harm their religion?
3
Explore the second branch
If sealing is beneficial for their religion, then punishment is good—contradictory. If it is harmful, then God does what is not best, undermining al-aslah.
4
Consider the incomplete conclusion
The author suggests that sealing causes no pain in this world, so how can it be punishment? The argument is cut off.
The source ends abruptly; the full rebuttal may continue.

?

Observable Differences Refute Intellectual Equality
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim of equal intellect with observational evidence and an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite claim that all people have equal intellect is contradicted by observable reality and leads to absurd conclusions.

Core Idea
Intellectual differences are as obvious as physical differences — denying them is sheer ignorance.

The Mu'tazilites argued that God does not favor anyone with extra intellect, and that all minds are equal by creation. But we see clear differences in people's abilities. In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi uses direct observation and a powerful analogy to show that denying such differences is absurd. A rational person must seek refuge from such a misguided belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the argument unfold naturally.
Pay attention to the tone – it is a refutation.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
Find the claim that Nasafi refutes: that God gives no one an increase of intellect and all intellects are equal in creation.
3
Examine the observational evidence
Nasafi points to what is seen: some have abundant intellect, sharp minds, while others are dull and slow. Note the contrast in achievements within the same time.
This is a direct appeal to common experience.
4
Consider the analogy
Nasafi argues that if one denies intellectual differences, one could equally deny obvious physical differences like height, strength, etc. This shows the claim leads to absurdity.
The analogy strengthens the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أفضى بهم تماديهم في الضلال إلى أن زعموا أن الله تعالى لم يفضل أحد على أحد بإعطاء زيادة العقل وعقول الناس كلها في الخلقة مستوية، مع ما يرى ويعاين من وفور عقول بعض الناس وجودة قرائحهم وقوة أذهانهم، وقلة عقول البعض وبلادة أفهامهم وكلال خواطرهم، بحيث يحرز واحد من أنواع العلوم وفنون الآداب وودائع العقول وخزائن الأفهام في مدة لا يقدر من وقع منه في الطرف الأقصى من البلادة والغباوة على إحراز جزء من أضعاف ألف جزء وفي تلك المدة، ولو لم يكن هذا تجاهلاً فلا تجاهل في العالم، ولو جاز ذلك لجاز لآخر أن يقول: إن الله تعالى سوى بين الناس في الحسن والدمامة والطول وقصر القامة وقوة البطش ورباطة الجأش والضعف والجبن وغير ذلك مما لا يحصى كثرة، وكل ذلك تجاهل، ومذهب يفضي بصاحبه إلى ارتكاب مثل هذه المحالات ويبدو عواره للبداهة كل هذا الظهور، فكان حقيقا على العاقل أن يستعيذ بالله تعالى منه ويسأل العصمة عن الوقوع فيه وفي أمثاله.
Then their persistence in error led them to claim that God Almighty did not favor anyone over anyone by giving an increase of intellect, and that the intellects of all people are equal in creation, despite what is seen and observed of the abundance of intellects of some people, the quality of their natural dispositions, the strength of their minds, and the lack of intellects of others, the dullness of their understandings, and the sluggishness of their thoughts, such that one person acquires types of sciences, arts of literature, deposits of intellects, and treasures of understandings in a period in which one who falls into the extreme limit of dullness and stupidity cannot acquire a part of a thousandth part in that same period. And if this is not ignorance, then there is no ignorance in the world. And if that were permissible, it would be permissible for another to say: 'God Almighty made people equal in beauty and ugliness, tallness and shortness of stature, strength of assault and firmness of heart, weakness and cowardice, and other things that cannot be counted in abundance.' All of that is ignorance, and a doctrine that leads its adherent to committing such impossibilities, and its defect becomes apparent to common sense with all this clarity. So it is fitting for the intelligent person to seek refuge with God Almighty from it and to ask for protection from falling into it and into its likes.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Mu'tazilite claim that God does not favor any human being over another by giving an increase of intellect, and that all people have equal intellects by creation. The author begins by stating that this claim arises from their persistence in error. Then he presents the plain observational evidence against it: we see that some people have abundant intellects, sharp minds, and quick comprehension, while others are dull and slow, such that in the same period of time one person can master many sciences and arts whereas another cannot grasp even a tiny fraction. If this stark difference were not ignorance, nothing would be. The author uses an analogy: if one accepts the Mu'tazilite denial of observable intellectual differences, one could also deny observable physical differences like beauty, height, strength, etc., which is absurd. Therefore, this doctrine is clearly false to anyone with common sense. In conclusion, a rational person should seek Allah's refuge from such a misguided view and ask for protection from falling into it.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that intellectual equality is a form of divine justice or that God does not favor anyone.
Correction: Observable reality shows clear intellectual differences, and denying them leads to absurd conclusions, as Nasafi demonstrates.

Checkpoint

A) Philosophical arguments about the nature of intellect.
B) Observable differences in people's intellectual abilities.
C) Quotations from the Quran and Hadith.
D) Logical syllogisms based on divine justice.

Key Takeaway
Denying observable intellectual differences leads to absurdities, so the Mu'tazilite claim of equal intellect is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you meet someone whose understanding is far greater than yours, do you feel a twinge of envy, or does it drive you to gratitude that God has placed such minds among humanity—and to humble reliance on Him for your own share of intellect?
?
You have just read that denying intellectual differences leads to absurdities. In your own life, where have you perhaps been blind to the obvious differences God has placed between you and others—intellectually or otherwise—and what would it mean to fully accept His distribution without resentment?
?
The author says it is fitting to seek refuge with Allah from such a misguided view. Have you ever felt a secret desire to be 'equal' to someone more gifted, as if God owed you the same capacity? What would it take for you to truly seek refuge from that ingratitude and embrace His wisdom in the differences He has created?

The Injustice of Pain Without Compensation
The author gives the first objection against the Mu'tazilite analogy: pain without compensation is injustice, and the child cannot consent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite argument that God's infliction of pain on children is justified by eventual compensation fails when children die as disbelievers without receiving any compensation.

Core Idea
Pain without compensation is injustice, especially when the child dies in disbelief.

The Mu'tazilites argued that God's infliction of pain on children is like a father's medical treatment—painful but ultimately beneficial. However, this analogy collapses when considering children who die without compensation. The author presents the first objection: many children die as disbelievers and receive no compensation, making the infliction unjust.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author structures the refutation with enumerated points.
2
Identify the first objection
Find the statement that many children die as disbelievers without compensation. Understand why this is problematic for the Mu'tazilite view.
3
Understand the condition of consent
The author explains that injustice persists until the right-holder is satisfied with compensation, and the child cannot consent at the time of pain. Reflect on why this matters.
This shows that intention to compensate is not enough.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this objection challenges the Mu'tazilite claim that all pain from God is ultimately just. Ask yourself: Does God's knowledge change the moral status?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحصل في العاقبة به حكمة كالأب يحجم صبيه ويسقيه الأدوية الكريهة ويحسن ذلك لأن فيه من منفعة البرء في العاقبة. وهذا الكلام باطل من وجوه: أحدها: أن كثيرا من الأطفال الذين تألموا في صغرهم ماتوا على الكفر ولا ينالون العوض في الآخرة وكان الله تعالى عالما بعاقبة أمرهم عند إيلامهم فيكون الباري عز وجل على هذا ظالما على هذا الطفل حيث آلمه علمه أنه لا مع يعوضه ولأن ما كان ظلما بغير عوض يعتقد ظلما وجورا عند الوجود إلى أن يرضى من له الحق بالعوض فينعقد فعل الله تعالى للحال ظلما ويصير هو ظالما إلى أن يزول أثر الظلم بإيصال العوض وكذا ما يكون ظلما بغير عوض لا يزول عنه معنى الظلم وإن كان الفاعل قصد التعويض ما لم يرض بذلك من له الحق ولا رضا من الصغير وقت الإيلام بالعوض وما ضرب من المثال بفصل الحجامة وسقي الأدوية فهو الذي يدل على بطلان مذهبهم وفساد قولهم فإن الأب إنما لم يخرج بذلك عن الحكمة وأطلق له ذلك الفعل إذا كان لا يقدر على تحصيل البرء والصحة بدون سابقة الإيلام بالحجامة والإضرار بسقي الأدوية الكريهة ولو فعل القدرة على ذلك يعد ظالما سفيها جائرا والله تعالى قادر على إيصال ذلك الذي يوصل إليه بطريق العوض بدون سابقة الإيلام فينبغي أن يكون الإيلام سفها اعتبارا بالشاهد الذي هو مقوله.
For the result in the end is wisdom, like the father who cups his son and gives him bitter medicine and does that well because in it is the benefit of cure in the end. And this statement is false in several ways: One of them: That many of the children who were pained in their childhood died upon disbelief and do not receive compensation in the afterlife, and God Almighty knew the outcome of their affair at the time of their pain, so the Creator, Exalted and Glorious, would on this be unjust to this child because He pained him knowing that he has no compensator. And because what is injustice without compensation is believed to be injustice and tyranny at the time of occurrence until the one who has the right is satisfied with compensation, so the act of God Almighty would at the moment be injustice and He becomes unjust until the trace of injustice is removed by delivering compensation. Similarly, what is injustice without compensation does not lose the meaning of injustice from it, even if the doer intended compensation, as long as the one who has the right is not satisfied with that. And there is no satisfaction from the child at the time of pain with compensation. And the example given of cupping and administering medicines is that which indicates the invalidity of their doctrine and the corruption of their statement. For the father only does not deviate thereby from wisdom and is allowed to do that act if he is not able to attain cure and health without prior pain of cupping and harm of administering bitter medicine. If he were able to do that [without pain], he would be considered unjust, foolish, tyrannical. And God Almighty is able to deliver that which is arrived at by way of compensation without prior pain, so the infliction of pain would be foolishness by analogy with the witnessed realm which is his analogy.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite argument concerning God's infliction of pain on children. The author first presents the Mu'tazilite analogy: just as a father gives his child bitter medicine and cupping for a beneficial outcome, so God's infliction of pain on children has a wise purpose and compensation. The author then declares this argument false in several ways and provides the first objection. The objection argues that many children who suffer die as disbelievers and receive no compensation in the afterlife; God knows this future outcome, so the infliction becomes unjust because it lacks compensation. The author explains that an act is considered unjust as long as the right-holder has not been satisfied with compensation, and the child cannot consent at the time of pain. Furthermore, the analogy of the father is turned against the Mu'tazilites: a father is only excused if he cannot achieve the cure without pain; but God is able to deliver the benefit without any prior pain, so inflicting pain unnecessarily would be foolish (safah) by analogy with human standards (shahid).

Key Terms
إيلام الأطفال
عوض
ظلم
حكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's actions are automatically just regardless of compensation or consent, because He is the owner of all things.
Correction: The author argues that justice requires compensation when harm is inflicted, and the right-holder's consent is necessary. God's ownership does not negate the need for compensation, as seen in the analogy of human justice.

Checkpoint
What is the first objection against the Mu'tazilite argument on inflicting pain on children?
A) Children do not feel pain.
B) Many children die as disbelievers without compensation.
C) God is not capable of compensation.
D) The father analogy is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Pain inflicted without compensation is unjust, especially when the recipient cannot consent and will not receive compensation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have suffered deeply and saw no relief, did you demand that God compensate you? How does this lesson reveal the arrogance in expecting payment for your pain, and will you now surrender your demand for compensation to God's wisdom?
?
Consider a time when you were hurt and the person who hurt you offered compensation later, but you remained angry because they did not seek your consent at the time. How does this mirror the child's position? Will you let go of your resentment against God for the pains He has permitted, knowing that His sovereignty is not bound by human notions of consent?
?
The child cannot consent at the moment of pain. Are you, as an adult, willing to consent to God's decree even when it brings you pain? Or do you, like the Mu'tazilites, insist that your consent must first be obtained? Will you submit to His will today without demanding compensation?

The Failure of the Father Analogy
The author rebuts the Mu'tazilite analogy by asserting God's ability to achieve the benefit without pain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author turns the Mu'tazilite father analogy against them by arguing that God could achieve the benefit without pain, making the infliction foolish.

Core Idea
If God can provide benefit without pain, then inflicting pain is needless and foolish.

In the second part of the refutation, the author directly addresses the Mu'tazilite comparison of God to a father who gives bitter medicine. He argues that a father is only excused because he cannot cure without pain, but God can. Therefore, the analogy backfires and shows that inflicting pain without necessity is unjust and foolish.

Guided Path
1
Read the rebuttal passage
Read the Arabic passage that turns the example of cupping and medicine against the Mu'tazilites. Notice how the author uses their own analogy to disprove their position.
2
Analyze the father's condition
The author explains that a father is only considered wise when he inflicts pain if he cannot achieve the cure without it. Understand this condition.
The father has no alternative but to cause pain.
3
Apply the principle to God
Since God is omnipotent, He can achieve the benefit without any prior pain. Therefore, inflicting pain would be foolish (safah) by analogy with human standards.
This is an argument from the shahid (witnessed realm).
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how this rebuttal completely undermines the Mu'tazilite analogy. The very example they gave actually proves their error.

Why God's Favor Does Not Diminish Blessing
The author refutes al-Ka'bi's claim that favor diminishes blessing, using Quran and reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's favor (manna) does not diminish the blessings He bestows, and how the author refutes al-Ka'bi's claim that compensation is better than free gift.

Core Idea
God's favor is not like human favor; it enhances blessings, not diminishes them.

This passage addresses a subtle theological objection raised by al-Ka'bi, who argued that gifts given as reward are better than those given freely because they carry no favor (manna) that diminishes the blessing. The author responds by examining the nature of divine favor and showing that al-Ka'bi's reasoning is based on a flawed analogy between human and divine relationships. As you read, notice how the author uses a Quranic verse to turn the argument on its head.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Ka'bi's argument
Start by carefully reading al-Ka'bi's claim that giving by reward is more beneficial than giving freely because favor reduces the blessing. Understand his reasoning about favor (manna) and compensation.
Al-Ka'bi is focusing on the psychological effect of favor in human transactions.
2
Observe the author's first counter
The author responds by calling this speech unbecoming of one who knows God. He reminds us that all blessings are from God and His favor is the greatest cause of joy.
Notice the bold rhetorical move: rather than arguing details, he reorients the discussion to the nature of God.
3
Examine the Quranic proof
The author quotes the verse 'But Allah bestows favor upon you that He has guided you to faith.' If favor diminished blessings, then guidance itself would be bitter—yet it is the greatest blessing.
This is a powerful reductio ad absurdum from the Qur'an itself.
4
Understand the analogy limitation
The author explains that favor makes blessings distasteful in human experience only because giver and receiver are equal in essence but differ in rank. This does not apply between God and humans, as God is infinitely superior.
Thus the analogy breaks down, and al-Ka'bi's argument collapses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرد على الكعبي القائل: إن النعمة تنتقص بالمنة. فاعترض الكعبي على هذا وزعم أن إعطاء ذلك بطريق الثواب أنفع للصبي من إعطائه ابتداء بالتفضل لما في الإعطاء ابتداء من لحوق المنة وفي الوصول بطريق العوض من ارتفاعها لما لا منة فيما نال بطريق الأعواض والمنة تنقص النعمة فيكون ما نيل بطريق العوض أهنأ وألذ. قيل: هذا كلام من عرف الله تعالى لا يستجيز من نفسه أن يخطر بباله فضلا عن التكلم والاحتجاج بمثله عند ملاقاة الخصوم وذلك لأن النعم كلها من الله تعالى وله علينا المنة العظمى ونتلذذ بالنعم بامتنانه ومن كانت منته عليه أوفر كان هو بذلك أسر وقلبه أفرح وعيشه ألذ فلو كانت منته تهدم الصنيعة وتنقص النعمة ينبغي أن تكون نعمة الهداية منغصة مكدرة على المؤمنين حيث قال تعالى: { بل الله يمن عليكم أن هداكم للإيمان } ثم لو عرف في هذا التائه في دينه المتحير في أمره علة تنغص النعم بالمن في الشاهد لما تكلم بهذا الكلام الشنيع مسمعه الفظيع موقعه وذلك أن علة تنغص النعمة في الشاهد بلحوق المنة استواء المنعم والمنعم عليه في الجوهر وتفاوتهما في الحال والرتبة ثم فوز أحدهما بالسعة في المال والرفعة والجلال في الشاهد والحال وحرمان الآخر عن كل ذلك ثم إن الفائز بالنعم
Refutation of al-Ka'bi who says: The blessing is diminished by the favor. So al-Ka'bi objected to this and claimed that giving that by way of reward is more beneficial for the child than giving it initially by gracious favor, because in giving initially there is the attachment of favor (manna) and in reaching by way of compensation there is its elevation, since there is no favor in what is obtained by way of compensations, and favor diminishes the blessing, so what is obtained by way of compensation is more wholesome and pleasant. It was said: This is speech from one who knows God Almighty would not permit himself to think of it, let alone speak and argue with it when confronting opponents. Because all blessings are from God Almighty and He has the greatest favor upon us, and we delight in blessings by His bestowal of favor. And whoever has more favor upon him is thereby more joyful, his heart more delighted, and his life more pleasant. So if His favor were to demolish the benefaction and diminish the blessing, then the blessing of guidance should be bitter and distasteful for the believers, where He said: 'But Allah bestows favor upon you that He has guided you to faith.' Then if this one, bewildered in his religion, perplexed in his affair, had known the cause that makes blessings distasteful by favor in the witnessed realm, he would not have spoken this hideous statement, with its terrible meaning and its awful implication. And that is because the cause of the distastefulness of blessing in the witnessed realm by the attachment of favor is the equality of the benefactor and the beneficiary in essence and their difference in state and rank. Then one of them obtains abundance in wealth and high status and majesty in the witnessed realm and state, while the other is deprived of all that. Then the one who attains blessings...
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the argument put forward by al-Ka'bi. Al-Ka'bi claimed that giving a blessing to a child as compensation (thawāb) is better than giving it as a free gift (tafaḍḍul) because a free gift involves favor (manna) which diminishes the blessing, whereas compensation avoids favor and thus is more wholesome. The author responds that this notion is incompatible with knowledge of God. All blessings come from God, and His favor upon us is the greatest source of joy and delight. If favor diminished blessings, then even the blessing of guidance (hidāya) would be bitter, contradicting the Quranic verse in which God says He bestows favor by guiding to faith. The author then explains the cause of distastefulness in the empirical world (shahid): favor becomes unpleasant only when the giver and receiver are equal in essence but differ in rank, leading to resentment. However, God is infinitely superior, so His favor is pure benevolence and enhances blessings. The argument ends mid-sentence, likely continuing to elaborate.

نعمة
ثواب
تفضل
شاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's favor (manna) is like human favor which can be seen as condescending or diminishing.
Correction: The author clarifies that God's favor is beloved because He is infinitely superior and His favor is a blessing in itself, not a source of diminishment.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation of al-Ka'bi, why does God's favor (manna) not diminish the blessing?
A) Because God's favor is not real.
B) Because the giver and receiver are not equal in essence.
C) Because compensation is better than free gift.
D) Because the child has no right to compensation.

Key Takeaway
God's favor is a blessing that enhances His gifts, and the analogy with human favor fails because God and humans are not equal in essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author says that whoever has more favor upon him is more joyful. Reflect: Is your heart truly joyful for God's continuous favors, or have you grown accustomed to them? Write down one specific favor you've overlooked and thank God for it today.
?
When someone does you a favor, does it ever feel like a burden? The lesson distinguishes human from divine favor. How can you start seeing all favors—from God and from people—as blessings that should increase your gratitude, not your pride? Commit to one action that reflects this change.

Why Paradise Would Be Spoiled If Reward Were Obligatory
Nasafi argues that Mu'tazilite view on reward would taint Paradise and shows impossibility of thanking God fully.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that if God were obliged to reward human obedience, Paradise would lose its perfection as a pure gift, and why human worship cannot obligate God.

Core Idea
If reward were owed, Paradise would not be pure grace — and we can never fully thank God.

Nasafi presents a powerful reductio ad absurdum against the Mu'tazilite claim that human obedience obligates God to reward. He shows that such a view would taint the very nature of Paradise and that the logic of thankfulness leads to an infinite regress, proving that all reward is by divine grace alone. This passage lays out the Maturidi position that God's reward is purely a favor, not a right.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow: first the tainting of Paradise, then the impossibility of thanking God fully.
The argument builds step by step.
2
Understand the core argument: why Paradise would be tainted
Identify the conditional statement: if the Mu'tazilite view were true, Paradise would be 'tainted' because it would be received as a right, not a gift. Ask: Why would that spoil its perfection?
3
Explore the infinite regress of thankfulness
Follow Nasafi's reasoning: worship is thanks for prior blessings, but thanking requires a new blessing (tawfiq), leading to an infinite chain. So we can never fully thank God for even one blessing.
This shows human inability to obligate God.
4
Connect to the Quranic verse
Read the verse Nasafi quotes (Ibrahim 14:34) and see how it supports his argument: God's blessings are countless, so we cannot even enumerate them, let alone thank for them fully.
The Qur'an itself confirms the point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لو كان الأمر على ما زعم لكانت الجنة العليا وما فيها من النعم منغصة على أولياء الله وأهل كرامته، لأن نيلهم ذلك كله كان بفضل من الله ونعمة منه، إذ لا أحد يستوجب بشيء من عباداته وطاعاته على الله تعالى شيئًا وإن زجي عمره في طاعته ولم يعصه طرفة عين، وذلك لأن العبد ما يأتيه من الطاعة يأتيه شكرًا لما أنعم عليه، ومن أدى إلى غيره حقا مستحقاً لا يستوجب بإزاء ما أدى شيئًا من العوض، على أن العبد لا يمكنه الخروج في جميع عمره وإن طال عن شكر نعمة واحدة وإن قلت لما أنه لا يؤدي شكره إلا بتوفيق من ربه وما وصل إليه من أثر التوفيق نعمة مستأنفة يقتضى عليها شكرًا مستأنفا ثم لا يؤدي شكر هذا التوفيق إلا بتوفيق. هكذا إلى ما لا يتناهى، وإذا كان لا يمكنه الخروج عن شكر نعمة واحدة فكيف عن شكر ما عليه من النعم الوافية الوافرة والمنن المتوالية المتظاهرة التي لا يمكنه إحصاؤها وعدها ويتعذر عليه حصرها وحدها على ما نطق به الكتاب بقوله تعالى : ( وَإِن تَعُدُّوا نِعْمَةَ اللهِ لَا تُحْصُوهَا ﴾
Then, if the matter were as they claim, the highest Paradise and its blessings would be tainted for the friends of Allah and the people of His honor, because their attainment of all that is by Allah's grace and favor, since no one deserves anything from Allah by virtue of his acts of worship and obedience, even if he spends his entire life in obedience and does not disobey Him for the blink of an eye. That is because the obedience that the servant performs comes as thanks for the blessings bestowed upon him, and whoever renders a deserved right to another does not deserve any compensation in return for what he rendered. Moreover, the servant cannot throughout his entire life, however long, escape from thanking even one single blessing, however small, because he does not perform its thanks except by the success (tawfīq) of his Lord, and what reaches him through the effect of success is a new blessing requiring new thanks, and then he does not perform the thanks of this success except by another success, and so on ad infinitum. If he cannot escape from thanking a single blessing, how can he escape from thanking the abundant and ample blessings and the successive and manifest favors that he cannot count or enumerate, and whose enumeration and limitation are beyond his capacity, as the Book declares in His saying: 'And if you count the favors of Allah, you cannot enumerate them' (Ibrahim 14:34).
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage presents a reductio ad absurdum against the Mu'tazilite position that a human's obedience obligates God to reward. Nasafi argues that if reward were obligatory and deserved, then the very act of receiving it would be tainted because it would not be pure grace. The passage begins by stating that if the Mu'tazilite claim were true, the highest Paradise and all its blessings would be spoiled (منغصة) for the saints, because they would receive it as an obligation rather than as unmerited favor. Nasafi asserts that no one deserves anything from God through worship, even if he lives in perfect obedience, because worship is itself a form of thankfulness for prior blessings. Thanking God for one blessing requires another blessing (the ability to thank), leading to an infinite regress. Therefore, a person can never fully thank God for even one blessing, let alone all blessings. He concludes by quoting the Quranic verse that counting God's blessings is impossible. The argument establishes that all reward is by divine grace, not obligation.

نعمة

توفيق
استيجاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human obedience makes God obligated to reward.
Correction: All reward is by God's grace; worship is itself a gift and can never fully thank God.

Checkpoint

B) Because they would receive Paradise as a deserved right rather than as pure grace.
C) Because they would have to constantly thank God.
D) Because God would be unjust.

Key Takeaway
Paradise is a pure gift; human worship never obligates God, and thankfulness requires infinite grace.

?
Imagine if Paradise were a ‘right’ instead of a gift — the text says it would be tainted. Do you treat your hope for Paradise as a deserved wage or as a desperate hope for mercy? Which view actually drives you to be more grateful and humble?

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Chain of Obligations
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that all blessings are obligatory on God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite claim that all blessings are obligatory upon God leads to an absurd chain of obligations and how Nasafi refutes it by pointing out that fulfilling a right does not create a new obligation.

Core Idea
Fulfilling an obligation discharges a debt, it does not create a new one.

The Mu'tazila argued that all of God's blessings are obligatory (wājib), creating a cascade of duties from God to humans and back. Nasafi exposes the logical flaw: fulfilling a right does not generate a new right for the recipient. In this lesson, we will follow his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite chain
Read the passage to identify the three-step chain: God must bless, human must thank, God must reward. Each step is claimed to be obligatory, with injustice if omitted.
2
Identify Nasafi's objection
Nasafi calls this 'beyond reason'. Focus on his central argument: fulfilling a right does not create a right for the one who fulfilled it.
3
Apply the witnessed analogy
Nasafi uses the example of human transactions: paying a debt frees the debtor, but does not make the creditor owe something. Recognize why the analogy between human and divine justice fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمعتزلة في هذا تدبير عجيب فإنهم يزعمون أن جميع ما فعل الله تعالى بالعبد وأسدى إليه من النعم كل ذلك حق على الله واجب وجوبًا لو امتنع عن قضائه إلى مستحقه لصار ظالماً جائراً، ثم إذا أوفى هذا الحق إلى مستحقه استوجب عليه شكرًا لو امتنع العبد عن أدائه صار ظالمًاً جائراً سفيها مانعا حقا مستحقا ثم إذا أدى ذلك استوجب على الله تعالى بذلك ثوابًا على وجه لو لم يعرفه الله تعالى لكان جائرا ظالمًا. وهذا خروج عن المعقول، وكيف يستوجب من قضى ما عليه من الحق إلى مستحقه شيئًا عليه هذا لعمري في الشاهد - الذي يجعلونه مفزعا يلجأون إليه في محالاً ممتنعا ساقطا كل مسألة من غير المساواة بينه ويبن الغائب في المعنى مندفعا لما أن قضاء الحق يوجب فراغ ذمة من عليه لا إيجاب حق على من له الحق.
The Mu'tazila have a strange method in this: they claim that all that Allah does to the servant and bestows upon him of blessings – all of it is a right upon Allah (ḥaqq) that is obligatory (wājib), such that if He refrained from fulfilling it to its rightful recipient, He would become an oppressor (ẓālim) and an unjust one (jā'ir). Then if He fulfills this right to its claimant, it necessitates thanksgiving (shukr) upon the servant; if the servant refrains from performing it, he becomes an oppressor, an unjust one, a fool (safīh), withholding a deserved right. Then if he performs that thanksgiving, it necessitates a reward (thawāb) from Allah in such a way that if Allah did not give that reward, He would be an oppressor and unjust. This is beyond reason. How can one who has fulfilled a right due from him to its claimant deserve something upon him? This, in the witnessed world (al-shāhid) – which they take as their recourse and refuge in every issue – is impossible and invalid, without equality between the witnessed and the absent in meaning being established, because fulfilling a right discharges the liability of the one who owes it, but does not create a right upon the one to whom it is due.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite doctrine that all of God's blessings are obligatory (wājib) upon Him. Nasafi begins by presenting their view in a stark chain: God must bestow blessings, otherwise He is unjust; if He bestows, the servant must thank, otherwise the servant is unjust; if the servant thanks, then God must reward, otherwise God is unjust again. Nasafi calls this 'beyond reason' and focuses on the logical flaw: fulfilling an obligation does not create a new obligation for the recipient. He uses the example of human transactions: paying a debt discharges the debtor's liability, but does not make the creditor owe the debtor something new. The Mu'tazila rely on analogy between the witnessed (human justice) and the absent (divine justice), but this analogy fails because the natures of rights are different. The key point is that fulfilling a right only frees the obligor from liability, it does not impose a new obligation on the obligee.

Key Terms
حق
وجوب
ظالم
جائر
شكر
ثواب
شاهد
غائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God is just, He must reward every good deed.
Correction: Nasafi argues that fulfilling a right (such as rewarding) does not create a new obligation; reward for thanks is not a necessary obligation on God, as the Mu'tazila claim.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the flaw in the Mu'tazilite chain of obligations?
A) The chain is too short.
B) Fulfilling an obligation does not create a new obligation for the recipient.
C) God is above obligations entirely.
D) The servant's thanks are never sufficient.

Key Takeaway
Fulfilling an obligation does not create a new obligation for the recipient; this refutes the Mu'tazilite chain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself expecting God to repay you for your acts of worship? How does Nasafi’s refutation of the Mu’tazilite chain challenge that expectation? What would it mean to serve God without any sense of entitlement?

Are there areas of your life where you treat God’s gifts as deserved rights rather than unearned grace? Confront that today: is your relationship with God based on obligation or on humble reception? What would change if you truly believed that every blessing is a pure gift?

The Reductio Against Mu'tazilite Compensation
Nasafi refutes Mu'tazilite claim that causing pain necessitates compensation, using reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite claim that God must compensate for pain leads to an absurd conclusion, and how Nasafi uses observed reality to refute it.

Core Idea
If pain guarantees reward, constant pain would be best—but it's not.

The Mu'tazilites held that God is obligated to do the most beneficial (aṣlaḥ) for His servants, and thus must compensate them for any pain they suffer. Nasafi shows this is self-defeating: if causing pain were always beneficial, then God would have to cause pain constantly. Since we see children are not in constant agony, the premise is false. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the source passage in Arabic and its English translation. Notice the logical structure: a conditional statement that leads to an absurdity.
The Arabic ends mid-sentence, but the argument is complete for our purposes.
2
Identify the two premises of the argument
The reductio relies on two premises: (1) causing pain (īlām) is a cause for obtaining compensation, and (2) there is no harm in it for the minor. Identify these in the text.
3
Trace the reductio: what does it imply for children?
If the premises are true, then it would be most beneficial (aṣlaḥ) for every child to be in constant pain to gain great reward. Nasafi states this consequence.
This is the key logical move.
4
See how observation disproves it
Nasafi points out that we do not observe children in constant pain; they are generally healthy. Since the consequence is false, the premises must be false.
This is an empirical refutation.
5
Understand the extension to adults
Nasafi extends the argument to adults: if an adult is harmed without sin, they would also deserve compensation, making God an agent of the servant's interest—another absurdity.
This reinforces the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : لو كان الإيلام سببًا لنيل العوض ولا مفسدة فيه للصغير لما أنه لا يعقل ليصير عاصيًا بالجزع وترك الصبر لكان الأصلح لكل صغير أن لا يخليه الله تعالى في ساعة من الساعات عن أنواع الآلام والأوجاع لينال بذلك الأعواض الخطيرة والأبدال العظيمة النفيسة في دار الآخرة، وحيث رأينا أن ذلك غير ثابت، ونرى الأطفال بضد هذه الحالة علمنا أن ما قالوا من استيجاب العوض أو من وجوب الأصلح باطل. ثم من زعمهم أنه لو فعل ذلك ببالغ لم توجد منه جناية يصير ذلك عقوبة عليها استوجب هذا البالغ أيضا العوض. ومن مذهبهم أن هذا هو الأصلح لهذا البالغ، وإذا كان كذلك وكان الله بذلك فاعلاً لمصلحة العبد فصار عاملاً له فكيف
Then we say: If causing pain (īlām) were a cause for obtaining compensation ('iwaḍ), and there were no harm (mafsada) in it for the minor – because it is not conceivable that he would become a sinner through impatience and abandoning patience – then the most beneficial (aṣlaḥ) for every minor would be that Allah not leave him for an hour without kinds of pains and aches, so that he might obtain grave compensations and precious replacements in the Abode of the Hereafter. And since we see that this is not established, and we see children in a contrary condition, we know that what they said about the necessity of compensation or the obligation of the most beneficial is false. Then, among their claims, if this were done to an adult who had not committed an offense, it would become a punishment upon him, and this adult would also deserve compensation. And according to their school, this is the most beneficial for this adult. If this is so, and Allah does this for the benefit of the servant, then He becomes an agent for him – how is that?
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazilite doctrine that God is obligated to compensate (عوض) children for pains they suffer, and that He must always do the most beneficial (أصلح) for His servants. Nasafi argues by reductio: if causing pain to a child were a guaranteed pathway to great reward in the afterlife, and if there were no negative consequences (since the child cannot sin by impatience), then it would be most beneficial for God to cause pain to children constantly. But observation shows the opposite – children are generally healthy and not in constant pain. Therefore, the Mu'tazilite premises are false. He then extends the argument to adults: if an adult receives pain without having committed any sin requiring punishment, then according to them that adult also deserves compensation, and this would be 'most beneficial' for him. This leads to the absurdity that God would cause harm for the servant's benefit, making God a mere agent of the servant's interest. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is clear.

Key Terms
إيلام
عوض
أصلح
طفل
بالغ
مصلحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God must compensate all suffering, and that this is compatible with His wisdom.
Correction: This passage shows that if God were obligated to compensate for every pain, He would have to cause constant pain to maximize reward, which contradicts observed reality and divine wisdom.

Checkpoint
What is the reductio ad absurdum Nasafi uses against the Mu'tazilite view on compensation for pain?
A) If pain brings reward, God would inflict constant pain on everyone.
B) If pain brings reward, no one would want to be healthy.
C) If pain brings reward, children would be sinning by not enduring it.
D) If pain brings reward, adults would demand more pain.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite view by showing that if pain always required compensation, constant pain would be most beneficial, but we don't see that.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Will you today surrender a specific situation where you've been demanding your own 'aṣlaḥ'?

Are you willing to let that evidence reshape your faith, even if it means letting go of a comfortable but false notion?

Refuting the Argument on Compensation for an Adult
Al-Nasafi refutes a specific argument about compensation in the context of al-Aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's refutation of the Mu'tazila argument that compensation is due for an adult in the doctrine of al-Aslah.

Core Idea
If God acts for our benefit, He becomes an agent—so who compensates whom?

Al-Nasafi continues his critique of the Mu'tazila doctrine of al-Aslah (the best). Here he targets a specific argument: that if God does something to an adult that causes no sin, that act would be a punishment deserving compensation. He exposes a logical contradiction in their reasoning by comparing God's role to that of an agent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recall that the Mu'tazila argue God must do what is best for His servants. Al-Nasafi has already called that obligation false. Now he addresses a specific sub-argument.
This builds on the previous discussion.
2
Analyze the hypothetical
Read the passage and identify the scenario: God does something to an adult that results in no sin. What do the Mu'tazila claim about this?
They claim it is a punishment and compensation is due.
3
Trace the contradiction
Notice how al-Nasafi turns their own reasoning against them. If God's act is for the servant's benefit, then God is acting as an agent. How can an agent be required to pay compensation?
The absurdity lies in the reversal of roles.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider how this refutation undermines the entire Mu'tazila theory of al-Aslah. If compensation is not due, then there is no obligation on God to do the best.
This is a rhetorical victory based on logical consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوب الأصلح باطل. ثم من زعمهم أنه لو فعل ذلك ببالغ لم توجد منه جناية يصير ذلك عقوبة عليها استوجب هذا البالغ أيضا العوض. ومن مذهبهم أن هذا هو الأصلح لهذا البالغ، وإذا كان كذلك وكان الله بذلك فاعلاً لمصلحة العبد فصار عاملاً له فكيف يلزمه العوض دون أن يستوجب به العوض على عرف العقول وصنيع الأطباء من الشاهد، والله الموفق.
The obligation of the best is false. Then among their claims is that if He did that to an adult and no sin occurred from him, that would become a punishment upon it, so this adult would also deserve compensation. And according to their doctrine, this is the best for this adult. And if that is the case, and God by that is acting for the benefit of the servant, then He becomes an agent for him. So how can compensation be incumbent upon Him without Him deserving compensation by it according to the custom of intellects and the practice of physicians from witnessed evidence? And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific argument related to the doctrine of al-Aslah (the best). Al-Nasafi first states that the obligation of al-Aslah is false. Then he presents a hypothetical: if God were to do something to an adult that causes no sin, that act would be considered a punishment, and thus the adult would deserve compensation. The Mu'tazila, who uphold al-Aslah, claim that this act is actually the best for the adult. But if God is acting for the servant's benefit, He becomes like an agent for him. Al-Nasafi then asks: how can God be obligated to pay compensation when He does not deserve it by the standards of rational custom and medical practice? This reveals a contradiction in their position.

Key Terms
وجوب الأصلح
العوض
مصلحة العبد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is that God is unjust for not compensating the adult.
Correction: Al-Nasafi is not arguing about God's justice; he is exposing a logical inconsistency in the Mu'tazila position by showing that their own reasoning leads to absurd consequences.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what is the consequence of the Mu'tazila claim regarding God's action for an adult's benefit?
A) God becomes an agent and must pay compensation
B) God is free from any obligation
C) The adult deserves punishment
D) The act is not for the adult's benefit

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila claim of compensation by showing that if God acts for the servant's benefit, He becomes an agent, and compensation would be owed to Him, not from Him.

?

?

What steps will you take to guard your heart from imagining God in human terms, especially when you feel He hasn't given you what you think is 'best'?
?
Al-Nasafi uses the example of physicians: they deserve compensation for their work. If God owes no one anything, then every good you have is pure grace. When was the last time you truly felt that your health, your faith, your very existence was unearned? How can you make that gratitude a daily reality, not just a concept?

The Necessary Consequence of Mu'tazila on God's Attributes
Al-Nasafi draws the necessary conclusion from the Mu'tazila doctrine of al-Aslah regarding God's will and generosity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Nasafi argues that the Mu'tazila doctrine of al-Aslah necessarily leads to denying God's will, generosity, and right to worship.

Core Idea
If God must do what is best, He cannot be generous – only just.

Al-Nasafi now draws out the inevitable implications of the Mu'tazila position on al-Aslah. If God is compelled to do what is best for His creatures, then His actions are not free acts of generosity but obligatory duties. This passage shows how such a view strips God of key attributes and even contradicts the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the strong language: 'no will', 'compelled', 'not generous'.
This is a reductio ad absurdum, not a direct statement of Mu'tazila belief.
2
Identify the core claim
The passage states that according to the Mu'tazila, God has no will or choice in His actions. Find the phrase that expresses this.
Look at the beginning: 'that God has no will...'.
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow how Al-Nasafi moves from the premise to the conclusion: because every action fulfills a right, God cannot be generous and deserves no thanks.
4
Reflect on the reductio
Understand that Al-Nasafi is not endorsing this view but showing its absurd consequences. Ask yourself: why would this contradict the Quranic verse mentioned?
The verse 'Allah is the possessor of great bounty' would be false if God only gives what is owed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نتيجة لازمة لقول المعتزلة: ثم حاصل هذه المسألة عندهم أن الله تعالى لا مشيئة له في فعله ولا اختياره بل هو مجبور على أن يفعل ما هو أصلح لعباده، وليس له أن يفعل سوى ما فعل، وليس بمحسن ولا منفصل ولا مفضل ولا جواد، لأنه لم يفعل فعلاً إلا قضى به حقًا مستحقا عليه على وجه يصير بمنعه ظالمًا، ومن هذا وصف فعله لا يوصف بالإفضال والإحسان وأنه كاذب بقوله وَاللَّهُ ذُو الْفَضْلِ الْعَظِيمِ، وليس بمستوجب على أحد شكرًا ولا عبادة، إذ القاضي حق الغير لا يستوجب ذلك، وليس =
A necessary consequence of the statement of the Mu'tazila: Then the conclusion of this matter according to them is that God – exalted is He – has no will in His action and no choice, but rather He is compelled to do what is best for His servants, and He cannot do other than what He has done. He is not a doer of good, nor a bestower of favors, nor a giver of bounty, nor generous, because He never performs an action except that He fulfills a right due upon Him in such a way that if He prevented it, He would become an oppressor. And with this description of His action, He cannot be described as bestowing favors and doing good, and He would be a liar in His statement 'And Allah is the possessor of great bounty' (2:105). And He does not deserve thanks or worship from anyone, because the one who fulfills the right of another does not deserve that. And not =
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage is a summary of the necessary conclusion that follows from the Mu'tazila doctrine of al-Aslah. Al-Nasafi states that according to them, God has no will or choice in His actions but is compelled (mujbar) to do what is best for His servants. He cannot do otherwise. Consequently, God cannot be described as a doer of good (muhsin), a bestower (munfasil – possibly a corruption), a giver of bounty (mufaddil), or generous (jawad). The reason is that every action God does is fulfilling a right due upon Him; if He withheld it, He would be unjust. Therefore, His actions cannot be considered acts of grace or favor. Moreover, this would contradict the Quranic verse 'Allah is the possessor of great bounty'. Additionally, since God only does what is obligatory, He does not deserve thanks or worship from anyone, just as a person who repays a debt is not thanked. The passage ends abruptly with 'and not', likely continuing the argument.

Key Terms
المشيئة
الاختيار
المجبور
الفضل
الإحسان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the passage is an accurate representation of Mu'tazila belief.
Correction: Al-Nasafi is presenting a reductio ad absurdum to show the unacceptable implications of the Mu'tazila position, not stating their actual doctrine.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Nasafi's argument, why does the Mu'tazila position lead to the conclusion that God does not deserve thanks?
A) Because God's actions are all gracious gifts
B) Because God is compelled to act and thus only fulfills a right, meriting no gratitude
C) Because God's will is free and generous
D) Because the Quran denies God's bounty

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi demonstrates that the Mu'tazila doctrine of al-Aslah, if followed consistently, strips God of His attributes of generosity and free will, and even contradicts the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you genuinely believed God was compelled to give you everything good, would your gratitude be real? How does this lesson expose any hidden entitlement you might feel towards God's blessings? Today, consciously thank God for one thing you normally take for granted as 'deserved'.
?
The Mu'tazila thought they were defending God's justice, but ended up stripping Him of generosity. In your own life, do you ever prioritize a virtue like fairness over grace? What would it look like to show unexpected generosity to someone today, mirroring God’s free bounty?
?
The passage says that if God only does His duty, He doesn't deserve thanks. How often do you treat your relationship with God as a transaction—'I pray, so He should give'? What would change in your prayer today if you approached it purely as a response to His unearned favor?

The Absurdity of Commanding the Impossible
Two reductio arguments against Mu'tazila obligation doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses reductio ad absurdum to refute the Mu'tazila's doctrine that God must do the best, specifically by showing that commanding to ask for what God cannot do leads to mockery and injustice.

Core Idea
If God cannot grant a request, commanding to ask is mockery.

The Mu'tazila argued that God is obligated to do what is best for all. The author here begins a series of logical arguments showing the absurd consequences of that view. Starting with a simple question: if God cannot do something, what does it mean to command His servants to ask for it? The passage we'll read shows that this leads to a contradiction in the very concept of divine command.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow.
2
Identify the two arguments
Find the first argument about commanding to ask, and the second about being praised for not doing. Notice how each begins with 'وأنه'.
3
Consider the logical structure
Ask yourself: what premise does the author assume from the Mu'tazila? How does he derive the absurdity?
This is a reductio ad absurdum, so the author is not endorsing the absurdity but showing it follows from the opponent's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ممن يرغب إليه في طلب مرغوب أو إزالة مرهوب، إذ ليس له أن يفعل ذلك فإنه حيث أمر عباده بالرغبة إليه في ذلك أمرهم بالسخرية والهزء أو أمر بأن يطلب منه أن يتسفه أو يجوز عليهم ويظلم ، أو أمرهم أن يطلبوا منه أن لا يظلمهم بمنعهم حقهم المستحق، وأنه تعالى أحب أن يحمد بما لم يفعل من الإحسان والإفضال، وأمر أن يشكروه على ما لم يفعل إذ ليس شيء من فعله بإفضال وأنه عاجز عما به صلاح خلقه وغير قادر على دفع الفساد عن أحد، وأن قدرته انتهت إذ لم يقدر على أصلح مما فعل بكل أحد وأنه أنعم على إبليس وفرعون وهامان وأبي جهل وغيرهم من الكفرة حسب ما أنعم على جميع الأنبياء والمرسلين والملائكة المقربين، وأنه ما اصطفى هؤلاء بشيء لم يصطف أولئك بمثله فكل كافر موفق معصوم منعم عليه مصطفى، وقد بينا في خلال كلامنا مجموع المعاني التي عصمنا الله تعالى عن قول هذا عقباه. ذكرناها هذه
From one who seeks from Him something desired or removal of something dreaded, for it is not for Him to do that. For when He commanded His servants to seek from Him in that, He commanded them to mockery and jest, or commanded that they ask Him to act foolishly or to oppress them and do injustice, or commanded them to ask Him not to oppress them by denying them their due right, and that He, exalted, loves to be praised for beneficence and favor He did not do, and commanded that they thank Him for what He did not do, since none of His action is by favor, and that He is incapable of what would bring about the well-being of His creation and unable to ward off corruption from anyone, and that His power ended since He was not able to do what is more beneficial than what He did for everyone, and that He bestowed favor upon Iblis, Pharaoh, Haman, Abu Jahl, and other disbelievers just as He bestowed favor upon all the prophets, messengers, and the angels brought near, and that He did not distinguish these over those with anything that He did not distinguish those with the like of it, so every disbeliever is successful, infallible, favored, and chosen. And we have explained in the course of our speech the totality of the meanings from which—may God, exalted, protect us from saying this consequence—we have mentioned them.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage presents a series of reductio ad absurdum arguments against the Mu'tazila's doctrine that God must do what is best (al-salah wa al-aslah) for all. The author begins with the premise that if God does not have the power to grant what is sought or remove what is feared, then commanding people to ask would be mockery. The argument then flows: if God is compelled to do the best, He would be commanding people to request things that are impossible or unjust, leading to absurdities. It continues that such a view implies God loves praise for favors not done, commands thanks for what He did not do, is incapable of ensuring real well-being, and that His power is limited. Furthermore, it would mean God equally favors prophets and the worst disbelievers, making all equally 'chosen'—an outrageous conclusion. Each point is a logical consequence the Mu'tazila must accept if they insist God is obligated to do the best. The author concludes that they have already covered these meanings in detail elsewhere, seeking protection from even uttering such ideas.

Key Terms
الإحسان
الإفضال
الرغبة
السخرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author claims that God actually commands mockery or injustice.
Correction: No, the author is showing that if the Mu'tazila's premise is accepted, it leads to that absurd conclusion; it is a reductio ad absurdum, not a direct claim.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is one absurd consequence of the Mu'tazila's doctrine that God must do the best?
A) That God commands mockery when He commands asking for what He cannot do
B) That God loves to be praised for what He has done
C) That God's power is unlimited
D) That prophets and disbelievers are not equal

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's view that God must do the best leads to the absurdity that commanding prayer becomes mockery.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray to Allah, do you ever assume He is obligated to answer your request? How does this lesson's reductio against the Mu'tazila shatter that assumption? Will you now view unanswered prayers as a sign of His mercy preserving you from being commanded mockery?
?
Think of a blessing you recently took for granted. In light of this passage, is it possible you treated it as something owed to you? What would it mean to truly see every good thing as a voluntary favor (إفضال) and not a right? Commit to one specific way you will show gratitude today for a favor you did not earn.
?
The Mu'tazila thought they were honoring God by making justice obligatory for Him. Have you ever tried to 'defend' God by imposing human standards on Him? Could your own theology contain hidden assumptions that, if followed through, actually diminish His majesty? What is one belief about God that you need to surrender to His absolute freedom?

Consequences of Obligating God: Incapability and Equal Favor
Three more reductio arguments: incapability, limited power, equal favor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three more absurd consequences of the Mu'tazila's doctrine: that it implies God is incapable of bringing good, that His power is limited, and that He equally favors prophets and disbelievers.

Core Idea
If God must do the best, He would be incapable of doing better for all.

The author continues his reductio arguments, now moving from the act of commanding to the very attributes of God. If God is obligated to do the best, then His power and favor become constrained in ways that contradict His perfection. The passage we'll read lists three further absurdities that follow from the Mu'tazila premise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to see the continuation of arguments.
2
List the three consequences
The author presents three outcomes: incapability to bring good, limited power, and equal favor to all. Note each.
The third consequence is the most striking: prophets and disbelievers become equal.
3
Reflect on the radical conclusion
The final sentence summarizes: if the Mu'tazila view holds, then every disbeliever is successful, infallible, favored, and chosen. This is clearly false, so the premise must be rejected.
The author also mentions having refuted this elsewhere.
