God's Sovereignty: Sunni vs Mu'tazila
Contrasts Sunni and Mu'tazilite views on God's actions towards creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fundamental contrast between Ahl al-Sunna and Mu'tazila regarding God's absolute sovereignty versus the Mu'tazilite demand for what is most beneficial.

Core Idea
God is not obligated to do what is most beneficial; He does as He wills.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi presents a sharp contrast between two theological schools. The Sunnis submit to God's absolute sovereignty, while the Mu'tazila impose human-like obligations on Him. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the Sunni doctrine of God's justice and power.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the strong language used for the Mu'tazila.
2
Identify the two groups and their core attitudes
The passage describes two groups: the first (Sunnis) and the second (Mu'tazila). Note how each relates to God's actions.
3
Analyze the Sunni response to calamities
Observe that Sunnis respond with patience and prayer, acknowledging God's ownership and sovereignty. They demand no compensation.
This reflects the Sunni view of God's absolute dominion.
4
Analyze the Mu'tazilite demands
The Mu'tazila claim God must do what is most beneficial, must reward obedience, and must compensate suffering. They treat these as rights.
This imposes obligations on God from a human perspective.
5
Reflect on the concluding statement
The author states that calling the Sunnis adversaries while exempting the Mu'tazila is obstinate stubbornness. Think about why.
The Mu'tazila are the ones who actually dispute God's actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المحن والمصائب قابلوا ذلك منه بالصبر وطلبوا منه إزالة ذلك بفضله وكرمه ولا يطالبون على ذلك منه عوضاً إقراراً منهم أن أنفسهم وأموالهم له يتصرف بذلك كله في ملكه يفعل ما يشاء ويحكم ما يريد لا يسأل عما يفعل وهم يسألون، فأما المعتزلة فهم الذين يخاصمونه ابتداء وانتهاء، فيقولون له ليس لك أن تفعل بنا إلا ما هو الأصلح لنا، وأن مصالحنا واجب عليك إعطاؤها وهي حق لنا مستحق قبلك إن لم تؤدها إلينا صرت ظالمًا جائراً ، سفيها مانعا حقا عليك ولا بقاء لك على ربوبيتك وسلطانك إلا بأداء ما هو حقنا قبلك، وإذا أطعناك بشيء مما أمرت وأدينا شكر بعض ما أوفيتنا من حقوقنا الواجبة عليك وجبت عليك مكافأتنا على ذلك ومقابلتنا بالإثابة على ما أدينا من حقك قبلنا لا يسعك إلا الخروج عن ذلك، وليس لك أن تؤلمنا من غير سابقة جناية إلا بشرط أن تعوضنا عن ذلك كما هو على كل من يتعرض ملك غيره ويتجاوز تصرفه عما هو مملوك له، ثم من عرف المذهبين وجعل الفريق الأول خصماء الله تعالى دون الفريق الثاني فهو مكابر معاند.
Afflictions and calamities: they (the first group) met that from Him with patience and asked Him to remove it by His bounty and generosity, and they do not demand from Him any compensation for it, acknowledging that their souls and wealth are His, He disposes all of that in His kingdom, He does what He wills and judges what He desires, He is not questioned about what He does but they are questioned. As for the Mu'tazila, they are the ones who dispute with Him from beginning to end, saying to Him: 'You cannot do with us except what is most beneficial for us, and our benefits are obligatory upon You to give, and they are a right due to us from You; if You do not deliver them to us, You become an oppressor, unjust, foolish, preventing a right due from You, and Your lordship and authority cannot endure except by delivering what is our right from You. And if we obey You in something of what You commanded and performed gratitude for some of what You gave us of our obligatory rights upon You, then You must reward us for that and recompense us with requital for what we performed of Your right from us; You have no choice but to fulfill that. And You may not cause us pain without a prior offense except on condition that You compensate us for it, as is for anyone who infringes upon another's property and exceeds his disposal over what is owned by another. Then, whoever knows the two schools and makes the first group adversaries of God without the second group is obstinately stubborn.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage contrasts the Sunni position with the Mu'tazilite position regarding God's actions toward His creation. The passage begins by describing the first group (Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a): when they face hardships and calamities, they respond with patience and pray for removal through God's grace, without demanding any compensation. This is because they acknowledge that their selves and properties are entirely God's possession; He does as He wills in His kingdom without anyone questioning Him, whereas He questions them. In contrast, the Mu'tazila are described as disputing with God from start to finish. They claim that God can only do what is most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ) for them, and that their well-being is an obligation upon Him. They assert that these benefits are a right (ḥaqq) due to them, and if God fails to grant them, He becomes unjust and tyrannical. They further claim that if they obey Him and show gratitude, He must reward them in return for fulfilling His right. They also argue that God cannot cause them pain without prior sin unless He compensates them, likening Him to someone who interferes with another's property. Finally, the author states that anyone who recognizes these two schools but still calls the first group (the Sunnis) adversaries of God while exempting the second (the Mu'tazila) is obstinately stubborn (mukābir).

Key Terms
الأصلح
حق
ربوبية
مكافأة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sunnis believe God acts arbitrarily without reason or justice.
Correction: The passage shows Sunnis acknowledge God's absolute sovereignty but do not claim He is unjust; rather, they accept His actions as His right. The Mu'tazila impose human-like obligations, which actually disputes God's authority.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila as described in this passage, what would happen if God did not give them what is most beneficial?
A) God would still be compassionate.
B) God would become unjust and tyrannical.
C) Nothing would change.
D) God would cease to exist.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni view upholds God's absolute sovereignty without imposing obligations, while the Mu'tazila demand that God act for human benefit and reward, effectively disputing His lordship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or loss, do you find yourself silently bargaining with God, expecting Him to repay or compensate you? According to this lesson, which school does that attitude actually belong to? Will you repent of it today?
?
Are you willing to acknowledge that everything you have—your health, wealth, even your loved ones—is God's property, and He may take it without owing you any explanation or compensation? How would that change your reaction to the next calamity?
?
Think of a time you felt God 'owed' you reward for your good deeds. Does that feeling reveal that the Mu'tazila mindset has crept into your heart? What concrete step will you take to submit to God's absolute sovereignty instead?

Refuting the Claim of Agreement with Magians
Refutes the accusation that Maturidiyya agrees with Magians on attributing marriage of daughters to God's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Maturidi position on marriage of daughters does not constitute agreement with the Magians, because the underlying reason (ʿilla) for attributing actions to God differs fundamentally.

Core Idea
Outward agreement with a different cause is not real agreement.

The Maturidi statement that marriage of daughters is by God's will has been misconstrued as concurrence with Zoroastrian doctrine. The author clarifies that while the Magians also attribute such acts to God, their justification is based on the belief that these acts are good, whereas the Maturidi reasoning is rooted in divine sovereignty. This lesson unpacks the distinction and refutes the accusation.

Guided Path
1
Read the accusation and response
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note how the author immediately dismisses the accusation as 'pure obfuscation' (tamwīh maḥḍ).
The author is defending the Maturidi position strongly.
2
Examine the Magian reasoning
The passage explains that the Magians attribute marriage to God's will based on their false assumption that it is good (ḥasan). If they knew it was evil, they would side with the Mu'tazila.
This shows their criterion is moral evaluation, not divine decree.
3
Understand the difference in cause (ʿilla)
The author argues that the Magians and Mu'tazila share the same cause (being good) for allowing or denying attribution to God, while Maturidis differ. Outward agreement in saying 'by God's will' is not real agreement because the underlying reason is different.
Real agreement requires agreement in both statement and reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصفنا بمساعدة المجوس تمويه محض. وما زعموا من مساعدتنا المجوس بقولنا: إن نكاح البنات بإرادة الله تعالى تمويه محض، فإنهم يضيفون ذلك إلى الله تعالى بناء على ظنهم الكاذب أن ذلك مشروع وهو حسن ولو علموا قبحه لساعدوا المعتزلة في نفي إرادة الله تعالى عن ذلك، ألا ترى أنك لو سألتهم عن علة إضافتهم ذلك إلى إرادة الله تعالى اعتلوا بكونه حسنًا لا بكونه محدثًا، فإذا هم مع المعتزلة في علة جواز الإضافة والمنع، وخالفونا أشد المخالفة كما خالفتنا المعتزلة، إلا أنهم ظنا منهم أن ذلك حسن ساعدونا في الظاهر والمساعدة في الظاهر مع الاختلاف في العلة لن يعد موافقة. والله الموفق.
Our being described as helping the Magians is pure obfuscation. And what they alleged about our helping the Magians by our saying 'Indeed, marriage of daughters is by the will of God' is pure obfuscation. For they (the Magians) attribute that to God based on their false assumption that it is permissible and good. Had they known its evil, they would have helped the Mu'tazila in negating the will of God from that. Do you not see that if you asked them about the cause of their attributing that to the will of God, they would give as cause its being good, not its being originated. So they are with the Mu'tazila in the cause of permissibility of attribution and prohibition, and they differ from us most severely, just as the Mu'tazila differed from us. Except that due to their assumption that it is good, they helped us in appearance, and help in appearance while differing in cause does not count as agreement. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage refutes the accusation that the Maturidis agree with the Magians (Zoroastrians) on the issue of marriage of daughters being attributed to God's will. The author begins by stating that the description of them as helping the Magians is pure obfuscation (tamwīh maḥḍ). The accusation stems from the Maturidi statement that 'marriage of daughters is by the will of God.' The author explains that the Magians themselves attribute such acts to God based on their false belief that these acts are permissible and good (ḥasan). If they knew they were evil (qabīḥ), they would side with the Mu'tazila in denying God's will in such matters. The author then presents an argument: if you ask the Magians why they attribute this to God's will, their reason (ʿilla) is that it is good, not because it is originated (muḥdath). Thus, in terms of the rationale for permissibility or prohibition of attribution, the Magians actually agree with the Mu'tazila, not with the Maturidis. Hence they differ from the Maturidis most severely, just as the Mu'tazila do. The only reason they appear to agree outwardly is their mistaken assumption of goodness. But such outward agreement with a different underlying cause does not constitute real agreement (muwāfaqa). The passage concludes with a supplication, 'And God is the Grantor of success.'

Key Terms
مجوس
إرادة الله
حسن
علة
تمويه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two groups outwardly say the same thing (e.g., 'marriage of daughters is by God's will'), they must be in doctrinal agreement.
Correction: The passage shows that the Magians' reason for attributing to God is that they consider the act good, while the Maturidis attribute it to God based on divine sovereignty. Outward agreement with a different underlying cause (ʿilla) does not count as true agreement.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the reason (ʿilla) the Magians give for attributing marriage of daughters to God's will?
A) That it is originated (muḥdath)
B) That it is good (ḥasan)
C) That it is commanded by God
D) That it is inevitable

Key Takeaway
Outward agreement in a theological statement does not constitute true agreement if the underlying reasoning (ʿilla) is fundamentally different.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever defended a belief or practice solely because it felt 'good' or 'natural' to you, rather than examining its true cause from revelation? How can you distinguish between cultural assumption and sound reasoning like the Magians failed to do?
?
The Magians' outward agreement with the Maturidis was based on a false premise. Is there any area in your life where you say the right words but with the wrong intention or reason? What would it take to align your inner cause with the truth?
?
If outward agreement with differing cause does not count as real agreement, how does that challenge your relationships with others who share your statements but not your foundational beliefs? Will you seek to understand their 'cause' or settle for superficial harmony?

The Possibility of Punishing Believers and the Argument from Divine Names for Forgiveness
Nasafi argues possibility of temporal punishment and uses divine names to prove forgiveness for believing sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why temporal punishment of believers is possible according to reason and revelation, and how the divine names of forgiveness support the view that believers who sin (without istihlal) can be forgiven.

Core Idea
Divine names like Ghafūr require that forgiveness applies to those who deserve punishment.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi's Tabṣirat al-Adilla tackles two interconnected issues: whether a believer can be temporarily punished for sins, and how the names of Allah (such as Ghafūr and 'Afuww) prove that forgiveness is for believers who sin without deeming it permissible. The author first argues that punishment of believers is not rationally impossible and is supported by scriptural reports. He then draws on the divine attributes to show that forgiveness must apply to those who are liable to punishment, refuting the Mu'tazila interpretation that limits forgiveness to minor sins only. Read carefully to see how the author weaves together rational possibility, scriptural evidence, and linguistic analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage twice
First read without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the flow: possibility of punishment, then evidence from istighfar, then divine names, then refutation of Mu'tazila.
The passage is a single continuous argument, so follow the author's sequence.
2
Identify the key claims
Find where the author asserts that punishing a believer is not impossible in reason, and where he says that divine names necessitate forgiveness for believers who sin.
Also note the consensus statement about disbelievers.
3
Understand the refutation of Mu'tazila
The author defines maghfira as overlooking someone who can be punished. Compare this with the Mu'tazila view that it applies only to minor sins for those avoiding major sins.
The key is that if a person cannot be punished, not punishing is not forgiveness.
4
Connect to the bigger picture
This passage defends the Ash'ari/Maturidi position that major sins do not expel a believer from faith, and that forgiveness is a real act of Allah for those who deserve it.
This relates to the debate on the status of grave sinners.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما ليس بمقرون بذكر الخلود فمن الجائز أن يكون فى المؤمن إذ تعذيب المؤمن بقدر ذنبه ليس بمستحيل في العقل ولا قام دليل شرعي على امتناعه، ووردت به أخبار كثيرة ثم ثبت استغفار الملائكة والأنبياء عليهم السلام للمؤمنين، وكذا المؤمنون أمروا بالاستغفار بعضهم لبعض والاستغفار لمن لا يجز تعذيبه سؤال ترك الظلم وهذا كفر، وكذا مغفرة من لم يستوجب العذاب محال وكذا العفو دل أن من العباد من يستوجب العقوبة ثم الله تعالى يغفر له بفضله ويعفو عنه. يحققه أنه تعالى غافر الذنب، وكذا يسمى غفورًا وغفارا، ورد بهذه الأسامي نصوص الكتاب، وكذا الله تعالى عفو وتسمية الله تعالى بما لا يتحقق منه ومدحه نفسه بما لا يتصور منه محال، ثم بالإجماع ليس ذلك في حق المكذبين ولا الخارجين عن الإسلام، فدل أن ذلك في حق من ارتكب المعصية من غير الاستحلال فكان في دليل أن العبد بالمعصية لا يكفر وبالكبيرة لا يخرج عن الإيمان ليتحقق عفوه والمغفرة له، ودليل جواز مغفرة ما دون الكفر إذ لولا ذلك لما تحقق له اسم الغافر ولا تصورت منه المغفرة والعفو. وحمل المعتزلة ذلك على مغفرة الصغائر لمن اجتنب الكبائر غير سديد، إذ المغفرة التجاوز عمن يجوز تعذيبه على ما ارتكب فأما ترك تعذيب من لا يجوز تعذيبه فليس بعفو ولا مغفرة كترك التعذيب على المباحات.
As for what is not accompanied by mention of eternity (of punishment), it is possible that it occurs for a believer, since punishing a believer according to his sin is not impossible in reason, nor has any scriptural evidence prevented it, and many reports have come concerning it. Then it is established that the angels and the prophets, peace be upon them, seek forgiveness for the believers, and likewise the believers are commanded to seek forgiveness for one another. Seeking forgiveness for one who cannot be punished is a request to abandon wrongdoing, and that is disbelief. Likewise, forgiveness for one who does not deserve punishment is impossible, and likewise pardon. It indicates that among the servants are those who deserve punishment, then Allah the Exalted forgives him by His bounty and pardons him. Its verification: He, the Exalted, is the Forgiver of sin, and He is called Ghafūr (Oft-Forgiving) and Ghaffār (Great Forgiver); the texts of the Book have come with these names. And likewise, Allah the Exalted is 'Afuww (Pardoner). To name Allah with what is not actualized from Him and to praise Himself with what is inconceivable from Him is impossible. Then, by consensus, that is not in the case of those who deny (the truth) nor those who leave Islam. So it indicates that that is in the case of one who commits sin without deeming (it) permissible. Thus it becomes evidence that a servant does not become a disbeliever by sin, and does not leave faith by a major sin, in order for His pardon and forgiveness to be actualized for him. And evidence of the permissibility of forgiveness for what is less than disbelief, since were it not for that, the name 'al-Ghāfir' (the Forgiver) would not be actualized for Him, nor would forgiveness and pardon be conceived from Him. And the Mu'tazila's interpretation of that as forgiveness of minor sins for one who avoids major sins is not sound, because forgiveness is overlooking one whom it is permissible to punish for what he committed. As for refraining from punishing one whom it is not permissible to punish, that is not pardon nor forgiveness, like refraining from punishment for permissible actions.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage is a combined argument and refutation. The author first establishes the possibility of temporal punishment for believers. He notes that many scriptural reports mention punishment for believers, and that seeking forgiveness for Muslims implies they may deserve punishment. He then argues from the divine names: Allah is called Ghāfir, Ghafūr, and 'Afuww. For these names to be meaningful, they must apply to actual forgiveness of those who deserve punishment. Since by consensus these names do not apply to disbelievers, they must apply to believers who sin without istihlal. This proves that a believer does not become a disbeliever by sin (major or minor), and that forgiveness for less than shirk is possible. The author then refutes the Mu'tazila interpretation that these verses only refer to forgiveness of minor sins when major sins are avoided. He points out that true forgiveness (maghfira) is overlooking someone who can be justly punished. If a person cannot be punished in the first place, refraining from punishment is not forgiveness, just as it is not forgiveness to refrain from punishing permissible acts. Therefore, the Mu'tazila limitation is incorrect.

مغفرة الصغائر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine names like Ghafūr mean Allah forgives everyone unconditionally, including disbelievers.
Correction: The names apply only to believers who sin without istihlal; by consensus they do not apply to disbelievers.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why do the Mu'tazila err in limiting forgiveness to minor sins for those who avoid major sins?
A) Because major sins are always forgiven regardless of repentance
B) Because forgiveness is defined as overlooking someone who can be punished, so limiting it to minor sins disregards the possibility of punishing major sins
C) Because the Qur'an explicitly says all sins are forgiven
D) Because the Mu'tazila deny the attribute of forgiveness

Key Takeaway
Allah's forgiveness is actual and applies to believers who deserve punishment for sins less than shirk, refuting the Mu'tazila limitation to minor sins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because Allah is 'Ghafūr', your sins are automatically forgiven without any consequence? Does this lesson change how you view the seriousness of your sins, even if you remain a believer?
?
When you sin, do you truly seek forgiveness with a heart that acknowledges you deserve punishment, or do you take it for granted? How can you deepen your istighfār to reflect that reality?
?
If you heard that a fellow believer committed a major sin, might you secretly think they are less of a Muslim? This lesson teaches that sin does not remove faith. How can you reconcile this with your own judgments of others?

Why Three Sects Cannot Truly Affirm Divine Forgiveness
Refutation of Khawarij, Mu'tazila, and Murji'a on forgiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Khawarij, Mu'tazila, and Murji'a positions logically prevent them from affirming that Allah is truly Ghafur and Rahim, and how the Maturidi view grounds forgiveness in God's will.

Core Idea
The sects' own premises make God's forgiveness inconsistent with their systems.

The Maturidi author begins by critiquing three major theological sects, showing that their doctrines about sin and punishment actually contradict the divine attributes of forgiveness. By exposing these inconsistencies, he clears the ground for the authentic Maturidi position: that forgiveness for lesser sins is entirely at God's discretion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three sects
Read the passage and list the three groups mentioned: Khawarij, Mu'tazila, and Murji'a.
2
Understand each sect's problem
For each sect, note why according to their principles forgiveness is inconceivable. The Khawarij and Mu'tazila allow punishment of sinners, making forgiveness impossible; the Murji'a restrict punishment to disbelievers, so no forgiveness is needed.
3
Find the Quranic basis
Locate the verse (4:116) that the author uses to establish the Maturidi alternative: Allah forgives what is less than shirk for whom He wills.
4
Draw the conclusion
Reflect on the final statement: no evidence specifies who is forgiven; it is delegated to God's will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتقرر بالوقوف على هذه المعاني أن لا تحقق لكونه تعالى غافرًا غفورا رحيمًا على أصل الخوارج والمعتزلة إذ لا يتصور عندهم مغفرة من يجوز له تعذيبه ولا على أصل المرجئة، فإن عندهم ما يجوز له إلا تعذيب الكافر والعفو عنه غير ثابت، ثم لما ثبت ذلك لم يقم لنا دليل تعيين من يغفر له ومن لا يغفر له بل يعذبه إذ ليس ذلك في العقل، وما ورد به الشرع بل فوض ذلك إلى مشيئته بقوله تعالى : إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَن يُشْرَكَ بِهِ وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَن يَشَاءُ . فقلنا له: المشيئة في ذلك فمن شاء عذبه بقدر ذنبه صغيرة كان ذلك أم كبيرة عدلاً منه ثم عاقبة أمره الجنة وإن شاء عفا عنه فضلاً منه، ورحمة وقوله تعالى : إِن تَجْتَنِبُوا كَبَابِرَ مَا تَنْهَوْنَ عَنْهُ نُكَفِّرْ عَنكُمْ سَيِّئَاتِكُمْ  الآية، قيل: إِن تَجْتَنِبُوا كَبَابِرَ مَا تَنْهَوْنَ عَنْهُ والمراد به الكفر، وقيل المراد من قوله : كَبَابِرَ مَا تَنْهَوْنَ عَنْهُ أنواع الكفر. يحققه: ثبوت جواز المغفرة لما دون الشرك بما قررنا من الدلائل، غيلان على الله تعالى أنه إذا غفر لمرتكب ذنبًا لا يجوز تعذيب غيره على ذلك أنه تعالى أثبت لنفسه المشيئة في ذلك، وعلق ذلك بما تحكم باطل مع الذنب مع أن
And it is established, by pausing upon these meanings, that there is no actualization for His being Ghāfir, Ghafūr, Raḥīm according to the principle of the Khawārij and Mu'tazila, since according to them forgiveness of one whom it is permissible to punish is not conceivable. Nor according to the principle of the Murji'a, for according to them it is not permissible to punish except the disbeliever, and pardon of him is not established. Then when that is established, we have no evidence specifying who will be forgiven and who will not be forgiven, but rather He punishes him, since that is not in reason, and what the Law has brought is that He delegated that to His will in His saying: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him, but He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' (4:116). So we say: The will is in that; so if He wills, He punishes him according to his sin, whether it be small or large, justly from Him, then his final end is Paradise; and if He wills, He pardons him out of bounty and mercy from Him. And His saying: 'If you avoid the great sins which you are forbidden, We will expiate from you your misdeeds' (4:31) – the verse. It was said: 'If you avoid the great sins which you are forbidden' and what is meant by it is disbelief; and it was said that what is meant by His saying 'the great sins' is the types of disbelief. He verifies: The establishment of the permissibility of forgiveness for what is less than association (shirk) by what we have established from the proofs. Ghīlān [said] against Allah the Exalted that if He forgives a perpetrator of a sin, it is not permissible to punish another for that, and that He the Exalted has established for Himself the will in that, and attached it to that which is ruled invalid with the sin, while [the text is incomplete]
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage is a conclusion and a refutation of three sects on the issue of forgiveness. The author first shows that the Khawarij and Mu'tazila cannot affirm the divine names Ghāfir, Ghafūr, and Raḥīm because they believe that anyone who commits a major sin is either an unbeliever (Khawarij) or in an intermediate state (Mu'tazila), making forgiveness of such a person impossible. The Murji'a, on the other hand, believe that only disbelievers can be punished, so forgiveness of a believer is not needed. The Maturidi position, based on the Qur'an (4:116), is that Allah forgives whatever is less than shirk for whom He wills. The author then explains the implications: punishment or forgiveness is entirely up to God's will; He may punish a believer for his sins (even small ones) justly, then ultimately admit him to Paradise, or He may pardon him out of grace. The verse about avoiding great sins (4:31) is interpreted by some as referring to shirk, and by others as referring to types of disbelief. The passage ends with an incomplete verification that the permissibility of forgiveness for less than shirk is established by proofs, and a mention of Ghilan (a Murji'i figure) who argued against the idea that forgiving one sin prevents punishing another—a point that is cut off.

خوارج
معتزلة
مرجئة
مغفرة
مشيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Khawarij and Mu'tazila can consistently affirm that Allah is Ghafur and Rahim.
Correction: The author argues that since they believe it is permissible to punish the sinner, forgiveness of that same person is inconceivable under their principles.

Checkpoint

A) The Khawarij
B) The Mu'tazila
C) The Murji'a
D) The Maturidiyya

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi position grounds forgiveness in God's will alone, bypassing the logical inconsistencies of the three sects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's forgiveness is entirely up to His will, or do you secretly think you can earn it by your own deeds? How does this passage challenge your reliance on your own actions?
?

?
If God's forgiveness is not tied to your own merit, how does that affect the way you approach repentance? Are you relying on your sincerity or on God's sheer mercy—and how does this lesson push you to rely solely on Him?

How Maturidis Understand God's Will in Forgiveness
Explanation of the Maturidi view on punishment, pardon, and further evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to the Maturidi school, whether a believer is punished or forgiven for sins less than shirk is entirely up to God's will, and that punishment is just and temporary, leading ultimately to Paradise.

Core Idea
God may punish or pardon, but the final destination for believers is Paradise.

After refuting the sects, the author now unfolds the positive Maturidi doctrine: forgiveness for less than shirk is possible but not guaranteed. God retains the discretion to punish justly or pardon graciously. The author supports this with a second Quranic verse and responds to a potential objection from Ghilan.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation to grasp the two possible outcomes: punishment or pardon.
Notice the emphasis on God's will (مشيئته).
2
Analyze the two outcomes
The author first states that if God wills, He punishes the sinner in proportion to his sin justly, then his end is Paradise; if He wills, He pardons out of bounty and mercy.
3
Examine the second Quranic verse
The author cites Quran 4:31 about avoiding great sins, with two interpretations: great sins mean disbelief or types of disbelief. This supports the idea that lesser sins may be expiated.
4
Note the objection of Ghilan
The passage ends with a mention of Ghilan's argument that forgiving one sinner would preclude punishing another, but the text is incomplete.
This shows the author engages with opposing views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتقرر بالوقوف على هذه المعاني أن لا تحقق لكونه تعالى غافرًا غفورا رحيمًا على أصل الخوارج والمعتزلة إذ لا يتصور عندهم مغفرة من يجوز له تعذيبه ولا على أصل المرجئة، فإن عندهم ما يجوز له إلا تعذيب الكافر والعفو عنه غير ثابت، ثم لما ثبت ذلك لم يقم لنا دليل تعيين من يغفر له ومن لا يغفر له بل يعذبه إذ ليس ذلك في العقل، وما ورد به الشرع بل فوض ذلك إلى مشيئته بقوله تعالى : إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَن يُشْرَكَ بِهِ وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَن يَشَاءُ . فقلنا له: المشيئة في ذلك فمن شاء عذبه بقدر ذنبه صغيرة كان ذلك أم كبيرة عدلاً منه ثم عاقبة أمره الجنة وإن شاء عفا عنه فضلاً منه، ورحمة وقوله تعالى : إِن تَجْتَنِبُوا كَبَابِرَ مَا تَنْهَوْنَ عَنْهُ نُكَفِّرْ عَنكُمْ سَيِّئَاتِكُمْ  الآية، قيل: إِن تَجْتَنِبُوا كَبَابِرَ مَا تَنْهَوْنَ عَنْهُ والمراد به الكفر، وقيل المراد من قوله : كَبَابِرَ مَا تَنْهَوْنَ عَنْهُ أنواع الكفر. يحققه: ثبوت جواز المغفرة لما دون الشرك بما قررنا من الدلائل، غيلان على الله تعالى أنه إذا غفر لمرتكب ذنبًا لا يجوز تعذيب غيره على ذلك أنه تعالى أثبت لنفسه المشيئة في ذلك، وعلق ذلك بما تحكم باطل مع الذنب مع أن
And it is established, by pausing upon these meanings, that there is no actualization for His being Ghāfir, Ghafūr, Raḥīm according to the principle of the Khawārij and Mu'tazila, since according to them forgiveness of one whom it is permissible to punish is not conceivable. Nor according to the principle of the Murji'a, for according to them it is not permissible to punish except the disbeliever, and pardon of him is not established. Then when that is established, we have no evidence specifying who will be forgiven and who will not be forgiven, but rather He punishes him, since that is not in reason, and what the Law has brought is that He delegated that to His will in His saying: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him, but He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' (4:116). So we say: The will is in that; so if He wills, He punishes him according to his sin, whether it be small or large, justly from Him, then his final end is Paradise; and if He wills, He pardons him out of bounty and mercy from Him. And His saying: 'If you avoid the great sins which you are forbidden, We will expiate from you your misdeeds' (4:31) – the verse. It was said: 'If you avoid the great sins which you are forbidden' and what is meant by it is disbelief; and it was said that what is meant by His saying 'the great sins' is the types of disbelief. He verifies: The establishment of the permissibility of forgiveness for what is less than association (shirk) by what we have established from the proofs. Ghīlān [said] against Allah the Exalted that if He forgives a perpetrator of a sin, it is not permissible to punish another for that, and that He the Exalted has established for Himself the will in that, and attached it to that which is ruled invalid with the sin, while [the text is incomplete]
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage is a conclusion and a refutation of three sects on the issue of forgiveness. The author first shows that the Khawarij and Mu'tazila cannot affirm the divine names Ghāfir, Ghafūr, and Raḥīm because they believe that anyone who commits a major sin is either an unbeliever (Khawarij) or in an intermediate state (Mu'tazila), making forgiveness of such a person impossible. The Murji'a, on the other hand, believe that only disbelievers can be punished, so forgiveness of a believer is not needed. The Maturidi position, based on the Qur'an (4:116), is that Allah forgives whatever is less than shirk for whom He wills. The author then explains the implications: punishment or forgiveness is entirely up to God's will; He may punish a believer for his sins (even small ones) justly, then ultimately admit him to Paradise, or He may pardon him out of grace. The verse about avoiding great sins (4:31) is interpreted by some as referring to shirk, and by others as referring to types of disbelief. The passage ends with an incomplete verification that the permissibility of forgiveness for less than shirk is established by proofs, and a mention of Ghilan (a Murji'i figure) who argued against the idea that forgiving one sin prevents punishing another—a point that is cut off.

Key Terms
مشيئة
عدل
فضل
رحمة
كبائر
غيلان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Maturidis guarantee forgiveness for all sins less than shirk.
Correction: The Maturidi position is that forgiveness is possible but dependent on God's will; He may punish justly before admitting to Paradise.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi view as presented, what is the final outcome for a believer who is punished for his sins?
A) He will never enter Paradise
B) He will enter Paradise after punishment
C) He will be forgiven automatically
D) He will become a disbeliever

Key Takeaway
God's will is the sole determinant of forgiveness for sins less than shirk, ensuring both justice and mercy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you secretly hope God will overlook it because 'it's just a small one'? Or do you fear His justice enough to truly repent? Which response is more real in your daily life?
?
If you knew that even the smallest sin could be punished justly, would you change anything about how you spend your day today? What would you do differently right now?
?
Do you ever assume that because God is merciful, He must forgive everyone—even those who don't repent? How does the Maturidi teaching that God's will is free challenge your assumptions and your own relationship with sin?

The True Meaning of Divine Forgiveness
Defines forgiveness and refutes Mu'tazila interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of divine forgiveness and why the Mu'tazila interpretation is unsound.

Core Idea
Forgiveness only applies to one who could justly be punished.

Imam Nasafi begins by clarifying the meaning of forgiveness. He argues that true forgiveness presupposes that the sinner could have been punished; otherwise, it is simply leaving aside punishment for permissible actions. This definition refutes the Mu'tazila claim that God only forgives minor sins for those who avoid major ones.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully.
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the author's precise wording.
The definition is concise but foundational.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim.
The Mu'tazila hold that forgiveness is for minor sins when one avoids major sins. See why the author rejects this.
The key is the condition of just punishment.
3
Reflect on the implication.
Without the possibility of just punishment, forgiveness loses its meaning. Consider the example of permissible acts.
This sets the stage for refuting other sects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
له اسم الغافر ولا تصورت منه المغفرة والعفو . وحمل المعتزلة ذلك على مغفرة الصغائر لمن اجتنب الكبائر غير سديد، إذ المغفرة التجاوز عمن يجوز تعذيبه على ما ارتكب فأما ترك تعذيب من لا يجوز تعذيبه فليس بعفو ولا مغفرة كترك التعذيب على المباحات. وتقرر بالوقوف على هذه المعاني أن لا تحقق لكونه تعالى غافرًا غفورا رحيمًا على أصل الخوارج والمعتزلة إذ لا يتصور عندهم مغفرة من يجوز له تعذيبه ولا على أصل المرجئة، فإن عندهم ما يجوز له إلا تعذيب الكافر والعفو عنه غير ثابت، ثم لما ثبت ذلك لم يقم لنا دليل تعيين من يغفر له ومن لا يغفر له بل يعذبه إذ ليس ذلك في العقل، وما ورد به الشرع بل فوض ذلك إلى مشيئته بقوله تعالى : إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَن يُشْرَكَ بِهِ وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَن يَشَاءُ . فقلنا له: المشيئة في ذلك فمن شاء عذبه بقدر ذنبه صغيرة كان ذلك أم كبيرة عدلاً منه ثم عاقبة أمره الجنة وإن شاء عفا عنه فضلاً منه ورحمة وقوله تعالى : إِن تَجْتَنِبُوا كَبَابِرَ مَا تُنْهَوْنَ عَنْهُ نُكَفِّرْ عَنكُمْ سَيِّئَاتِكُمْ الآية قرأ: إِن تَجْتَنِبُوا كَبَابِرَ مَا تُنْهَوْنَ عَنْهُ والمراد به الكفر وقيل المراد من قوله : كَبَابِرَ مَا تُنْهَوْنَ عَنْهُ أنواع الكفر. ويحققه: ثبوت جواز المغفرة لما دون الشرك بما قررنا من الدلائل غيلان على الله تعالى أنه إذا غفر لمرتكب ذنبًا لا يجوز تعذيب غيره على ذلك أنه تعالى أثبت لنفسه المشيئة في ذلك، وعلق ذلك بما تحكم باطل مع أن الذنب مع أن الله تعالى متفضل على من غفر له، ومن تفضل على شخص لا يجب عليه التفضل على غيره ولا يحرم عليه العدل على من سو سواه.
He has the name 'al-Ghafir', and forgiveness and pardon cannot be conceived from Him. The Mu'tazila interpreted that as forgiveness of minor sins for one who avoids major sins, but this is not sound, because forgiveness is overlooking one who it is permissible to punish for what he committed. As for leaving aside punishment of one who it is not permissible to punish, that is not pardon nor forgiveness, like leaving aside punishment for permissible things. It is established by examining these meanings that there is no reality to His being 'Ghafir', 'Ghafur', 'Rahim' according to the Khawarij and Mu'tazila, because forgiveness from one who it is permissible to punish is not conceivable according to them, nor according to the Murji'ah, because according to them it is not permissible except to punish the disbeliever, and pardon for him is not established. Then when that was established, we have no proof specifying whom He forgives and whom He does not forgive but rather punishes, because that is not in the intellect, and what the Law came with? Rather He delegated that to His will by His saying: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him, but He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills.' So we said to him: The will is in that: whoever He wills, He punishes according to his sin, whether it is small or great, as justice from Him, and then his final end is paradise; and if He wills, He pardons him as favor and mercy from Him. And His saying: 'If you avoid the great sins which you are forbidden, We will expiate from you your misdeeds' — the verse — he read: 'If you avoid the great sins which you are forbidden' and what is meant by it is unbelief, and it is said what is meant by His saying 'the great sins which you are forbidden' are the types of unbelief. And it is verified by the proofs we have established: the permissibility of forgiveness for what is less than shirk. Ghilan [said] regarding Allah that if He forgives a committer of a sin, it is not permissible to punish another for that, because He has established for Himself the will in that, and attached it to what is false, while the sin, and because Allah is bestowing favor on whom He forgives, and if He bestows favor on a person, He is not obligated to bestow favor on another, nor is justice prohibited upon others.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument defining the true nature of divine forgiveness and refuting the positions of the Mu'tazila, Khawarij, and Murji'ah. The author begins by stating that God has the name 'al-Ghafir', but forgiveness cannot be truly conceived unless it is directed at someone who deserves punishment. The Mu'tazila's claim that forgiveness is only for minor sins when one avoids major sins is unsound, because forgiveness necessarily involves overlooking a sin that could be justly punished. If a person cannot be punished at all (like for permissible actions), then there is no forgiveness. The author then shows that on the principles of the Khawarij and Mu'tazila, God cannot truly be called 'Forgiving' because they hold that a believer who commits a major sin deserves eternal punishment, so forgiveness would be impossible. Similarly, the Murji'ah deny forgiveness entirely except for disbelievers. Since the intellect cannot determine who is forgiven, God has delegated this to His will, as stated in the Quran (4:48, 4:31). The author explains that God may punish any sin according to His justice, and the final abode of a believer is paradise; or He may pardon out of favor. The verse about avoiding great sins is interpreted as referring to unbelief (the greatest sin) or its types. The argument is further supported: forgiveness for lesser sins is possible based on textual proofs. The author then rebuts the claim of Ghilan (a Qadari) that if God forgives one sinner, He must forgive all, by stating that forgiveness is a favor, not an obligation, and God may act justly with others.

Key Terms
الغافر
المغفرة
العفو
المعتزلة
الصغائر
الكبائر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Forgiveness means God overlooks sins regardless of whether the sinner could be punished.
Correction: Forgiveness only occurs when the sinner could have been justly punished; otherwise it is simply leaving aside punishment for permissible things.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is a necessary condition for forgiveness?
A) The sin must be minor.
B) The sinner must avoid major sins.
C) The sinner must deserve punishment.
D) The sin must be permissible.

Key Takeaway
Forgiveness presupposes just punishment; without it, there is no forgiveness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you assume God will forgive you just because you avoid major sins? How does this lesson challenge your assumption?
?
Have you ever considered that God's forgiveness is not automatic but is a genuine remission of punishment you deserve? How does this change your attitude towards sin?
?
If forgiveness requires that you could be justly punished, does that make you more mindful of your actions? How can you live in light of this truth?

Divine Forgiveness and God's Sovereign Will
Refutes Khawarij, Mu'tazila, Murji'ah and establishes God's will in forgiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that for the Khawarij and Murji'ah, God cannot truly be called Forgiving, and that forgiveness is ultimately dependent on God's will as revealed in the Quran.

Core Idea
God's forgiveness is an act of will, not a mechanical rule.

After defining forgiveness, Imam Nasafi shows that the Khawarij, Mu'tazila, and Murji'ah all undermine God's attribute of forgiveness. The Khawarij and Mu'tazila deny forgiveness for major sins, while the Murji'ah only allow it for disbelievers. The author then relies on the Quranic verse (4:48) to affirm that forgiveness is entirely at God's discretion, limited only by the condition of not forgiving shirk.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Khawarij and Mu'tazila position.
They hold that a major sinner cannot be forgiven; thus for them, God cannot truly be Forgiving.
Focus on why forgiveness is inconceivable on their principles.
2
Understand the Murji'ah position.
They only allow punishment for disbelievers, so forgiveness for believers is irrelevant.
Both extremes negate the attribute.
3
See the limitation of intellect.
The intellect cannot determine who is forgiven; we must rely on revelation.
Introduces the Quranic foundation.
4
Learn the Quranic basis.
Verse 4:48 shows God forgives whom He wills except shirk. This establishes His discretion.
Punishment and pardon are both just and merciful.

Source Text

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Interpreting 'Avoid Great Sins' and Rebuttal of Ghilan
Interprets verse 4:31 and rebuts Ghilan's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct interpretation of the verse on avoiding great sins and the rebuttal of the claim that God must treat all sinners equally.

Core Idea
Forgiveness is a grace from God, not a requirement of justice.

Imam Nasafi now addresses a key verse often misunderstood by the Mu'tazila. He clarifies that 'great sins' in the verse refers to shirk. He then refutes an objection from Ghilan, who argued that God cannot forgive some sinners without being unjust to others. The author shows that forgiveness is a favor, and God is not obligated to bestow favors equally.

Guided Path
1
Understand the interpretation of the verse.
The great sins (كبائر) are interpreted as unbelief (shirk) or its types, not all major sins.
This limits the promise of expiation to those who avoid shirk.
2
Learn the proof for permissibility of forgiveness.
The author restates that forgiveness for lesser sins is established by proofs.
This reinforces the earlier conclusion.
3
Understand and rebut Ghilan's argument.
Ghilan claimed that if God forgives one sinner, He must forgive all equally. The author shows this is false because forgiveness is a favor, not a duty.
God can treat individuals differently without injustice.

له اسم الغافر ولا تصورت منه المغفرة والعفو . وحمل المعتزلة ذلك على مغفرة الصغائر لمن اجتنب الكبائر غير سديد، إذ المغفرة التجاوز عمن يجوز تعذيبه على ما ارتكب فأما ترك تعذيب من لا يجوز تعذيبه فليس بعفو ولا مغفرة كترك التعذيب على المباحات. وتقرر بالوقوف على هذه المعاني أن لا تحقق لكونه تعالى غافرًا غفورا رحيمًا على أصل الخوارج والمعتزلة إذ لا يتصور عندهم مغفرة من يجوز له تعذيبه ولا على أصل المرجئة، فإن عندهم ما يجوز له إلا تعذيب الكافر والعفو عنه غير ثابت، ثم لما ثبت ذلك لم يقم لنا دليل تعيين من يغفر له ومن لا يغفر له بل يعذبه إذ ليس ذلك في العقل، وما ورد به الشرع بل فوض ذلك إلى مشيئته بقوله تعالى : إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَن يُشْرَكَ بِهِ وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَن يَشَاءُ . فقلنا له: المشيئة في ذلك فمن شاء عذبه بقدر ذنبه صغيرة كان ذلك أم كبيرة عدلاً منه ثم عاقبة أمره الجنة وإن شاء عفا عنه فضلاً منه ورحمة وقوله تعالى : إِن تَجْتَنِبُوا كَبَابِرَ مَا تُنْهَوْنَ عَنْهُ نُكَفِّرْ عَنكُمْ سَيِّئَاتِكُمْ الآية قرأ: إِن تَجْتَنِبُوا كَبَابِرَ مَا تُنْهَوْنَ عَنْهُ والمراد به الكفر وقيل المراد من قوله : كَبَابِرَ مَا تُنْهَوْنَ عَنْهُ أنواع الكفر. ويحققه: ثبوت جواز المغفرة لما دون الشرك بما قررنا من الدلائل غيلان على الله تعالى أنه إذا غفر لمرتكب ذنبًا لا يجوز تعذيب غيره على ذلك أنه تعالى أثبت لنفسه المشيئة في ذلك، وعلق ذلك بما تحكم باطل مع أن الذنب مع أن الله تعالى متفضل على من غفر له، ومن تفضل على شخص لا يجب عليه التفضل على غيره ولا يحرم عليه العدل على من سو سواه.
He has the name 'al-Ghafir', and forgiveness and pardon cannot be conceived from Him. The Mu'tazila interpreted that as forgiveness of minor sins for one who avoids major sins, but this is not sound, because forgiveness is overlooking one who it is permissible to punish for what he committed. As for leaving aside punishment of one who it is not permissible to punish, that is not pardon nor forgiveness, like leaving aside punishment for permissible things. It is established by examining these meanings that there is no reality to His being 'Ghafir', 'Ghafur', 'Rahim' according to the Khawarij and Mu'tazila, because forgiveness from one who it is permissible to punish is not conceivable according to them, nor according to the Murji'ah, because according to them it is not permissible except to punish the disbeliever, and pardon for him is not established. Then when that was established, we have no proof specifying whom He forgives and whom He does not forgive but rather punishes, because that is not in the intellect, and what the Law came with? Rather He delegated that to His will by His saying: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him, but He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills.' So we said to him: The will is in that: whoever He wills, He punishes according to his sin, whether it is small or great, as justice from Him, and then his final end is paradise; and if He wills, He pardons him as favor and mercy from Him. And His saying: 'If you avoid the great sins which you are forbidden, We will expiate from you your misdeeds' — the verse — he read: 'If you avoid the great sins which you are forbidden' and what is meant by it is unbelief, and it is said what is meant by His saying 'the great sins which you are forbidden' are the types of unbelief. And it is verified by the proofs we have established: the permissibility of forgiveness for what is less than shirk. Ghilan [said] regarding Allah that if He forgives a committer of a sin, it is not permissible to punish another for that, because He has established for Himself the will in that, and attached it to what is false, while the sin, and because Allah is bestowing favor on whom He forgives, and if He bestows favor on a person, He is not obligated to bestow favor on another, nor is justice prohibited upon others.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument defining the true nature of divine forgiveness and refuting the positions of the Mu'tazila, Khawarij, and Murji'ah. The author begins by stating that God has the name 'al-Ghafir', but forgiveness cannot be truly conceived unless it is directed at someone who deserves punishment. The Mu'tazila's claim that forgiveness is only for minor sins when one avoids major sins is unsound, because forgiveness necessarily involves overlooking a sin that could be justly punished. If a person cannot be punished at all (like for permissible actions), then there is no forgiveness. The author then shows that on the principles of the Khawarij and Mu'tazila, God cannot truly be called 'Forgiving' because they hold that a believer who commits a major sin deserves eternal punishment, so forgiveness would be impossible. Similarly, the Murji'ah deny forgiveness entirely except for disbelievers. Since the intellect cannot determine who is forgiven, God has delegated this to His will, as stated in the Quran (4:48, 4:31). The author explains that God may punish any sin according to His justice, and the final abode of a believer is paradise; or He may pardon out of favor. The verse about avoiding great sins is interpreted as referring to unbelief (the greatest sin) or its types. The argument is further supported: forgiveness for lesser sins is possible based on textual proofs. The author then rebuts the claim of Ghilan (a Qadari) that if God forgives one sinner, He must forgive all, by stating that forgiveness is a favor, not an obligation, and God may act justly with others.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God must forgive all sinners equally or be unjust.
Correction: God's forgiveness is a favor; He may forgive some and not others without injustice, as He is not obliged to treat all equally.

Checkpoint
What is the author's interpretation of 'great sins' (كبائر) in the verse?
A) All major sins.
B) Only shirk (unbelief).
C) Only murder.
D) Sins that are not minor.

Key Takeaway
Forgiveness is a grace, not an obligation; God may show favor to whom He wills.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek Allah's forgiveness for your sins, do you rely on His justice (assuming He must forgive if you repent) or on His grace (knowing He is not obligated)? How does this lesson challenge your assumption?
?
Ghilan's objection sounds fair: if God forgives one person, why not another who did the same sin? Do you ever feel resentment when you see others receive blessings you think they don't deserve? How can you surrender your sense of 'fairness' to Allah's perfect wisdom?
?
The author interprets 'avoid great sins' as avoiding shirk. Is your primary concern avoiding major outward sins, or have you truly examined whether your heart is free from hidden shirk (e.g., relying on created things)? What concrete step can you take today to purify your worship?
